Intracranial Herniation Patterns
Critical threshold: Midline shift >5 mm warrants urgent neurosurgical notification. Pupillary dilation = uncal herniation until proven otherwise.
PatternStructures InvolvedKey Imaging FindingsClinical Significance
Subfalcine (cingulate)Cingulate gyrus herniates under falx cerebriMidline shift, ipsilateral lateral ventricle compressed, contralateral enlarged; ACA displacementMost common; may compress ACA causing contralateral leg weakness; may progress to transtentorial
Transtentorial uncal (descending)Medial temporal lobe (uncus, parahippocampal gyrus) through tentorial notchEffacement of suprasellar cistern, PCA compression (occipital infarct), midbrain distortion, CN III compression (ipsilateral pupil dilation)Most clinically critical; CN III palsy (down-and-out pupil), Kernohan's notch (contralateral hemiparesis — false localizing)
Central transtentorialBilateral hemispheres/diencephalon descend through tentoriumDownward displacement of diencephalon, midbrain, bilateral uncal herniation; diffuse sulcal effacementBilateral signs; Cheyne-Stokes respiration → central neurogenic hyperventilation; rostrocaudal deterioration
Upward cerebellar (ascending)Cerebellum herniates upward through tentorial notchEffacement of quadrigeminal cistern, superior vermian displacement, obstructive hydrocephalusOccurs with posterior fossa mass/bleed; can cause obstructive hydrocephalus and midbrain compression
TonsillarCerebellar tonsils herniate through foramen magnumTonsils >5 mm below McRae's line, effacement of cisterna magna, obex displacementCompresses medulla (respiratory/cardiac arrest); can be precipitated by lumbar puncture in presence of raised ICP
📄Wijdicks EF et al. Herniation patterns in massive hemispheric infarction. Cerebrovasc Dis. 2001;12(2):75-9.
📄Radiopaedia — Brain herniation (overview)

Stroke
Stroke Quick Reference
Early CT findings of ischemia: Loss of gray-white differentiation · Insular ribbon sign · Dense MCA sign · Sulcal effacement · Hypodense parenchyma
WindowInterventionKey Criteria
0–4.5hIV tPANIHSS ≥4, no hemorrhage, no large established infarct (>1/3 MCA territory)
0–24hThrombectomyLarge vessel occlusion; DAWN/DEFUSE-3 criteria if 6–24h
Thrombectomy coreEligibilityCore infarct <70 mL; tissue at risk >15 mL (CT perfusion)
📋 Reporting Checklist — Acute Ischemic Stroke
  • ASPECTS score (if MCA territory; score /10 — specify affected regions)
  • Vessel occlusion site: ICA / M1 / M2 / M3 / ACA / PCA / basilar (hyperdense vessel sign)
  • Affected vascular territory
  • Collateral grade: good / poor / absent (on CTA source images)
  • Early ischemic changes: grey-white differentiation loss / sulcal effacement / dense MCA sign
  • CT perfusion (if performed): core volume (mL), penumbra volume (mL), mismatch ratio
  • Hemorrhagic transformation: absent / HI1 / HI2 / PH1 / PH2
  • Treatment eligibility comment: tPA window / thrombectomy window (per last known well)
  • Prior infarcts: location, chronicity
ASPECTS (MCA Stroke)
10-point score; subtract 1 point for each involved region. Score ≤6 associated with poor outcome. Regions: Caudate (C), Lentiform (L), Insula (I), Internal capsule (IC), M1–M6 (cortical MCA territory).
CT Perfusion — Thrombectomy Thresholds
ParameterThresholdRepresents
CBF <30%Core infarctIrreversibly infarcted tissue
Tmax >6sPenumbraHypoperfused but salvageable tissue
MismatchPenumbra − Core>15 mL AND >1.8 ratio = favorable
DAWN (2018) & DEFUSE-3 (2018): Extended window thrombectomy to 24h. Mismatch volume >10 mL (DAWN) or >15 mL with ratio >1.8 (DEFUSE-3). Core <70 mL required.
Trauma
Cervical Spine — Craniocervical Junction
MeasurementNormal
Basion-dens interval (BDI)≤12 mm
Basion-axial interval (BAI)≤12 mm
Atlantodental interval (ADI)≤3 mm adult; ≤5 mm child
Powers ratio<1.0 (≥1.0 = occipital-C1 dissociation)
Prevertebral soft tissue C2<7 mm
Prevertebral soft tissue C6<22 mm
Cervical Spine Fractures
Cervical Ligamentous Injury
MRI indications: CT-negative with persistent pain/neurological symptoms, or high clinical suspicion. STIR/fat-sat T2 most sensitive for ligamentous injury. Obtain within 48–72 h if cord injury suspected.
LigamentLocationMRI FindingsSignificance
Transverse atlantal (TAL)Anchors dens to C1 lateral massesT2/STIR hyperintensity or discontinuity at attachment; ADI >3 mm adult (>5 mm child)Critical stability structure; rupture → atlantoaxial instability; associated with Jefferson burst (type III)
Alar ligamentsBilateral: dens to occipital condylesSTIR: asymmetric signal/disruption; loss of taut low-signal bandLimit rotation; bilateral injury → craniocervical instability
Posterior ligamentous complex (PLC)Supraspinous + interspinous + ligamentum flavum + facet capsulesT2/STIR hyperintensity in interspinous space; facet capsule disruption; CT: widened interspinous distance, perched/jumped facetsPrimary restraint to flexion; disruption = instability; AO Spine morphology modifier
Posterior longitudinal ligament (PLL)Posterior vertebral bodies C2–sacrumT2 hyperintensity/discontinuity posterior to vertebral bodies; associated anterior cord edemaDisruption in flexion injuries; increases risk of posterior disc herniation and cord compression
Anterior longitudinal ligament (ALL)Anterior vertebral bodiesT2 hyperintensity anterior to vertebral body; prevertebral hematomaDisrupted in hyperextension injuries; less critical than PLC for stability
Instability criteria: >3.5 mm horizontal displacement OR >11° angulation between adjacent vertebrae. Flexion-extension radiographs contraindicated acutely. STIR MRI most sensitive for acute ligamentous disruption.
RadioGraphics 2015: Cervical Spine Ligamentous Injury AJR: Cervical Ligament Assessment
Spine Trauma
Cervical spine search pattern: Alignment → Bones → Cartilage → Disc spaces → Soft tissues (ABCDS). Check prevertebral soft tissue: <7mm at C2, <22mm at C6.
MeasurementNormal Value
Basion-dens interval (BDI)≤12 mm
Basion-axial interval (BAI)≤12 mm
Powers ratio<1 (occipital-C1 dissociation if >1)
Atlantodental interval (ADI)≤3 mm adult, ≤5 mm child
Prevertebral soft tissue C2<7 mm
Prevertebral soft tissue C6<22 mm

AO Spine Classification Systems
AO Foundation
Thoracolumbar Spine — AO Spine Classification
Denis three-column concept: Anterior column (ALL + anterior ½ vertebral body/disc) · Middle column (posterior ½ body/disc + PLL) · Posterior column (posterior elements). Disruption of ≥2 columns = unstable.

Blunt Cerebrovascular Injury (BCVI) Denver Criteria
Screen when: Cervical spine fracture (especially C1–C3, involving foramen transversarium) · Skull base fracture · Severe facial fractures · Seatbelt sign to neck · GCS <6 with facial fractures
I
Luminal irregularity or dissection <25% narrowing
II
Dissection/intramural hematoma ≥25% narrowing, intraluminal thrombus, or raised intimal flap
III
Pseudoaneurysm
IV
Occlusion
V
Transection with free extravasation — surgical emergency
📄Source: Neuroimaging Clinics — Denver Criteria for BCVI
📋 Reporting Checklist — Blunt Cerebrovascular Injury (BCVI)
  • Denver grade: I (irregularity/dissection) / II (intraluminal thrombus) / III (pseudoaneurysm) / IV (occlusion) / V (transection)
  • Vessel(s) involved: ICA (specify segment: petrous / cavernous / supraclinoid) / VA (V1–V4)
  • Bilateral involvement: yes / no
  • Pseudoaneurysm: size (mm), location
  • Dissection extent: proximal and distal limits
  • Luminal reduction: estimate % stenosis
  • Intraluminal thrombus: present / absent
  • Associated injury: skull base fracture (ICA canal) / cervical spine fracture (transverse foramen)
  • Ischemic changes: present / absent on CT or CTP (territory at risk)
Visceral Neck Trauma
Zone I: Below cricoid to thoracic inlet · Zone II: Cricoid to angle of mandible (most common injury zone) · Zone III: Above angle of mandible to skull base
Infection
Complications of Sinusitis
ComplicationImaging FindingsUrgency
Preseptal (periorbital) cellulitisCT: soft tissue swelling anterior to orbital septum; eyelid thickening; no orbital fat involvement; no proptosisUrgent — IV antibiotics; no surgical drainage typically required; differentiate from postseptal by location relative to orbital septum
Subperiosteal abscessCT: lenticular fluid collection between periorbita and orbital wall (medial wall most common from ethmoid sinusitis); rim enhancement; medial rectus displacement; mild proptosisUrgent — surgical drainage usually required (especially if >1 cm, visual compromise); pediatric patients may respond to IV antibiotics alone if small
Orbital abscess (postseptal cellulitis)CT: intraorbital hypodense collection with rim enhancement; proptosis; fat stranding; may have gas; optic nerve involvement possibleEmergent surgical drainage — visual loss risk; optic nerve compression causes afferent pupillary defect; extraocular muscle entrapment
Cavernous sinus thrombosisCT/MRV: filling defect in cavernous sinus; convex lateral wall; bilateral involvement; thrombosed superior ophthalmic vein; sphenoid/ethmoid sinusitis; venous infarcts. Pathognomonic: bilateral cavernous sinus involvementEmergent — high mortality; bilateral CN III/IV/VI palsies; septic emboli; urgent IV antibiotics ± anticoagulation
Epidural abscess (intracranial)CT/MRI: biconvex extra-axial collection with rim enhancement adjacent to infected sinus; does not cross sutures; adjacent bone erosion; dural enhancementEmergent neurosurgical drainage; associated with frontal sinusitis or temporal bone osteomyelitis
Subdural empyemaCT/MRI: crescentic collection with restricted diffusion (DWI); crosses sutures; dural/leptomeningeal enhancement; midline shift; can be bilateral interhemisphericEmergent surgical drainage; high morbidity/mortality; frequently from frontal or ethmoid sinusitis
Pott's puffy tumorCT: frontal bone erosion/osteomyelitis; subperiosteal abscess of forehead; epidural abscess; frontal sinusitis; sequestrum formationUrgent — combined neurosurgical and ENT management; IV antibiotics; frontal sinus drainage and craniotomy for intracranial extension
📄Chandler JR et al. The pathogenesis of orbital complications in acute sinusitis. Laryngoscope. 1970;80(9):1414-28.
Odontogenic Infection
🔗
Odontogenic infection spaces
LearningHeadandNeck.com
Peritonsillar & Retropharyngeal Abscess
Danger: Retropharyngeal abscess can extend inferiorly via the danger space (space 4) to the posterior mediastinum causing descending necrotizing mediastinitis — a surgical emergency with high mortality. Any retropharyngeal collection extending below C6 / thoracic inlet requires urgent surgical evaluation.
SpaceLocationImaging FindingsDanger / Key Points
Peritonsillar abscessBetween tonsillar capsule and superior pharyngeal constrictor muscleCT: hypodense fluid collection with peripheral enhancement lateral to tonsil; displacement of uvula away from affected side; mass effect on oropharynxMost common deep neck infection; typically unilateral; needle aspiration or I&D; can extend to parapharyngeal space
Retropharyngeal abscessPosterior to pharynx, anterior to prevertebral fascia (between visceral and alar fascia)CT: low-density fluid collection with rim enhancement posterior to pharynx; prevertebral soft tissue thickening >7 mm at C2 or >22 mm at C6; scalloped or lobulated margin; gas = pathognomonic abscessCan extend to mediastinum via danger space; airway compression; most common in children under 6; group A Strep, Staph aureus
Danger space (space 4)Between alar fascia (anterior) and prevertebral fascia (posterior); extends skull base to diaphragmCT: fluid tracking along fascial planes from skull base to posterior mediastinum; often contiguous with retropharyngeal collectionDirect conduit for descending necrotizing mediastinitis; no natural barriers to inferior spread; critical to identify extent on CT chest
Ludwig angina (submandibular)Bilateral submandibular, sublingual, and submental spacesCT: bilateral soft tissue swelling/cellulitis of floor of mouth; gas and/or abscess in submandibular space; displacement and elevation of tongue; airway compromiseRapidly progressive; dental origin (2nd/3rd molar); airway emergency — early intubation or surgical airway; gas-forming organisms; high mortality without aggressive management
📄Vieira F et al. Deep neck infection. Otolaryngol Clin North Am. 2008;41(3):459-83.
Epiglottitis / Supraglottitis
CT findings: Thickened epiglottis >8 mm (AP diameter); aryepiglottic fold thickening >3 mm; prevertebral soft tissue swelling; supraglottic airway narrowing; "thumb-shaped" epiglottis replaces normal thin curved epiglottis; periglottic fat stranding; enhancement with IV contrast. Vallecula effacement.
Airway warning: Do NOT attempt to visualize the epiglottis with tongue blade or laryngoscopy without immediate airway control available — can precipitate complete obstruction. Imaging only if diagnosis uncertain AND patient is stable with monitored airway. Any signs of drooling, stridor, or "tripod" positioning = urgent airway management first.
Imaging pearl: Thumbprint sign on lateral neck radiograph (thickened epiglottis resembling a thumb). Vallecula sign: loss of air-filled vallecula. Adults: polymicrobial or H. influenzae; also consider epiglottic abscess (requires CT). Children: H. influenzae type b (Hib) historically, now rare with vaccination. Adults more common presentation today. Supraglottitis = involvement of aryepiglottic folds, arytenoids, and supraglottic structures beyond epiglottis alone.
📄Shah RK et al. Epiglottitis in adults. Laryngoscope. 2004;114(9):1542-5.
📄Radiopaedia — Epiglottitis
Intracranial Infections
DiagnosisCT FindingsMRI FindingsKey Features
Bacterial meningitisOften normal early; diffuse sulcal effacement; hydrocephalus; may see leptomeningeal enhancement on CECTFLAIR: sulcal/cisternal hyperintensity (pus in CSF); post-gadolinium: leptomeningeal enhancement; DWI: sulcal restriction (purulent exudate); complications: ventriculitis, empyema, infarctsCT/LP sequence: CT first only if papilledema, focal neuro deficit, immunocompromised, or GCS <10. Do NOT delay LP/antibiotics for imaging if no contraindication. Pyogenic organisms: S. pneumoniae, N. meningitidis, Listeria (elderly)
Viral encephalitis (HSV)May be normal early; subtle hypodensity in temporal lobes / insular cortex (unilateral or bilateral)FLAIR/T2: hyperintensity in medial temporal lobes, insula, cingulate, and inferior frontal lobes (limbic predilection); DWI: restricted diffusion (cytotoxic edema); hemorrhage (T2* blooming) in severe cases; spares basal ganglia (unlike arboviruses)HSV-1 most common cause of sporadic encephalitis in adults. Bilateral but asymmetric temporal involvement. EEG: periodic lateralizing discharges. Treat empirically with acyclovir. CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis, elevated protein, PCR positive.
Brain abscessRing-enhancing hypodense lesion with mass effect; central low density; surrounding edema; may have air if gas-forming organismsT1: hypointense center, ring of T1 hyperintensity (capsule); T2: hyperintense center (pus), low signal capsule (T2 hypointense rim), surrounding edema; DWI: RESTRICTED diffusion of center (pus — hallmark); post-contrast: smooth ring enhancementDWI restriction distinguishes abscess from necrotic tumor (cystic/necrotic tumor: no restriction or peripheral restriction only). Capsule thinner on ventricular side (higher rupture risk into ventricles). Sources: dental, sinus, cardiac shunt (paradoxical), post-traumatic, cryptogenic.
Subdural empyemaHyperdense or isodense crescentic extra-axial collection; effacement of sulci; midline shift; may cross falx (unlike epidural)T1: iso-hypointense; T2/FLAIR: hyperintense; DWI: RESTRICTED (pus); post-contrast: thick peripheral enhancement and dural enhancement; crosses sutures (unlike epidural empyema)Surgical emergency — mortality high without immediate drainage. Sources: sinusitis (frontal most common), otitis media, meningitis. Bilateral in 10-20%. Can extend to interhemispheric fissure.
Epidural abscessBiconvex (lenticular) extra-axial hyperdense collection; does NOT cross sutures; does NOT cross midlineT1: iso-hypointense; T2: hyperintense; DWI: restricted; post-contrast: peripheral ring enhancement; biconvex shape; limited by suturesLess common than subdural empyema; does not cross sutures (unlike subdural). Often associated with adjacent calvarial osteomyelitis. Cranial epidural different from spinal epidural (latter more common). Surgical drainage usually required.
📄Britt RH et al. Brain abscess. Neurosurg Clin N Am. 1992;3(2):403-20. / Kastrup O et al. Neuroradiology in CNS infection. Ther Adv Neurol Disord. 2008;1(1):47-62.
Other
Dural Sinus Thrombosis (CVST)
Key imaging signs: CT: hyperdense sinus (acute) — "dense sinus sign"; post-contrast: "empty delta sign" (filling defect in posterior superior sagittal sinus). MRI: T1 loss of flow void → T1 bright (subacute); T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in sinus; blooming artifact on SWI/GRE (deoxyhemoglobin/hemosiderin); restricted diffusion in venous infarct (often not typical wedge pattern). MRV: filling defect or absent sinus.
Treatment pearl: Anticoagulate even in the presence of intracranial hemorrhage (venous hemorrhage — anticoagulation reduces propagation and mortality). Do not withhold heparin based on hemorrhage alone. Consult neurology/neurosurgery urgently.
SinusLocationVenous Territory Drained
Superior sagittal sinus (SSS)Along superior falx, vertex to torcularBilateral parasagittal cortex, frontal and parietal lobes; CSF resorption (arachnoid granulations)
Transverse sinusAlong posterior tentorium, torcular → sigmoidPosterior temporal, occipital, and inferior parietal lobes; cerebellum via superior petrosal
Sigmoid sinusS-shaped, posterior temporal bone → jugular foramenContinuation of transverse; drains to internal jugular vein; mastoid air cells (otogenic thrombosis)
Cavernous sinusBilateral parasellar, sphenoid boneOrbit (ophthalmic vein), superficial middle cerebral vein, sphenoparietal sinus; CN III, IV, V1/V2, VI traverse
Straight sinusJunction of falx and tentorium → torcularDeep structures: deep cerebral veins (internal cerebral veins, basal vein of Rosenthal), cerebellum via inferior cerebellar veins
Inferior sagittal sinusFree edge of falx inferior marginMedial hemispheres (cingulate gyrus), falx
📄Ferro JM et al. Cerebral venous thrombosis. Nat Rev Dis Primers. 2017;3:17023.
📄Radiopaedia — Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis
Intracranial Hemorrhage
Blood product evolution on MRI — Hyperacute: T1 iso/↓, T2 ↑ (oxyhemoglobin) → Acute: T1 iso, T2 ↓ (deoxyhemoglobin) → Early subacute: T1 ↑, T2 ↓ (intracellular methemoglobin) → Late subacute: T1 ↑, T2 ↑ (extracellular methemoglobin) → Chronic: T1 ↓, T2 ↓ (hemosiderin)
TypeLocationKey Features
EpiduralEpidural spaceBiconvex (lenticular), crosses falx/tentorium, does NOT cross sutures; assoc. with temporal bone/MMA injury
SubduralBetween dura and arachnoidCrescent-shaped, crosses sutures, does NOT cross falx; bridging vein injury
SubarachnoidSubarachnoid spaceFollows cisterns and sulci; aneurysm vs trauma vs AVM
IntraparenchymalBrain parenchymaHypertensive (basal ganglia, thalamus, pons, cerebellum); amyloid (cortical/subcortical)
IntraventricularVentriclesExtension from parenchymal bleed; hydrocephalus risk
📋 Reporting Checklist — Intracranial Hemorrhage
  • Hemorrhage type: EDH / SDH / SAH / IPH / IVH (specify all present)
  • Location: lobe / fossa / cistern / ventricle (be specific)
  • Volume: ABC/2 method for IPH (report in mL)
  • Midline shift: measure in mm, specify direction
  • Herniation pattern: subfalcine / uncal / transtentorial / tonsillar / none
  • Hydrocephalus: present / absent; obstructive vs communicating
  • Effacement of basal cisterns: patent / partially effaced / obliterated
  • Underlying lesion: AVM / tumor / cavernoma / aneurysm / none identified
  • Prior hemorrhage or evolving change (compare to prior exam if available)
Pituitary Apoplexy
Pathophysiology & imaging: Hemorrhage and/or infarction into a pituitary adenoma (rarely normal gland). CT: hyperdense sellar/suprasellar mass (acute hemorrhage); may show expansion of sella. MRI: T1 bright signal (methemoglobin, subacute hemorrhage) within adenoma; T2 variable; surrounding edema. DWI: may show restricted diffusion (ischemic component). Optic chiasm compression → bitemporal hemianopia. Cavernous sinus invasion possible. Macroadenoma most commonly affected.
Endocrine emergency: Acute cortisol deficiency (secondary adrenal insufficiency) is life-threatening — hypotension, hypoglycemia, hyponatremia. Notify endocrinology immediately. Empirical hydrocortisone may be required before lab results. Ophthalmology if visual loss. Neurosurgery if chiasmal compression or deteriorating vision. MRI preferred over CT for full characterization.
📄Rajasekaran S et al. UK guidelines for the management of pituitary apoplexy. Clin Endocrinol (Oxf). 2011;74(1):9-20.
📄Radiopaedia — Pituitary apoplexy
PRES (Posterior Reversible Encephalopathy Syndrome)
MRI findings: FLAIR/T2 hyperintensity predominantly in posterior watershed zones (parieto-occipital most common) reflecting vasogenic edema. DWI: usually NOT restricted (distinguishes from infarct) — ADC elevated (T2 shine-through). Restricted diffusion, if present, indicates irreversible injury. Enhancement variable. Resolves with treatment of underlying cause.
Common etiologies: Hypertensive emergency, eclampsia/preeclampsia (most common), immunosuppressants (cyclosporine, tacrolimus), chemotherapy (cisplatin), sepsis/TTP, renal failure.
DistributionRegions Involved
Typical (posterior watershed)Bilateral parieto-occipital lobes (most common); posterior frontal; posterior temporal; superior cerebellum
Atypical (anterior)Frontal lobes, basal ganglia, brainstem, cerebellum; unilateral; may mimic other pathology
Complicated PRESHemorrhage (petechial to lobar), restricted diffusion (ischemia), sulcal FLAIR (SAH variant)
PRES vs posterior circulation infarct: DWI is the key differentiator. PRES = ADC elevated (vasogenic edema, no restriction). Posterior circulation infarct = DWI bright + ADC dark (cytotoxic edema, restricted). Both show FLAIR/T2 hyperintensity in posterior fossa — always check DWI + ADC map. Vascular territory distribution favors infarct; non-vascular confluent pattern favors PRES.
📄Liman TG et al. Posterior Reversible Encephalopathy Syndrome. Curr Treat Options Neurol. 2019;21(11):55.
📄Radiopaedia — PRES
Programmable Shunt Settings
AJNR: Programmable Shunt Programming After MRI
AJNR: Programmable Shunts & MRI
MRI concern: Some programmable shunts reset after MRI. Always check the shunt brand and MRI safety — and follow up post-scan to confirm setting. Use mrisafety.com for device-specific data.

Trauma
Traumatic Aortic Injury
I
Intimal tear — medical management
II
Intramural hematoma — medical vs. TEVAR
III
Pseudoaneurysm — TEVAR indicated
IV
Rupture — emergent TEVAR/surgery
📋 Reporting Checklist — Traumatic Aortic Injury
  • AAST grade: I (minimal intimal tear) / II (intramural hematoma) / III (pseudoaneurysm) / IV (rupture)
  • Location: sinotubular junction / isthmus / descending (cm from left subclavian origin)
  • Periaortic / mediastinal hematoma extent; left hemothorax volume
  • Pseudoaneurysm size: max diameter (mm) vs adjacent normal aortic diameter
  • Active extravasation: present / absent
  • Branch vessel involvement: left subclavian / celiac / SMA / renals / iliacs
  • Aortic diameter proximal and distal to injury (for TEVAR sizing)
  • Hemothorax: bilateral / unilateral; estimated volume
  • Comparison to prior imaging if available
📄Radiopaedia — Thoracic aortic injury
Traumatic Cardiac & Pericardial Injury
Hemopericardium: Pericardial fluid HU 40–70 = blood = hemopericardium until proven otherwise. Tamponade = clinical + echocardiographic diagnosis (right heart collapse, IVC plethora) — CT alone is insufficient. Any hemopericardium in trauma warrants urgent cardiothoracic/trauma surgery notification. Pneumopericardium (air in pericardial sac) = laceration until proven otherwise.
Functional assessment: Valvular injury and wall motion abnormality require echocardiography (TTE/TEE). Troponin + ECG complement imaging for myocardial contusion. MRI gold standard for myocardial injury characterization when stable.
InjuryCT FindingsKey Points
Hemopericardium / tamponadeHigh-density pericardial fluid (40–70 HU); pericardial thickening; pneumopericardium; associated sternal/rib fracturesClinical tamponade: hypotension, JVD, muffled heart sounds (Beck's triad); RV collapse on echo; CT alone does not diagnose tamponade physiology
Myocardial contusionCT often normal; RV most commonly affected (anterior position); focal wall motion abnormality; intramural hematoma (rare hyperdense focus in myocardium); pericardial effusionMost common blunt cardiac injury; RV most vulnerable due to anterior location; troponin elevation + ECG changes (arrhythmia, RBBB) confirm; cardiac monitoring mandatory ≥24h
Myocardial laceration / ruptureDiscontinuity of myocardial wall; massive hemopericardium; exsanguination into pericardium; usually fatal at sceneRA and RV most commonly lacerated; survivors present with rapidly deteriorating hemodynamics; surgical emergency; most do not survive to imaging
Traumatic valvular injuryCT insensitive; TEE/TTE required; aortic valve most common in blunt trauma (sudden compression during diastole)Aortic > mitral > tricuspid; acute aortic regurgitation may require emergency valve replacement; acute MR may cause flash pulmonary edema
Coronary artery injuryRare; CT may show intramural hematoma, dissection flap, or occlusion of coronary artery; LAD most commonPresents as acute MI in setting of trauma; cardiac cath / CTA for diagnosis; PCI or CABG depending on anatomy
Traumatic VSD / ASDCT may miss; echo with Doppler required; interventricular or interatrial septal defectPresents as acute shunt; severity depends on size; may require catheter-based or surgical repair
Blunt aortic injurySee TAI section; isthmus most common; periaortic hematoma, intimal flap, pseudoaneurysmMost common lethal cardiovascular injury in blunt trauma; rapid deceleration mechanism; left hemothorax + mediastinal widening
Cardiac herniation (rare)Heart displaced outside pericardial sac; abnormal cardiac position; contralateral mediastinal shift; strangulation of great vesselsRight-sided herniation more common (thinner pericardium); "snowman" or "snow globe" cardiac appearance on CT; surgical emergency
📄Restrepo CS et al. Imaging findings of cardiac complications of trauma. RadioGraphics. 2012;32(3):637–55.
📄Radiopaedia — Blunt cardiac injury
Diaphragmatic Injury
Key concepts: Left-sided injuries more common than right (liver protective effect on right). Mechanism: blunt (MVA, crush) or penetrating. CT signs: organ herniation through defect (stomach, bowel, omentum), collar sign, dependent viscera sign, diaphragmatic discontinuity. Delayed diagnosis common — up to 30% missed on initial imaging. Reformats (coronal/sagittal) essential. Penetrating: any trajectory crossing diaphragm = suspect injury.
SignDescriptionSensitivity
Collar signWaist-like constriction of herniated organ (stomach, bowel, omentum) at the level of the diaphragmatic defect; best seen on coronal/sagittal reformats~54–85% (most specific sign for diaphragmatic laceration)
Dependent viscera signHerniated bowel or stomach resting directly on posterior ribs (right: liver; left: stomach/bowel) without intervening diaphragm; seen on axial images at lung base~55–90% for left-sided injury
Diaphragmatic discontinuityDirect visualization of gap or defect in diaphragmatic contour on CT; best on thin-slice coronal/sagittal reformats; obscured by hemorrhage or elevated hemidiaphragm~71% sensitivity; increased with thin-slice MDCT
Intrathoracic visceraStomach, bowel loops, omentum, or solid organ above diaphragm level in thorax; associated with shift of mediastinum away from herniated side; NGT coiling in thorax (stomach herniation)Highly specific when present; NGT in thorax = pathognomonic for gastric herniation
📄Sliker CW. Imaging of diaphragm injuries. Radiol Clin North Am. 2006;44(2):199-211.
📄Radiopaedia — Diaphragmatic rupture
Flail Chest
Definition: ≥3 consecutive ribs fractured in ≥2 places creating a free-floating chest wall segment that moves paradoxically (inward on inspiration, outward on expiration). Clinical diagnosis; CT defines extent of rib fractures and associated injuries.
FeatureDetails
MechanismHigh-energy blunt trauma (MVA, crush, fall from height); anterior flail chest from sternal fracture + bilateral rib fractures
CT findings≥3 consecutive rib fractures in ≥2 sites per rib; displaced or comminuted fractures; identify exact extent for surgical planning; 3D reconstruction helpful
Pulmonary contusionMost critical associated injury; peripheral GGO/consolidation underlying fractured ribs; CT extent predicts ventilator requirement; may lag on CXR by 6h
Associated injuriesPneumothorax / hemothorax; splenic/hepatic lacerations (lower rib fractures); TAI (upper rib fractures); brachial plexus injury (1st/2nd rib)
Management implicationsSignificant pulmonary contusion → positive pressure ventilation (PPV); surgical rib fixation (ORIF) considered for severe cases, ventilator-dependent patients; multi-disciplinary trauma surgery
Pearls: First rib fracture = high-energy marker → screen for TAI, vascular injury, brachial plexus. Bilateral anterior flail ("stove-in chest") from sternal + bilateral rib fractures = severe mechanism. Rib series CXR is inferior to CT for fracture counting — use CT.
📄Radiopaedia — Flail chest
Infection
Pneumonia Patterns
PatternTypical OrganismsKey CT/CXR FeaturesNotes
Lobar/segmental consolidationStreptococcus pneumoniae, KlebsiellaDense homogeneous consolidation within one lobe or segment; air bronchograms; Klebsiella: "bulging fissure" signMost common bacterial pattern; Klebsiella classically upper lobe (alcoholics, diabetics)
Bronchopneumonia/lobularStaph aureus, Haemophilus, aspirationPatchy bilateral airspace opacities; peribronchial distribution; centrilobular nodules; tree-in-bud; lower lobe predominanceAspiration: posterior upper lobe segments and superior lower lobe segments (supine position)
Interstitial/atypicalMycoplasma, viral (influenza, RSV, COVID-19), PCPReticular opacities; GGO; bilateral symmetric; peribronchial thickening; mosaic attenuationPCP: perihilar bilateral GGO in HIV; pneumatoceles → spontaneous PTX risk; LDH elevated
CavitaryStaph aureus, Klebsiella, TB, fungal (Aspergillus, Mucor), anaerobesAir-filled cavity; wall >4mm; air-fluid level = abscess; satellite nodules (TB)TB: upper lobe + apical lower lobe; cavitation + tree-in-bud = active TB until proven otherwise
Round pneumoniaS. pneumoniae (children >> adults)Spherical/ovoid consolidation; posterior lower lobes; well-defined margins; can mimic massMore common <8 yo; incomplete collateral pathways; follow to resolution to exclude malignancy
Complications to report: Lung abscess (thick-walled cavity with air-fluid level) · Empyema (split pleura sign, lenticular collection, pleural enhancement) · Bronchopleural fistula (air-fluid level at pleural margin + pneumothorax component) · Necrotizing pneumonia (GGO → consolidation → cavitation; absent enhancement within necrotic zones)
PCP pearl: Bilateral perihilar GGO in HIV-positive patient with elevated LDH = PCP until proven otherwise. Pneumatoceles develop in ~30% → spontaneous pneumothorax risk. May have near-normal CXR early — HRCT far more sensitive.
HIV / Immunocompromised Pulmonary Infections
CD4 count guides differential: >500: bacterial pneumonia, TB · 200–500: bacterial, TB, early PCP · 100–200: PCP, MAC, CMV · <100: PCP, MAC, CMV, disseminated fungal (Cryptococcus, Aspergillus, Histoplasma), Kaposi sarcoma
OrganismCT PatternKey Features
P. jiroveci (PCP)Bilateral perihilar / upper lobe GGO; spares costophrenic angles; "crazy paving" on HRCT; no pleural effusionCD4 <200; elevated LDH; pneumatoceles (30%) → PTX; CXR near-normal early; DX: BAL
Bacterial (S. pneumoniae, Pseudomonas)Lobar or multifocal consolidation; same pattern as immunocompetent but often more severe, multifocal, or with bacteremiaAny CD4 count; Pseudomonas at low CD4; recurrent bacterial pneumonia (≥2/yr) = AIDS-defining
TuberculosisCD4 >200: typical (upper lobe, cavitation, tree-in-bud) · CD4 <200: atypical — lower lobe, miliary, mediastinal adenopathy, no cavitationConsider TB at any CD4; miliary = hematogenous dissemination; adenopathy + low-density necrotic nodes = TB
MAC (M. avium complex)Tree-in-bud pattern; centrilobular nodules; consolidation; bilateral symmetricCD4 <50; often disseminated; distinguishing from TB requires culture; responds to macrolide + ethambutol
CMV pneumonitisBilateral GGO + consolidation; peribronchovascular distribution; small nodules; similar to PCP patternCD4 <50; coexists with other opportunistic infections; DX by BAL or biopsy; treat with ganciclovir
CryptococcusSingle or multiple pulmonary nodules/masses; consolidation; cavitation; miliary patternAny CD4 low; often disseminated with meningitis; India ink positive on CSF; serum/CSF cryptococcal antigen
Kaposi sarcomaFlame-shaped perihilar consolidation; nodules along bronchovascular bundles; Kerley B lines; pleural effusion; mediastinal adenopathyHHV-8 related; skin lesions usually present; classic "flame-shaped" peribronchial thickening; diagnosis by bronchoscopy
📄Kanne JP et al. Pulmonary Manifestations of HIV Infection. AJR Am J Roentgenol. 2012;198(6).
Pulmonary Infection — CT Pattern Approach
CT pattern approach: Identify dominant pattern (consolidation, GGO, nodular, cavitary, interstitial) → correlate with clinical context (immune status, geography, acuity) → generate targeted differential. Patterns often overlap; clinical integration is essential.
CT PatternCommon Infectious CausesKey Differentiating Features
Lobar/segmental consolidationS. pneumoniae, Klebsiella, Legionella, HaemophilusAir bronchograms; bulging fissure sign (Klebsiella); Legionella: lower lobe, rapid progression, may cavitate
Bronchopneumonia (patchy, bilateral)Staph aureus, aspiration organisms, Haemophilus, gram-negativesPeribronchial distribution; centrilobular nodules; lower lobe predominance; multiple foci; tree-in-bud
Ground-glass opacity (GGO)Viral (influenza, RSV, COVID-19, CMV), PCP, early bacterialBilateral symmetric; COVID-19: peripheral + lower lobe + crazy paving; PCP: perihilar; influenza: diffuse; consider DAD if severe
Nodular patternTB (miliary), fungal (Histoplasma, Coccidioides), septic emboli (Staph), CryptococcusMiliary (<2mm, random): TB, fungal, hematogenous · Centrilobular: endobronchial spread · Subpleural: aspergillosis, septic emboli
Halo signAngioinvasive Aspergillus (neutropenic), Mucor, Candida, viral hemorrhagicGGO halo around nodule = hemorrhage from angioinvasion; Aspergillus in neutropenic fever = empiric antifungal; CT early in course before cavitation (air crescent sign)
Reversed halo sign (atoll sign)Mucormycosis, organizing pneumonia, PCPRing of consolidation around central GGO; Mucor in immunocompromised > Aspergillus; aggressive angioinvasion, bone erosion
CavitationStaph (pneumatoceles), Klebsiella, TB, anaerobes (lung abscess), fungal (Aspergillus), NocardiaThick wall + air-fluid level = abscess; thin-walled = pneumatocele; upper lobe + satellite nodules = TB; neutropenic + rim-enhancing = Aspergillus
Tree-in-budEndobronchial TB, MAC, aspiration, bronchopneumoniaCentrilobular branching opacities; endobronchial spread of infection; active TB = infectious until sputum AFB cleared
Crazy pavingPCP, viral pneumonia, PAP, COVID-19GGO + interlobular septal thickening; PCP in HIV; consider PAP (bilateral symmetric, lower zone) if non-infectious causes
Fungal pearls: Endemic fungi (Histo, Coccidioides, Blasto) based on geography. Opportunistic fungi (Aspergillus, Mucor, Candida) in immunocompromised. Aspergilloma = fungus ball within pre-existing cavity (air crescent sign, Monod sign). Angioinvasive Aspergillus in neutropenia → halo sign early, air crescent sign on recovery.
📄Franquet T. Imaging of Pulmonary Viral Pneumonia. Radiology. 2011. / CT of Pulmonary Infections. AJR. 2013;201(6).
Pleural Disease
Split pleura sign = empyema: Enhancement and thickening of both visceral and parietal pleural layers with fluid separating them. Loculated effusion + fever + elevated WBC = empyema until proven otherwise — requires drainage. Distinguish from lung abscess: empyema is lenticular, conforms to pleural space, acute angles; abscess is within parenchyma, surrounded by lung.
DiagnosisImaging FindingsKey Points
Simple effusion (transudate)Dependent layering; meniscus sign on upright CXR; blunting of costophrenic angle (>200 mL); CT HU 0–15; no pleural enhancementCauses: CHF, cirrhosis, nephrotic syndrome; bilateral in CHF; treat underlying cause
EmpyemaLenticular collection conforming to pleural space; split pleura sign; loculations; CT HU 10–40; obtuse angles with chest wallComplication of pneumonia, trauma, surgery; requires drainage; thickened pleura + loculations = complex empyema
HemothoraxCT HU 35–70 (acute blood); dependent layering; hematocrit effectTrauma, coagulopathy, malignancy; loculation develops rapidly; retained hemothorax → fibrothorax; drain if large or infected
ChylothoraxCT HU near water (0–20); bilateral; large volume; may have fat-fluid levelThoracic duct injury, lymphoma, post-surgical; triglycerides >110 mg/dL diagnostic
Malignant effusionNodular pleural thickening; >1cm thickness; circumferential enhancement; mediastinal pleural involvementLung, breast, lymphoma, mesothelioma most common; mesothelioma: unilateral, circumferential, no mediastinal shift (encasement)

Aortic Dissection Classification
Stanford Classification
TypeInvolvementManagement
Type AAscending aorta (±arch, ±descending) — any dissection involving ascendingSurgical emergency — immediate OR
Type BDescending aorta only (distal to origin of L. subclavian artery)Medical management (BP control, heart rate control); TEVAR if complicated
Complicated Type B: Malperfusion (visceral, renal, limb) · Rupture / impending rupture (periaortic hematoma, hemothorax) · Refractory hypertension or pain despite maximal medical therapy → TEVAR
DeBakey Classification
TypeInvolvementStanford EquivalentManagement
IAscending aorta + arch + descending (entire aorta)Type ASurgical
IIAscending aorta only (stops at arch)Type ASurgical
III aDescending only, above diaphragmType BMedical / TEVAR
III bDescending, extends below diaphragmType BMedical / TEVAR
📋 Reporting Checklist — Aortic Dissection
  • Stanford type: A (involves ascending) / B (descending only); DeBakey I / II / IIIa / IIIb
  • Intimal flap: location (ascending / arch / descending) and longitudinal extent
  • Entry tear: most proximal location (cm from aortic valve / sinotubular junction)
  • Aortic root / annulus involvement (Type A): yes / no
  • Pericardial effusion / hemopericardium: present / absent; size if present
  • Coronary ostia involvement (Type A): right / left / both / none
  • Branch vessel involvement: celiac / SMA / renal arteries / iliac arteries
  • True vs false lumen: identify dominant flow lumen; false lumen thrombosis extent
  • End-organ malperfusion: bowel / renal infarct / spinal cord ischemia
  • Maximum aortic diameter and location; comparison to prior if available
Normal Aortic Measurements
LocationNormal DiameterAneurysm Threshold
Aortic root / sinus of Valsalva≤4.0 cm>4.5 cm (consider surgery with CTD/bicuspid)
Ascending aorta (mid)≤4.0 cm>5.0–5.5 cm; lower threshold with Marfan/bicuspid
Aortic arch≤3.5 cm>4.5 cm
Descending thoracic aorta≤2.5 cm>4.5–5.0 cm
Infrarenal aorta (AAA)<3.0 cm≥3.0 cm = aneurysm; repair ≥5.5 cm (men) / ≥5.0 cm (women)
Endoleak Classification (Post-EVAR)
TypeSourceAction
I (a/b)Attachment site leak (proximal = Ia; distal = Ib)Repair — high pressure; interventional or open
IIBranch vessel back-fill (IMA, lumbar arteries)Observe unless sac growing (>5mm in 6 mo); embolize if sac grows
IIIGraft defect / modular junction separationRepair — high pressure; relining or extension
IVGraft porosity (early post-op, <30 days)Usually self-limiting; stop anticoagulation if possible
V (endotension)Sac enlargement without identifiable leak on imagingInvestigate with delayed/dual-phase CT; may need reintervention
PE Classification
CategoryDefinitionMortality
MassiveSustained hypotension (<90 mmHg) >15 min OR requiring vasopressors25–65%
SubmassiveSBP >90 mmHg + RV strain (CT/echo/ECG/troponin/BNP)~3%
Low riskNone of the above<1%
RV strain on CT: RV:LV ratio >0.9 (four-chamber view) · Straightening or leftward bowing of IV septum · Reflux of contrast into IVC/hepatic veins
📄Sista AK et al. Stratification, Imaging, and Management of Acute Massive and Submassive PE. Radiology. 2017;284(1):5-24.
📋 Reporting Checklist — Pulmonary Embolism
  • Clot location: saddle / main PA / lobar / segmental / subsegmental (list affected vessels)
  • Bilateral vs unilateral involvement
  • RV/LV diameter ratio on axial CT (>1.0 = RV strain)
  • Interventricular septal bowing: D-sign present / absent; direction
  • Main pulmonary artery diameter (normal <29mm)
  • Pulmonary infarct: present / absent; location
  • Pleural effusion: present / absent; laterality; size
  • Clinical risk stratification: massive / submassive (RV strain) / low-risk

Fleischner Society 2017 Pulmonary Nodule Guidelines
Applies to incidentally detected nodules in patients ≥35 years without known malignancy or immunocompromise. High-risk features: history of smoking, family history of lung cancer, emphysema on CT, upper lobe location, spiculated margins.
Solid Nodules
MorphologySizeLow RiskHigh Risk
Single solid<6 mm (<100 mm³)No routine follow-upOptional CT at 12 mo
6–8 mm (100–250 mm³)CT at 6–12 mo, then 18–24 mo if stableCT at 6–12 mo, then 18–24 mo if stable
>8 mm (>250 mm³)CT at 3 mo, PET/CT, or tissue sampling (same for low and high risk)
Multiple solid<6 mmNo routine follow-upOptional CT at 12 mo
≥6 mm (dominant nodule)CT at 3–6 mo, then consider CT at 18–24 mo if stable
Subsolid Nodules
MorphologySizeRecommendation
Single pure GGO<6 mmNo routine follow-up
≥6 mmCT at 6–12 mo to confirm persistence; if persistent, CT every 2 yr until 5 yr
Single part-solid<6 mmNo routine follow-up
≥6 mmCT at 3–6 mo to confirm persistence; if stable and solid component <6 mm, annual CT for 5 yr; if solid component ≥6 mm, consider PET/CT or tissue sampling
Multiple subsolidAnyCT at 3–6 mo; if stable, manage based on most suspicious individual nodule using above criteria
Pearls: Part-solid nodules carry the highest malignancy risk per unit size — monitor solid component growth closely. GGOs: most are adenocarcinoma spectrum (AAH, AIS, MIA, invasive adenocarcinoma). Doubling time <400 days = suspicious for malignancy.
📄MacMahon H et al. Fleischner Society 2017. Radiology. 2017;284(1):228-243.

Pulmonary Edema & ARDS
FeatureCardiogenic EdemaARDS (Non-Cardiogenic)
DistributionCentral/perihilar ("bat wing"); gravity-dependent; bilateral symmetricDiffuse bilateral; peripheral and dependent; heterogeneous
Pleural effusionsBilateral, common; often moderate-to-largeUsually absent or minimal
CardiomegalyPresent (CTR >0.5 on PA CXR)Normal cardiac size
Air bronchogramsUncommonProminent; extensive
Septal lines (Kerley B)Present; horizontal peripheral lines at lung basesAbsent
Vascular pedicle widthWidened (>70mm on AP supine)Normal
Response to diuresisRapid improvementPersists despite diuresis
CXR/CT pearls: CXR lags behind clinical deterioration by 12–24h — normal CXR does NOT exclude early ARDS. HRCT: dependent GGO + consolidation with relative sparing of non-dependent lung. "Crazy paving" (GGO + septal thickening) seen in PCP, PAP, COVID-19. Serial CT useful for monitoring barotrauma complications (pneumatocele, PTX).
Foreign Body Aspiration
Key concepts: Right mainstem bronchus more commonly affected than left (more vertical, larger caliber, aligned with trachea). Adults (decreased consciousness, dental procedures): lower lobe predilection. Children: right > left. Organic/vegetable material (peanuts, nuts) is radiolucent on XR — CT required. Direct visualization of foreign body on CT is definitive; indirect signs more commonly encountered. Expiratory or lateral decubitus XR can reveal air trapping.
FindingMechanismNotes
Obstructive emphysema (air trapping)Ball-valve effect: air enters on inspiration, cannot exit on expiration; hyperlucent lobe/lung on CXR; CT: hyperlucency with reduced vascularity; mosaic attenuationMost common early finding; best seen on expiratory CXR or expiratory CT; contralateral mediastinal shift on expiration (Holzknecht sign)
AtelectasisComplete obstruction with absorption of distal air; lobar or segmental collapse; air bronchograms absent distal to obstructionMore common with complete occlusion; right lower lobe most common in adults; may develop over hours to days
Post-obstructive pneumoniaRetained secretions distal to obstruction lead to bacterial overgrowth; consolidation in obstructed lobe/segment; recurrent pneumonia in same locationRecurrent consolidation in identical location = foreign body or endobronchial lesion until proven otherwise; CT for direct visualization
Direct visualization on CTRadio-opaque foreign body (metal, bone, teeth) visible directly; radiolucent objects identified by bronchial wall thickening, surrounding air trapping, soft tissue density within airway lumenThin-slice CT with multiplanar reformats; virtual bronchoscopy can aid localization; bronchoscopy both diagnostic and therapeutic
📄Radiopaedia — Foreign body aspiration (adults)
📄Radiopaedia — Foreign body aspiration (children)
Thoracic Lymph Node Stations
Key stations: 1 = Low cervical/supraclavicular · 2R/L = Upper paratracheal · 4R/L = Lower paratracheal · 5 = Subaortic (AP window) · 7 = Subcarinal · 10 = Hilar · 11 = Interlobar · 12-14 = Peripheral. Short axis >1 cm = enlarged (except subcarinal: >1.2 cm).

Liver Segments (Couinaud)
Segments I–VIII based on hepatic veins (divide segments) and portal veins (divide right vs left). Functional right lobe: V–VIII. Functional left lobe: I–IV. Right hepatic vein: divides VI/VII from V/VIII. Middle hepatic vein: true lobar fissure (with gallbladder fossa).

Bladder Injury — AAST Grading & Rupture Types
Advance one grade for multiple injuries up to grade III.
GradeTypeDescription
IHematomaContusion, intramural hematoma
LacerationPartial thickness laceration
IILacerationExtraperitoneal bladder wall laceration <2 cm
IIILacerationExtraperitoneal (≥2 cm) or intraperitoneal (<2 cm) bladder wall laceration

TypeLocationKey Feature
IntraperitonealDome (weakest point)Contrast tracks along bowel loops, paracolic gutters — surgical repair
ExtraperitonealAnterolateral wallContrast in perivesical space (flame-shaped); usually assoc. with pelvic fracture — catheter drainage often sufficient
CombinedBothSurgical repair
📋 Reporting Checklist — Bladder Rupture
  • Type: extraperitoneal (EPR) / intraperitoneal (IPR) / combined — per CT cystography
  • Defect location: anterior wall / posterior wall / dome / bladder neck
  • Extent of extravasation: EPR (flame-shaped perivesical) / IPR (contrast outlining bowel loops)
  • Pelvic hematoma: size and location (perivesical / pre-sacral)
  • Associated pelvic ring fracture: present / absent
  • Urethral injury signs: urethral disruption / Foley catheter malposition
  • Foley catheter position: within bladder / malpositioned / not present
Urethral Injuries
Type I — Stretch/elongation, no extravasation · Type II — Partial disruption above UGD · Type III — Complete disruption above UGD (most common posterior injury) · Type IV — Bladder neck injury · Type V — Partial/complete anterior urethral injury
Retrograde urethrogram required to evaluate for urethral injury before catheter placement in pelvic fracture with blood at urethral meatus, perineal bruising, or inability to void.

Pancreatic Injury Grading — AAST
Advance one grade for multiple injuries up to grade III. Proximal pancreas = to the patient's right of the superior mesenteric vein.
GradeTypeDescription
IHematomaMinor contusion without duct injury
LacerationSuperficial laceration without duct injury
IIHematomaMajor contusion without duct injury or tissue loss
LacerationMajor laceration without duct injury or tissue loss
IIILacerationDistal transection or parenchymal injury with duct injury
IVLacerationProximal transection or parenchymal injury involving ampulla
VLacerationMassive disruption of pancreatic head
Key CT signs: Peripancreatic fluid/stranding · Parenchymal laceration (low-attenuation line) · Ductal injury suggested by complete transection or peripancreatic fluid tracking along duct · MRCP or ERCP for definitive duct assessment. Grade III–V injuries typically require surgery or endoscopic intervention.

CT signs of bowel injury: Free air (pneumoperitoneum) · Bowel wall discontinuity · Mesenteric hematoma/stranding · Free fluid without solid organ injury · Extraluminal oral contrast (if given). Any free air in trauma patient without prior laparotomy = bowel perforation until proven otherwise.
📄Radiopaedia — Small bowel trauma
Acute Appendicitis
CT criteria: Appendix diameter >6mm (outer wall to outer wall) + wall thickening + periappendiceal fat stranding. Appendicolith present in ~30% (increases perforation risk). MRI preferred in pregnancy and pediatrics. CT sensitivity ~94%, specificity ~95%.
FindingCTUSNotes
Appendix diameter >6mmOuter wall–outer wall; distended with fluidNon-compressible tubular structure >6mmPrimary criterion; measure at widest point
Periappendiceal fat strandingHaziness/streaking in periappendiceal fatEchogenic surrounding fatIncreases specificity when combined with dilation
AppendicolithCalcified focus within appendiceal lumen; extraluminal if perforatedShadowing echogenic intraluminal focus~30% of cases; associated with perforation/gangrene
Wall enhancement/thickeningStratified wall enhancement; wall >2mmHyperechoic mucosa with thickened layersLoss of enhancement = gangrenous/necrotic wall
Perforation signsFree air (RLQ), periappendiceal abscess (rim-enhancing collection), phlegmonFree fluid RLQ; disrupted wall layersPerforation rate ~20–30%; IR drainage for abscess >3cm
Non-visualizationMay be normal (retrocecal, pelvic) or suggest alternative diagnosisNon-visualization common (~20–30%); proceed to CT in adultsAlways report explicitly — do not call normal
Perforated appendicitis: Localized free extraluminal air in RLQ · Periappendiceal abscess · Phlegmon (ill-defined mass without discrete rim) · Free fluid in RLQ/pelvis. Gangrenous appendicitis: Absent mural enhancement · Intraluminal gas · Focal wall defect. Both require urgent surgical or IR consultation.
Pearls: State diameter + fat stranding + appendicolith separately in report. Retrocecal appendix (~26%): may present with flank pain — ensure full coronal/sagittal review. Tip appendicitis: isolated inflammation of the tip, may be subtle.
📄Radiopaedia — Acute appendicitis
📋 Reporting Checklist — Appendicitis
  • Appendix diameter: measure outer wall to outer wall (mm; >6mm abnormal)
  • Periappendiceal fat stranding: present / absent
  • Appendicolith: present / absent; location (tip / body / base)
  • Wall enhancement: normal stratified / loss of mural enhancement (gangrenous)
  • Perforation signs: localized free air / phlegmon / abscess (size if present)
  • Appendix position: retrocecal / pelvic / pre-ileal / other
  • Free fluid: present / absent; location
  • Alternative diagnosis if appendix not visualized
Adrenal Washout Calculator
APW = absolute percentage washout; RPW = relative percentage washout
Lesion TypeHU Criteria
Adrenal adenoma (lipid-rich)Unenhanced HU ≤10
Adrenal adenoma (washout)APW ≥60% OR RPW ≥40%
APW formula(Enhanced − Delayed) / (Enhanced − Unenhanced) × 100
RPW formula(Enhanced − Delayed) / Enhanced × 100
PheochromocytomaOften >10 HU unenhanced; avoid washout protocol if suspected
MyelolipomaMacroscopic fat (very low HU, <-30)

Bowel Ischemia
Critical finding: Pneumatosis intestinalis (intramural gas) + portal venous gas = grave prognosis — indicates advanced bowel necrosis with transmural infarction. Report immediately and obtain urgent surgical consultation. Distinguish pneumatosis from intraluminal gas: pneumatosis follows bowel wall contour, seen on multiple planes, often linear or bubbly in pattern.
SMA embolus vs thrombosis: SMA embolus: filling defect typically 3–10 cm from SMA origin (beyond origin at middle colic artery), often cardiac source (Afib, endocarditis); spares proximal jejunum. SMA thrombosis: occlusion at or near SMA origin, atherosclerotic disease, ischemia of entire SMA territory including proximal jejunum; longer segment ischemia; prior intestinal angina history.
EtiologyCT FindingsTerritory
SMA occlusion — embolusFilling defect in SMA 3–10 cm from origin; bowel wall thickening → thinning (necrosis); pneumatosis; portal venous gas; lack of bowel wall enhancement; mesenteric strandingMid-jejunum to transverse colon; spares proximal jejunum and duodenum (SMA branches beyond origin occluded)
SMA occlusion — thrombosisAtherosclerotic calcification/plaque at SMA origin; near-complete or complete occlusion at origin; extensive small bowel and right colon ischemia; collateral vessels may be visibleEntire SMA territory including proximal jejunum; duodenum may be involved; longer ischemic segment than embolus
SMV thrombosisHyperdense SMV (acute thrombus); filling defect in SMV ± portal vein; mesenteric edema and ascites (venous congestion); bowel wall thickening (target sign/halo); hemorrhagic infarction more common than arterial; pneumatosis less common earlyVariable; often patchy; mesenteric venous distribution; may spare arterially supplied areas; hypercoagulable states, portal hypertension, pancreatitis
Non-occlusive mesenteric ischemia (NOMI)Patent mesenteric vessels; diffuse bowel wall thickening; poor bowel wall enhancement; mesenteric edema; no filling defect; SMA vasospasm may be visible on angiographyWatershed areas most vulnerable: splenic flexure, sigmoid; diffuse distribution possible; ICU/low-flow states, dialysis, vasopressors
Colonic ischemia (watershed)Bowel wall thickening ± thumbprinting (submucosal edema); pericolonic stranding; watershed zones; typically no occlusion on CT; pneumatosis in severe casesSplenic flexure (Griffiths point) and rectosigmoid junction (Sudeck point) — watershed zones between SMA/IMA and IMA/internal iliac territories
Strangulation / closed-loopClosed loop morphology + wall thickening + loss of enhancement + mesenteric engorgement + ascites + pneumatosis; combination of arterial and venous compromise due to twistingIsolated closed loop; variable size; sigmoid or internal hernia most common; rapidly progressive to full-thickness necrosis
📄Menke J. Diagnostic accuracy of MDCT for acute mesenteric ischemia: A systematic review. Eur Radiol. 2010;20(12):2805-14.
📄Radiopaedia — Bowel ischaemia
Bowel Obstruction
Closed loop obstruction — surgical emergency: Two adjacent transition points creating an isolated loop with no decompression. Signs: C- or U-shaped dilated loop, mesenteric swirl (twisted mesentery), beak sign (tapered ends at transition), whirl sign (twisting of mesenteric vessels/fat), dilated loop with wall thickening or pneumatosis. Requires urgent surgical consultation regardless of other findings.
Caliber thresholds: SBO: dilated loops >2.5 cm proximal to transition point with decompressed loops distally. LBO: colon >6 cm (cecum >9 cm = impending perforation / Ogilvie risk). Water-soluble contrast enema or CT colonography can characterize LBO level and etiology. Pneumoperitoneum = perforation.
FeatureSBO (Small Bowel)LBO (Large Bowel)
LocationCentral abdomen; jejunum (left upper) vs ileum (right lower)Peripheral/frame-like distribution of dilated colon
Caliber threshold>2.5 cm (small bowel); >3 cm (duodenum)>6 cm colon; >9 cm cecum = imminent perforation risk
Transition pointDilated proximal loops → decompressed distal loops; identify cause (adhesion, hernia, mass)Transition at obstructing lesion (carcinoma, diverticular stricture, volvulus); may have competent ileocecal valve → closed loop cecal distension
Closed loopC/U-shaped dilated loop; two transition points; mesenteric swirl; beak sign; high risk of strangulationVolvulus creates closed loop (sigmoid or cecal); competent ileocecal valve in LBO creates cecal closed loop
Common causesAdhesions (most common post-surgical), incarcerated hernia (internal or external), Crohn's stricture, malignancy, intussusception, gallstone ileusColorectal carcinoma (most common), diverticular stricture, volvulus (sigmoid > cecal), extrinsic compression, Ogilvie syndrome (pseudo-obstruction)
📄Paulson EK et al. Acute small bowel obstruction: CT evaluation. Radiology. 2011;261(3):686-98.
📄Radiopaedia — Small bowel obstruction
📋 Reporting Checklist — Bowel Obstruction
  • Level: gastric outlet / proximal SBO / distal SBO / LBO
  • Transition point: location (describe relative to bowel anatomy / quadrant / adjacent structure)
  • Proximal bowel caliber at transition (mm); degree of dilation
  • Etiology if identifiable: hernia (type/location) / adhesion band / mass / volvulus / intussusception
  • Closed loop: present / absent (C/U-shaped loop, mesenteric swirl, beak sign)
  • Ischemia signs: wall thickening / pneumatosis / portal venous gas / mesenteric haziness / free fluid
  • Free air: present / absent (perforation)
  • Decompressed rectum: confirms complete obstruction
Gallbladder & Biliary Emergencies
Emphysematous cholecystitis: Gas in the gallbladder wall (intramural) or lumen on CT = gas-forming organisms (Clostridium, E. coli). Seen in diabetics, elderly, vascular disease. High mortality (15–25%) — emergent cholecystectomy (percutaneous cholecystostomy if too ill). Do not confuse with intraluminal gas (normal post-procedure) — intramural gas follows wall contour, non-dependent, linear/bubbly.
Gangrenous cholecystitis CT findings: Intraluminal membranes (desquamated mucosa — pathognomonic), irregular/asymmetric wall thickening, absent or asymmetric wall enhancement, pericholecystic fluid/stranding, discontinuous mucosal line. Perforation: pericholecystic abscess, free fluid, defect in wall. Higher surgical morbidity than uncomplicated cholecystitis.
DiagnosisUS FindingsCT FindingsUrgency
Acute cholecystitisGallstones + sonographic Murphy sign (tenderness with probe over GB); GB wall thickening >3 mm; pericholecystic fluid; GB distension (>5 cm transverse)GB wall thickening; pericholecystic fat stranding; GB distension; impacted stone in neck/cystic duct; periportal edemaUrgent cholecystectomy within 72h or percutaneous cholecystostomy in poor surgical candidates
Gangrenous cholecystitisIntraluminal membranes (echogenic strands); absent Murphy sign (denervation); irregular wall; no wall vascularity on DopplerIntraluminal membranes; wall irregularity/asymmetry; absent wall enhancement; pericholecystic abscess; pneumobiliaEmergent — higher complication rate; perforation risk; urgent surgical consultation
Emphysematous cholecystitisEchogenic foci in wall with "dirty shadowing" or ring-down artifact (gas); may obscure GB anatomyGas in GB wall (intramural, non-dependent) and/or lumen; pericholecystic gas; may have associated liver abscessEmergent cholecystectomy; gas-forming organisms; diabetic emergency; high mortality
CholedocholithiasisHyperechoic foci in CBD with posterior acoustic shadowing; CBD dilatation >6 mm (>8 mm post-cholecystectomy); visualized in ~50% by USHyperdense foci in CBD on non-contrast; MRCP superior for stones <3 mm; impacted ampullary stone at distal CBD; biliary dilatationUrgent ERCP if cholangitis or obstructive jaundice; elective if asymptomatic
Cholangitis (Charcot's / Reynolds pentad)Biliary dilatation; CBD stones; GB stones; may show periportal edema; abscess formation in severe casesBiliary dilatation; obstructing stone or stricture; periportal edema; hepatic abscesses (pyogenic); pneumobilia (post-sphincterotomy or emphysematous); portal vein thrombosisEmergent — Charcot's triad (RUQ pain, fever, jaundice) ± Reynolds pentad (+ shock + AMS). IV antibiotics + urgent biliary decompression (ERCP preferred); ICU level care
Gallstone ileusDistended bowel loops; large ectopic gallstone; biliary-enteric fistula (pneumobilia); may show cholecystoduodenal fistulaRigler's triad: SBO + ectopic gallstone + pneumobilia; stone typically at terminal ileum (narrowest point); Bouveret syndrome = gastric outlet obstruction (duodenal impaction)Urgent surgical enterolithotomy; fistula repair at same or staged procedure depending on patient stability
Mirizzi syndromeImpacted stone in GB neck/cystic duct causing extrinsic compression of CHD; proximal biliary dilatation with normal distal CBD; gallbladder contractedStone impacted at GB neck; fusiform narrowing of CHD/CBD at cystic duct level; biliary dilatation above; choledochal fistula in advanced cases (type II–IV)Urgent — high surgical complexity; ERCP pre-op for biliary drainage; cholecystectomy ± biliary reconstruction; distinguish from malignancy (Klatskin tumor)
📄Shakespear JS et al. CT findings of acute cholecystitis and its complications. AJR. 2010;194(6):1523-9.
📄Radiopaedia — Acute cholecystitis
📋 Reporting Checklist — Cholecystitis
  • Gallbladder wall thickness (mm; >3mm abnormal if fasting)
  • Sonographic Murphy sign: positive / negative / not assessed
  • Gallstones: present / absent; location (neck / body / fundus impaction)
  • Pericholecystic fluid / fat stranding: present / absent
  • GB distension: transverse diameter (>5cm abnormal)
  • Intraluminal membranes or sloughed mucosa (gangrenous features): present / absent
  • Wall enhancement on CT: hyperenhancing / heterogeneous / absent
  • Gas in GB wall or lumen (emphysematous): present / absent — surgical emergency
  • Biliary dilation: CBD diameter; choledocholithiasis present / absent
Diverticulitis
CT findings: Colonic diverticula + pericolonic fat stranding + colonic wall thickening at site of inflammation. Sigmoid colon >90%. CT sensitivity ~97%, specificity ~100%. Hinchey classification guides management of complicated disease.
StageDescriptionManagement
Hinchey IaPericolic/mesenteric phlegmon or confined pericolic abscess (<4 cm)IV antibiotics; most resolve conservatively
Hinchey IbPericolic abscess >4 cmIV antibiotics ± percutaneous drainage if accessible
Hinchey IIPelvic, retroperitoneal, or distant abscessPercutaneous drainage; elective surgery after resolution
Hinchey IIIPurulent peritonitis (ruptured pericolonic abscess)Surgical emergency
Hinchey IVFeculent peritonitis (free colonic perforation)Surgical emergency; highest mortality
Complicated FeatureCT FindingsManagement Implication
Contained perforation / phlegmonPericolonic gas bubbles + inflammatory mass; no free pneumoperitoneumIV antibiotics; admission; most resolve without intervention
Free perforationPneumoperitoneum; diffuse peritoneal enhancement; ascitesSurgical emergency (Hinchey III/IV)
AbscessRim-enhancing fluid collection adjacent to or distant from colon; may contain gas>3–4 cm = IR percutaneous drainage; elective sigmoid resection 6–8 weeks later
FistulaColovesical: air/fecal matter in bladder · Colovaginal: air in vaginaSurgical repair after bowel prep; colovesical most common
ObstructionLBO/SBO from extrinsic compression by phlegmon or fibrosisNG decompression; surgical consult if complete
Surgical emergency: Free air + diffuse peritoneal enhancement = Hinchey IV. Always report free intraperitoneal air with diverticulitis — even small amounts change management.
📄Radiopaedia — Hinchey classification
Ectopic Pregnancy
Ruptured ectopic — hemorrhagic emergency: Free pelvic/abdominal fluid + positive β-hCG = ruptured ectopic until proven otherwise. Do NOT delay for MRI. Hemodynamically unstable patient goes directly to OR. Hemodynamically stable: US to confirm. Hemoperitoneum extending to Morrison's pouch or paracolic gutters = large hemorrhage.
Heterotopic pregnancy: Simultaneous intrauterine and ectopic pregnancy. Rare in general population (~1:30,000) but significantly increased with ART (~1:100–500). Presence of an intrauterine pregnancy does NOT exclude a co-existing ectopic. Always evaluate adnexa even when IUP is confirmed in ART patients.
FindingDescription
Empty uterus with β-hCG >1500–2000 mIU/mLDiscriminatory zone: β-hCG above which IUP should be visible on TVUS (1500–2000 mIU/mL for TVUS; 6500 mIU/mL for transabdominal). Empty uterus above discriminatory zone = ectopic or failed IUP. Note: heterotopic can have IUP + ectopic simultaneously.
Ring of fire signTrophoblastic "ring of fire" Doppler signal around ectopic sac; echogenic ring on B-mode ("bagel sign"); distinct from the ovary (surrounded by ovarian parenchyma = corpus luteum cyst); TVUS most sensitive
Tubal ring sign / extrauterine gestational sacRound or oval adnexal structure separate from ovary; may contain yolk sac or embryo (definitive diagnosis); Doppler: "ring of fire" around ectopic sac (trophoblastic flow)
Free pelvic fluid (hemorrhage)Non-dependent echogenic free fluid in cul-de-sac, Morrison's pouch, paracolic gutters; complex/echogenic = hemoperitoneum; simple free fluid less specific; volume correlates with degree of hemorrhage
Interstitial ectopicGestational sac within myometrium at cornual region; interstitial line sign (echogenic line connecting ectopic sac to endometrium); thin myometrial rim <5 mm; presents later (8–12 weeks) with greater hemorrhage risk than tubal ectopic; 2–4% of ectopics; high mortality
📄Barnhart KT. Ectopic pregnancy. N Engl J Med. 2009;361(4):379-87.
📄Radiopaedia — Ectopic pregnancy
📋 Reporting Checklist — Ectopic Pregnancy
  • β-hCG level vs discriminatory zone (>1500–2000 mIU/mL should demonstrate IUP)
  • Uterine findings: IUP present / empty uterus / pseudogestational sac
  • Adnexal ring sign: location (tubal / interstitial / ovarian / cervical)
  • Gestational sac contents: yolk sac / embryo (CRL in mm if visible)
  • Cardiac activity: present / absent
  • Free pelvic fluid: location / amount / echogenicity (hemorrhagic = ruptured)
  • Myometrial rim thickness if interstitial (<5mm = high rupture risk)
  • Heterotopic pregnancy consideration if ART patient
Epiploic Appendagitis & Omental Infarct
Clinical importance: Both are self-limiting conditions treated conservatively (NSAIDs, analgesia). Both clinically mimic appendicitis or diverticulitis. CT is diagnostic and avoids unnecessary surgery and antibiotics. Report confidently when findings are characteristic to prevent unnecessary admission or intervention.
FeatureEpiploic AppendagitisOmental Infarct
LocationImmediately adjacent to colon wall (sigmoid most common, then cecum/ascending); antimesenteric borderLarger area of right-sided omentum most common (right lower quadrant > right upper quadrant); not directly attached to colon
SizeSmall, ovoid; typically 1.5–3.5 cm; proportional to appendage sizeLarger; typically >3–5 cm; cake-like fatty mass; may be very large (>10 cm)
Central hyperdense dotCentral hyperdense focus (thrombosed central vessel) within fatty lesion — characteristic sign (~70%)Absent (no central vessel); heterogeneous fatty stranding without central dot
Fat stranding patternOval pericolonic fat with thin hyperattenuating rim (inflamed/thrombosed epiploic appendage); surrounding inflammation limitedDiffuse, cake-like omental fat stranding; less well-defined; no discrete rim; larger area of involvement
Self-limitingYes — resolves in 2–4 weeks; rarely recurs; conservative managementYes — resolves in 4–6 weeks; conservative management; rarely requires surgery for refractory cases
📄Singh AK et al. Omental infarct and epiploic appendagitis: imaging characteristics and treatment. Emerg Radiol. 2005;11(2):82-7.
📄Radiopaedia — Epiploic appendagitis
📄Radiopaedia — Omental infarction

Strangulation — report urgently: Bowel wall thickening · Absent bowel wall enhancement · Pneumatosis intestinalis · Mesenteric edema/hemorrhage · Portal venous gas · Free peritoneal fluid. These indicate compromised bowel viability — surgical emergency. Always assess for strangulation when bowel is within any hernia sac.
📄Radiopaedia — Abdominal hernia
Ovarian Torsion
Ultrasound findings: Enlarged ovary (>4 cm, often >5 cm); peripheral follicles displaced to periphery (hallmark — "string of pearls"); ovary displaced from normal position (may be midline or contralateral); absent or decreased Doppler flow; free pelvic fluid; associated adnexal mass (dermoid, cystadenoma) in ~50–60% of cases; thickened edematous stroma.
Critical pitfall: Normal Doppler flow does NOT exclude ovarian torsion. Intermittent torsion or partial torsion can preserve some flow. Clinical presentation + enlarged ovary + peripheral follicles = high suspicion regardless of Doppler. This is a surgical emergency — diagnostic laparoscopy if clinical suspicion is high. Delay causes ovarian infarction and loss.
Pearls: Right > left (sigmoid colon limits left ovarian mobility). Associated ovarian mass in ~50–60% of cases — teratoma, cystadenoma most common. Pediatric patients: torsion can occur with normal ovaries. MRI if equivocal US — T2 hyperintense edematous stroma, absent enhancement in late/infarcted torsion. Doppler twisting of vascular pedicle ("whirlpool sign") is specific but not always present.
📄Shadinger LL et al. Preoperative sonographic and clinical characteristics as predictors of ovarian torsion. J Ultrasound Med. 2008;27(1):7-13.
📄Radiopaedia — Adnexal torsion
📋 Reporting Checklist — Ovarian Torsion
  • Side: right / left
  • Ovarian volume: L × W × H × 0.523 (mL); abnormal if >20cc or asymmetrically enlarged
  • Doppler flow: absent / reduced / asymmetric vs contralateral
  • Whirlpool sign of vascular pedicle: present / absent
  • Free pelvic fluid: present / absent; simple / complex / hemorrhagic; amount
  • Underlying ovarian lesion: size (cm), morphology
  • Uterine deviation toward affected side: present / absent
  • Contralateral ovary: normal / abnormal (volume and flow)
Pancreatitis
Revised Atlanta Classification: Interstitial edematous pancreatitis vs. necrotizing pancreatitis (parenchymal and/or peripancreatic necrosis). CT should be performed ≥48-72h after onset for accurate necrosis assessment.
CollectionTimingDefinition
Acute peripancreatic fluid (APFC)<4 wkInterstitial pancreatitis; no wall; homogeneous
Pseudocyst>4 wkEncapsulated fluid; well-defined wall; from APFC
Acute necrotic collection (ANC)<4 wkNecrotizing pancreatitis; contains heterogeneous debris
Walled-off necrosis (WON)>4 wkEncapsulated necrosis; endoscopic/surgical drainage

E≥2 peripancreatic fluid collections or gas in/adjacent to pancreas4

📋 Reporting Checklist — Pancreatitis
  • Necrosis: extent (%) and location — head / body / tail
  • Peripancreatic fluid collections: number, location, size (cm)
  • Walled-off necrosis vs pseudocyst: wall thickness, complexity, >4 weeks duration
  • Pancreatic duct: disruption / dilation (>3mm body, >5mm head) / disconnected duct
  • Splenic vein thrombosis: present / absent
  • Pseudoaneurysm: present / absent; vessel involved; size
  • Biliary: CBD dilation (>8mm) / choledocholithiasis / gallstones
Gastrointestinal Volvulus
Sigmoid volvulus: Massively dilated sigmoid colon >6 cm, apex pointing toward RUQ ("coffee bean" or "bent inner tube" pointing right), loss of haustral markings, convergence of walls at twist site. Cecal volvulus: ileocecal junction identified out of position (cecum absent from RLQ), dilated cecum in left upper abdomen, "coffee bean" opening toward LUQ. Both require urgent decompression or surgery.
TypeImaging SignsNotes
Sigmoid volvulusMassively dilated inverted U-shaped sigmoid loop; apex points to RUQ; "coffee bean" or "bent inner tube" sign on XR; convergence of walls at left pelvis (twist site); loss of haustra; CT: whirl sign at twist, beak sign, dilated sigmoid >6 cm; often massive colonic dilationMost common volvulus (60–75%); elderly/institutionalized patients; chronic constipation; sigmoid redundancy; endoscopic decompression often successful; high recurrence; sigmoidectomy definitive
Cecal volvulusCecum absent from RLQ; dilated ovoid gas-filled cecum in left mid-abdomen or LUQ; "coffee bean" opening toward LUQ; small bowel dilation; CT: whirl sign at ileocecal junction; cecum >10 cm; dilated terminal ileum; appendix may be visible with cecumSecond most common volvulus (25–40%); younger patients; congenital lack of retroperitoneal fixation; right hemicolectomy required; endoscopic decompression generally not definitive; high failure/recurrence rate; cecal bascule variant (folding without twisting)
Gastric volvulus — organoaxialStomach rotates around long axis (cardiopyloric axis); greater curvature flips superiorly; two air-fluid levels on upright CXR (double bubble); inversion of stomach; NGT cannot be passed; associated with diaphragmatic defectMore common type; associated with paraesophageal/diaphragmatic hernia; Borchardt triad: severe epigastric pain + retching without vomiting + inability to pass NGT; surgical emergency if strangulation
Gastric volvulus — mesenteroaxialRotation around short axis (perpendicular to cardiopyloric axis); antrum rotates anteriorly and superiorly; intermittent symptoms; partial obstruction patternLess common; often intermittent and partial; associated with diaphragmatic eventration; may reduce spontaneously; surgical repair of predisposing defect
📄Peterson CM et al. Volvulus of the gastrointestinal tract. AJR. 2009;192(2):W105-15.

Acetabular Fractures
📄Scheinfeld MH et al. Acetabular fractures. RadioGraphics. 2015;35(2):555-577.
📋 Reporting Checklist — Acetabular Fractures
  • Elementary fracture type: anterior wall / anterior column / posterior wall / posterior column / transverse
  • Associated fracture pattern: T-type / anterior column + posterior hemitransverse / both column / transverse + posterior wall
  • Femoral head: congruent / subluxed / dislocated (direction)
  • Articular step-off: measure in mm (surgical threshold typically >2–3mm)
  • Intraarticular fragments: present / absent; size and location
  • Posterior wall deficiency: estimate % involvement (surgical if >40%)
  • Marginal impaction of articular surface: present / absent
  • Ipsilateral femoral neck fracture: present / absent
Ankle Fractures
📋 Reporting Checklist — Ankle
  • Fracture level and pattern
  • Lateral and medial clear space (ligamentous integrity)
  • Weber/Lauge-Hansen: look for missed associated injury
  • Dislocation

Lauge-Hansen Classification

📄Okanobo H et al. Simplified diagnostic algorithm for Lauge-Hansen classification. RadioGraphics. 2012;32(2):E71-84.

Tibial Plafond (Pilon) Fractures — Ruedi-Allgower Classification

📋 Reporting Checklist — Pilon
  • Number and location of major fragments at the articular surface
  • Degree/location of articular depression (>2-3mm significant)
  • Associated tendon injury or entrapment
  • Syndesmotic disruption, bony fragments in syndesmosis
  • Retinaculum injury
Pilon fracture: High-energy axial-load fracture of the distal tibial articular surface (plafond). Caused by axial compression (fall from height, MVA). Classified by Rüedi-Allgöwer (I: undisplaced; II: displaced without comminution; III: comminuted/impacted) or AO/OTA system. Staged treatment: initial spanning external fixator → delayed ORIF.
Shoulder

Relevant Anatomy

Glenohumeral joint: Ball-and-socket; glenoid covers only 25–30% of humeral head → stability depends on labrum, capsule, rotator cuff, and ligaments.
Rotator cuff (SITS): Supraspinatus (abduction, initiation) · Infraspinatus (ER) · Teres minor (ER) · Subscapularis (IR, anterior stabilizer).
Glenoid labrum: Fibrocartilaginous ring deepening glenoid; anterior-inferior = most common Bankart site. Normal superior labrum variants (Buford complex, sublabral foramen).
Key ligaments: IGHL (inferior glenohumeral ligament = primary anterior stabilizer with arm abducted/ER) · MGHL · SGHL · CC ligaments (trapezoid + conoid = AC joint stability) · CA arch.

Glenohumeral Dislocation Types

TypeFrequencyXR FindingsAssociated Injuries
Anterior
(subcoracoid most common)		 ~95% Humeral head medial/inferior to glenoid; loss of normal overlap; head projects below coracoid on AP; Y-view: head anterior to glenoid center Hill-Sachs defect (posterolateral head impaction, ~35–40%); Bankart lesion (anteroinferior glenoid rim fracture, ~5–8% osseous); HAGL; GT fracture (elderly); rotator cuff tear (elderly)
Posterior
(often missed on AP)		 ~3–4% "Light bulb" sign (fixed IR of humeral head → symmetric rounded contour on AP); vacant glenoid sign (posterior widening of glenohumeral joint space >6 mm); "trough line" sign (medial impaction); Y-view: head posterior to glenoid center Reverse Hill-Sachs (anteromedial "trough" impaction); reverse Bankart (posterior glenoid rim); LT fracture; seizure or electrocution mechanism
Inferior (Luxatio Erecta) <1% Arm locked in abduction; humeral head displaced inferiorly below glenoid, inferior to coracoid on AP; arm held overhead clinically Axillary nerve/artery injury; rotator cuff tear; GT fracture; capsular tear; high-energy mechanism
Superior Rare Head displaced superiorly; AC joint disruption usually present AC joint disruption; clavicle fracture; rotator cuff destruction

📋 Reporting Checklist — Glenohumeral Dislocation
  • Direction: anterior / posterior / inferior (luxatio erecta)
  • Posterior: "light bulb" sign / vacant glenoid / trough line
  • Humeral head: Hill-Sachs defect (location, size); reverse Hill-Sachs "trough" (>25% involvement?)
  • Glenoid rim: Bankart fracture (osseous fragment size — % of glenoid width); reverse Bankart
  • Greater tuberosity fracture: present / displaced (>5 mm in young, >3 mm in athletes)
  • Lesser tuberosity: fracture (posterior dislocation marker)
  • Surgical neck: associated fracture (→ changes management)
  • Post-reduction: concentricity of joint; residual fragment interposition
