Clavicle
📋 Reporting Checklist — Clavicle
  • Fracture location (lateral/middle/medial third)
  • Angulation and end displacement direction
  • Comminution
  • Degree of overlap (generally surgical if >100% displaced)
  • AC and SC joint integrity
  • Coracoclavicular distance

Rockwood Classification of AC Joint Injury

Types I–III nonoperative; IV–VI operative
TypeCharacteristics
IClavicle not elevated relative to acromion
IIClavicle elevated, not above superior acromion border
IIIClavicle above acromion; CC <25mm (nonoperative)
IVClavicle displaced posterior into trapezius
VCC >25mm — operative
VIClavicle inferiorly displaced behind coracobrachialis/biceps — operative
Compartment Syndrome
Do not delay fasciotomy for imaging. Compartment syndrome is a clinical diagnosis (pain out of proportion, pain with passive stretch, paresthesias, paralysis, pallor, pulselessness — the "6 P's"). Compartment pressure >30 mmHg or within 30 mmHg of diastolic BP = fasciotomy. Promptly report any diffuse muscle swelling within a compartment and urge urgent clinical correlation. Irreversible muscle necrosis begins within 4–6 hours of ischemia.
Imaging role: Imaging is limited in acute compartment syndrome — it is a clinical diagnosis. MRI: diffuse T2/STIR hyperintensity of all muscles within involved compartment (edema/ischemia); loss of normal muscle architecture; absent enhancement in advanced ischemia/necrosis. Useful for: subacute/chronic exertional compartment syndrome (exercise-induced), post-fasciotomy assessment, evaluating extent of muscle necrosis. US: muscle swelling, loss of normal echogenicity (not routinely used for diagnosis).
CompartmentContentsClinical Sign of Compromise
Anterior legTibialis anterior, extensor hallucis longus (EHL), extensor digitorum longus (EDL), peroneus tertius; deep peroneal nerve; anterior tibial vesselsFoot drop (deep peroneal nerve palsy); weakness of ankle/toe dorsiflexion; sensory loss first web space; pain with passive toe/ankle plantarflexion
Posterior deep legTibialis posterior, flexor digitorum longus (FDL), flexor hallucis longus (FHL); posterior tibial nerve and vesselsWeakness of toe flexion and foot inversion; tibial nerve dysfunction (plantar numbness); pain with passive toe dorsiflexion; most commonly missed compartment
Lateral legPeroneus longus and brevis; superficial peroneal nerveWeakness of foot eversion; sensory loss dorsum of foot (superficial peroneal nerve); pain with passive foot inversion
Volar forearmFinger and wrist flexors (FDP, FDS, FCR, FCU), pronators; median and ulnar nerves; anterior interosseous nerveVolkmann's ischemic contracture (finger/wrist flexion contracture); weakness of finger/wrist flexion; median/ulnar nerve dysfunction; pain with passive finger extension; most common upper extremity compartment syndrome
📄Shadgan B et al. Diagnostic techniques in acute compartment syndrome. J Orthop Trauma. 2008;22(8):581-7.
📄Radiopaedia — Acute compartment syndrome
Distal Humerus
📋 Reporting Checklist — Distal Humerus
  • Column involvement — medial or lateral
  • Direction and displacement of epicondylar avulsion / single column fractures
  • Comminution or two-column injury

Coronoid Process Fractures

Type 1 — transverse tip · Type 2 — anteromedial facet (MCL injury) · Type 3 — base (>50%)

Olecranon Fractures — Mayo Classification

📋 Reporting Checklist — Olecranon
  • Degree of displacement (>2mm = surgery)
  • Comminution (= surgery)
Type 1 non-displaced · Type 2 displaced >3mm, intact MCL · Type 3 displaced + unstable
Elbow

Radial Head Fractures — Mason-Johnston Classification

📋 Reporting Checklist — Radial Head
  • Degree of displacement
  • Articular surface involvement (%)
  • Comminution or dislocation
Type 1 — no/minimal (<2mm) displacement · Type 2 — >2mm, 30–50% articular surface · Type 3 — comminution without dislocation · Type 4 — comminution + dislocation

Elbow Dislocation

Terrible Triad: Posterior elbow dislocation + radial head fracture + coronoid fracture. Get CT if 2/3 identified to exclude third injury.

Monteggia Fracture-Dislocation — Bado Classification

Ulnar fracture + proximal radius dislocation. All require surgery.
📄Sheehan SE et al. Traumatic elbow injuries. RadioGraphics. 2013;33(3):869-88.
Foot

Talus — Hawkins-Canale (Neck) & Sneppen (Body) Classifications

Hawkins sign: Subchondral lucent band of the talar dome seen 4–9 weeks after injury = revascularization = osteonecrosis will NOT develop.

Calcaneal Fractures — Sanders Classification

📋 Reporting Checklist — Calcaneus
  • Cortical fragments between dominant fragments (impede reduction)
  • Extension into calcaneocuboid joint
  • Number of sagittal lines entering posterior subtalar joint
  • Degree of articular fragment depression
  • Peroneal retinaculum avulsion
  • Peroneal tendon entrapment
  • Fragments inferior to sustentaculum tali — FHL tendon or tibial nerve entrapment

Midfoot

Lisfranc Injury

Low energy (sprain): Lateral/dorsal M2 on C2 displacement (most reliable) · >2mm M1-M2 diastasis on AP · Fleck sign (avulsion from M2 base).
High energy (fracture-dislocation): Homolateral (all MTs lateral) · Divergent (M1 medial, M2–M5 lateral) · Isolated.
Phalangeal tuft fracture: correlate for nailbed injury (open fracture). Isolated lesser trochanter avulsion: think malignancy.
📄Siddiqui NA et al. Tarsometatarsal joint evaluation. RadioGraphics. 2014;34(2):514-31.
Hand & Wrist

Distal Radius Fractures

📋 Reporting Checklist — Distal Radius
  • Loss of volar tilt
  • Loss of radial inclination
  • Shortening relative to ulna
  • Intraarticular extension
  • Articular diastasis/depression
  • Comminution
  • Ulnar styloid fracture — location, displacement; if base fracture check DRUJ/TFCC integrity
  • Disruption of carpal arcs
Essex-Lopresti: radial head fracture + DRUJ dislocation + interosseous membrane rupture.
Knee

Tibial Plateau — Schatzker Classification

📋 Reporting Checklist — Tibial Plateau
  • Split, depressed, or bicondylar morphology
  • Contralateral joint space widening
  • Articular step-off (mm)
  • Non-surgical if diastasis <3–4 mm AND depression <4–5 mm

Patellar Fractures

Surgical if: >2 mm articular incongruity · >3 mm fragment separation · extensor mechanism disruption

Knee Ligamentous & Avulsion Injuries

Fibular Fractures

Fibular head fracture: peroneal nerve injury (foot drop) + posterolateral corner injury risk.
Fibular shaft fracture: image the ankle to exclude Maisonneuve fracture.
Mid Tibia / Fibula
📋 Reporting Checklist — Mid Tibia/Fibula
  • Simple, complex, or wedge morphology
  • Shortening or distraction
  • Intercalated fragment (segmental fracture)
Key terms: Butterfly fragment — triangular, not entire circumference · Ring butterfly — full circumference · Bayonet apposition — overriding fragments · Segmental fracture — intercalated fragment, impaired vascularity
Pelvic Fractures
Stable pelvic fractures: Isolated pubic ramus/ischium · Unilateral both rami · Isolated sacral (transverse) · Avulsion fractures · Duverney (iliac wing, associated with ileus)

Young & Burgess Classification

PatternMechanismInstability
LC I–IIILateral force; sacral fracture, ramusUsually stable (LC I); rotational (LC II–III)
APC I–IIIAnterior force; symphysis diastasis (open book)APC II–III unstable; major vascular risk
VSAxial force; vertical hemipelvis displacementHighly unstable; highest mortality
📄Khurana B et al. Pelvic ring fractures. RadioGraphics. 2014;34(5):1317-1333.
📋 Reporting Checklist — Pelvic Fractures
  • Ring disruption: complete / incomplete; number of disruption sites
  • Classification: APC (I/II/III) / LC (I/II/III) / VS / CM — specify grade
  • Anterior ring: pubic symphysis diastasis (mm) / pubic rami fractures (ipsilateral / contralateral / bilateral)
  • Posterior ring: SI joint disruption (widening mm) / sacral fracture — Denis zone (I/II/III)
  • Acetabular fractures: present / absent
  • Pelvic hematoma size; active arterial extravasation → recommend IR
  • Urologic injury signs: bladder / urethral injury
  • Associated L5 transverse process fracture: present / absent
Proximal Femur Fractures
TypeLocationClassificationKey Points
Femoral HeadIntracapsularPipkin (I–IV)Complication of hip dislocation; I = below fovea; II = above fovea; III + neck fracture; IV + acetabular fracture
Subcapital / Femoral NeckIntracapsularGarden (I–IV)AVN risk increases with grade; I = impacted valgus; II = complete nondisplaced; III = partial displaced; IV = fully displaced. <65 yrs → ORIF; >65 yrs → hemiarthroplasty or THA

Intracapsular — Pipkin Classification (Femoral Head)

Hip Dislocation

📋 Reporting Checklist — Hip Dislocation
  • Direction: posterior (most common, ~90%) vs anterior
  • Posterior: femoral head displaced superolaterally; lesser trochanter NOT visible on AP
  • Anterior: femoral head displaced inferomedially; obturator or ilioinguinal subtypes
  • Associated femoral neck fracture — closed reduction CONTRAINDICATED
  • Associated femoral head impaction fracture (more common anteriorly → THA)
  • Post-reduction: assess concentricity; femoral head AVN risk (↑ with delay >6 hrs)

Femoral Neck Fractures — Garden Classification

📋 Reporting Checklist — Femoral Neck
  • Location: subcapital / transcervical / basicervical
  • Displaced or nondisplaced
  • Garden grade (higher grade = higher AVN risk)
  • Contralateral hip imaging if stress fracture suspected

Intertrochanteric — Evans Classification

Stability key factor — determines fixation strategy (sliding hip screw vs cephalomedullary nail).
TypePatternStability

📋 Reporting Checklist — Intertrochanteric
  • Posteromedial cortex: intact (stable) vs comminuted (unstable)
  • Greater trochanter: separate fragment?
  • Reverse obliquity pattern (unstable — requires nail, not SHS)
  • Subtrochanteric extension

Subtrochanteric Fractures

Isolated lesser trochanter avulsion in adults → malignancy until proven otherwise.
Subtrochanteric zone = lesser trochanter to 5 cm below. High-stress region: lateral cortex under tension, medial cortex under compression → requires cephalomedullary nail (not sliding hip screw).
FeatureTraumatic (High-Energy)Atypical (Bisphosphonate)
PatientYoung, high-energy mechanismOlder; long-term bisphosphonate use (>3–5 yrs)
Fracture orientationComminuted, spiral, obliqueTransverse or short oblique
Lateral cortexVariablePeriosteal thickening / "beaking" or flare
Medial spikeMay be presentCharacteristic medial spike
ComminutionCommonAbsent or minimal
ProdromeNoneThigh/groin pain before fracture
BilateralRare28–47% — image contralateral femur

📄Sheehan SE et al. Proximal Femoral Fractures. RadioGraphics. 2015;35(5):1563–84.
Proximal Humerus
Neer 4-part classification: A "part" = segment displaced >1 cm OR angulated >45°. Four anatomic segments: humeral head, greater tuberosity (GT), lesser tuberosity (LT), humeral shaft. Most proximal humerus fractures (80–85%) are 1-part (minimally displaced) → conservative management.
TypeParts InvolvedKey FeatureManagement Pearl
1-PartAny segment; none displaced >1 cm / >45°Most common (80–85%)Sling immobilization; early PT
2-Part Surgical NeckShaft + head-GT-LT unitMost common 2-part; shaft displaced anteriorly/medially by pectoralis majorUsually closed reduction + percutaneous pinning or ORIF
2-Part GTGT displaced from head>5 mm displacement → rotator cuff equivalent; >3–5 mm surgical in young active patientsSurgical fixation if displaced — risk of impingement/cuff dysfunction
2-Part LTLT displaced (subscapularis avulsion)Associated with posterior glenohumeral dislocation; posterior Hill-Sachs and reverse BankartORIF; assess for associated dislocation
3-PartGT or LT + surgical neckRotator cuff imbalance; head rotated by intact cuff attachmentORIF (younger) or hemiarthroplasty (older/osteoporotic)
4-PartAll 4 segmentsAVN risk 13–34% (blood supply from anterior circumflex stripped)THA or hemiarthroplasty; ORIF in young patients with good bone quality
4-Part Valgus-ImpactedAll 4 segments; head impacted into shaft in valgusBetter prognosis despite appearance; medial periosteum preserved → lower AVN (10–20%)ORIF preferred; more predictable outcome than classic 4-part
Head-SplittingArticular surface fractureCoronal split of humeral head; often comminuted articular surfaceUsually requires arthroplasty; ORIF rarely feasible
Associated injuries to assess: Hill-Sachs defect (posterolateral head impaction with anterior dislocation) · Reverse Hill-Sachs (anteromedial, with posterior dislocation) · Bankart/reverse Bankart (glenoid rim) · HAGL (humeral avulsion of glenohumeral ligament) · Rotator cuff tears (US/MRI if clinically suspected after healing)
📋 Reporting Checklist — Proximal Humerus
  • Number of parts: identify GT, LT, surgical neck, head
  • Each part: displacement (>1 cm?) and angulation (>45°?)
  • GT displacement direction (superior vs posterior)
  • Head-shaft angle: valgus vs varus alignment
  • Glenohumeral joint: dislocation? Hill-Sachs? Bankart?
  • Articular surface: head-splitting component?
  • Acromion and coracoid: fracture? AC joint?
📄Sandstrom CK et al. Acute Shoulder Trauma. RadioGraphics. 2015;35(2):475–92.
Scapula
📋 Reporting Checklist — Scapula
  • Scapular body: location, displacement, comminution
  • Glenoid (Ideberg): articular surface involvement; step-off; Ideberg type (I–VI)
  • Scapular neck: displaced (>1 cm translation Type 2a) or angulated (>40° Type 2b)
  • Acromion: fracture type (I nondisplaced; II displaced; III with rotator cuff compromise)
  • Coracoid: fracture (base vs tip); associated CC ligament disruption
  • Superior Suspensory Complex (SSC): 2 disruptions = unstable/surgical; 3 = floating shoulder
    SSC ring: glenoid → coracoid → CC ligament → distal clavicle → AC joint → CA ligament → acromion
  • Associated injuries: rib fractures, pneumothorax/hemothorax, clavicle, neurovascular
  • Glenohumeral joint: dislocation or subluxation
Ideberg classification of glenoid fossa fractures
TypeDescription
1a/1bAnterior/posterior rim
2Transverse to lateral margin
3Transverse to superior margin
4Transverse to medial margin
5a/5b/5cCombined transverse
6Comminuted crush
Infection
Necrotizing Fasciitis
SURGICAL EMERGENCY: Gas tracking along fascial planes is pathognomonic on CT. Do not delay surgical debridement for additional imaging if clinical suspicion is high. CT findings: gas in fascia (linear/bubbly hypodensities along fascial planes), asymmetric fascial thickening, fluid tracking along fascial planes, absent or diminished fascial enhancement, skin thickening. Report any gas in soft tissue planes with urgency. Mortality 20–40% — increases significantly with operative delay beyond 12–24 hours.
Imaging limitations: CT sensitivity 88–90%, specificity ~93%. Negative CT does NOT exclude necrotizing fasciitis. If clinical suspicion is high (pain out of proportion, rapid progression, skin changes, toxicity, crepitus), operate regardless of CT findings. LRINEC score (Laboratory Risk Indicator for Necrotizing Fasciitis) can aid but does not replace clinical judgment.
TypeOrganismsLocation / Population
Type I — polymicrobialMixed aerobic + anaerobic (E. coli, Klebsiella, Bacteroides, Peptostreptococcus, Clostridium); synergistic infectionMost common type (~80%); trunk, perineum, lower extremities; diabetics, immunocompromised, post-operative, elderly; gas production common from anaerobes
Type II — monomicrobial (Group A Strep)Group A Streptococcus pyogenes (±S. aureus); potent exotoxins; toxic shock syndromeExtremities; previously healthy adults; minor trauma or no entry point; rapidly progressive; toxic shock syndrome (fever, hypotension, multiorgan failure); less gas than Type I
Fournier's gangrenePolymicrobial (Type I); E. coli, Klebsiella, Bacteroides, ClostridiumPerineum, scrotum, perianal region; males >> females; diabetics; CT: gas in scrotal/perineal soft tissues, fascial thickening, abscess; can spread to thigh and abdominal wall via fascial planes; wide surgical debridement ± orchiectomy
📄Malghem J et al. Necrotizing fasciitis: contribution and limitations of diagnostic imaging. Joint Bone Spine. 2013;80(2):146-54.
📄Radiopaedia — Necrotizing fasciitis
Osteomyelitis & Septic Arthritis
MRI — gold standard: T1: marrow replacement (low signal replacing normal bright fatty marrow — most sensitive finding); STIR/T2: hyperintense edema in marrow and surrounding soft tissues; post-contrast: abnormal marrow enhancement, periosteal enhancement, soft tissue/joint enhancement. Periosteal reaction on radiograph delayed 7–10 days after onset — normal XR does not exclude osteomyelitis. Three-phase bone scan useful when MRI contraindicated.
Septic arthritis — urgent: Joint space widening + periarticular edema + joint effusion on MRI. Cartilage destruction rapid (within 24–48h of untreated infection). Requires urgent joint aspiration (synovial fluid WBC >50,000, predominant PMNs) and surgical washout. Do not delay aspiration for MRI if clinical suspicion high. Common organisms: S. aureus (all ages), N. gonorrhoeae (sexually active adults), Group B Strep (neonates).
FeatureOsteomyelitisSeptic Arthritis
Primary MRI sequenceT1 (marrow replacement); STIR (edema); post-contrast T1 fat-sat (enhancement, abscess rim)T2/PD fat-sat (effusion, synovitis); post-contrast T1 fat-sat (synovial enhancement, pannus); STIR (periarticular edema)
Key MRI findingsT1 low signal replacing normal marrow fat; STIR/T2 marrow hyperintensity; periosteal reaction; subperiosteal abscess (rim-enhancing fluid collection lifting periosteum); Brodie abscess (focal T2 bright lesion with penumbra sign)Joint effusion (T2 bright); synovial thickening and enhancement; periarticular bone marrow edema (reactive); cartilage thinning/destruction (late); joint space narrowing
Periosteal reactionDelayed 7–10 days on XR; aggressive (permeative, onion-skin, Codman triangle) in acute hematogenous; visible earlier on MRI as periosteal T2 hyperintensity and enhancementNot primary feature; periarticular osteopenia on XR; erosions in chronic/RA; subchondral changes late
Joint involvementSecondary involvement via direct extension from metaphysis (children: metaphysis vascular, adjacent to physis); septic arthritis can coexist especially in hip/shoulder where metaphysis is intracapsularPrimary joint space involvement; metaphyseal osteomyelitis can seed joint; always image adjacent bone when septic arthritis diagnosed
Common organismsS. aureus (most common all ages); S. epidermidis (post-op/implant); Salmonella (sickle cell); Pseudomonas (IV drug use, diabetic foot); MRSA increasingly prevalentS. aureus (most common); N. gonorrhoeae (young adults); Group B Strep/gram-neg (neonates); Kingella kingae (children <5); Strep pneumoniae
📄Pineda C et al. Radiographic imaging in osteomyelitis: the role of plain radiography, CT, ultrasonography, magnetic resonance imaging and scintigraphy. Semin Plast Surg. 2009;23(2):80-9.
📄Radiopaedia — Osteomyelitis
Pediatrics
Salter-Harris Classification
I
Through physis only — periosteal sleeve; X-ray may be normal
II
Through physis + metaphysis (most common; Thurston-Holland corner fragment)
III
Through physis + epiphysis (intra-articular; risk growth arrest — surgical)
IV
Metaphysis + physis + epiphysis (crosses entire plate — surgical)
V
Crush/compression of physis (poor prognosis; often missed acutely)
SCFE — Slipped Capital Femoral Epiphysis
Stable (can bear weight) vs Unstable (cannot — higher AVN risk). Klein's line should intersect >⅙ of femoral head on AP. Lateral view most sensitive for early cases.

Appendix — Ultrasound
Technique: Graded compression at point of maximum RLQ tenderness, progressively displacing overlying bowel gas. Normal appendix: <6mm, compressible, no surrounding fat stranding. Non-visualization does NOT exclude appendicitis — proceed to CT in adults if clinically indicated.
FindingSensitivitySpecificity
Appendix >6mm, non-compressible, blind-ending~75–85%~90–95%
Periappendiceal hyperechoic fat stranding~70%~93%
Appendicolith (shadowing echogenic focus)~25–35%~99%
Perforation (loss of wall layers, free fluid)~50%~95%
Hyperemia on color Doppler~87%~96%
Operator and patient factors: Highly operator-dependent; best in thin patients and children. Non-visualization rate ~20–30% in adults. Algorithm: US first in children/pregnancy → non-diagnostic in adults → CT; in pregnancy → MRI preferred over CT.
📄Radiopaedia — Acute appendicitis (ultrasound)

Lower Extremity DVT
Technique — compression ultrasound (CUS): Non-compressibility of a vein with transducer pressure = DVT (primary criterion). Assess bilaterally: common femoral vein (CFV), femoral vein (FV), popliteal vein, posterior tibial and peroneal veins. Add color Doppler and augmentation.
FindingAcute DVTChronic DVT
CompressibilityNon-compressible; vein lumen does not collapse; may feel "spongy"Non-compressible; firm and fibrotic; may be partially compressible with recanalization
EchogenicityAnechoic or hypoechoic; fresh thrombus may be nearly invisible — Doppler essentialHeterogeneous; hyperechoic; echogenic organized thrombus; wall thickening and fibrosis
Vein caliberEnlarged and distendedContracted, narrowed, or normal with recanalization
Color DopplerAbsent or markedly reduced intraluminal flow; filling defectPartial recanalization with flow around organized thrombus; collateral vessels
AugmentationAbsent with distal compressionReduced or present with recanalization
Respiratory phasicity (CFV)Absent phasic variationMay be absent or reduced
Proximal DVT (CFV, FV, popliteal) = high PE risk — anticoagulation required. Report level of proximal extent explicitly. Isolated distal (infrapopliteal/calf) DVT: serial CUS in 1 week if anticoagulation withheld; anticoagulate if high PE risk or symptomatic.
Misnomer alert: The "superficial femoral vein" is a DEEP vein — DVT here carries the same PE risk and requires full anticoagulation. Use "femoral vein" in reports to avoid clinical confusion.
📄Radiopaedia — Deep vein thrombosis
📋 Reporting Checklist — DVT
  • Veins involved: common femoral / femoral / popliteal / posterior tibial / peroneal / great saphenous (list all)
  • Laterality: right / left / bilateral
  • Proximal extent: document most cephalad level of thrombus
  • Free-floating thrombus: present / absent (increased PE risk — note prominently)
  • Acute vs chronic characteristics (echogenicity, collaterals, wall thickening)
  • Compression documented at all levels examined
  • IVC involvement: assessed / not assessed
OB / First Trimester / PUL
Discriminatory zone (for IUP): β-hCG threshold above which IUP should be visible (transvaginal: ~1500–2000 IU/L for GS). Absence of IUP above threshold raises concern for ectopic or failed IUP.
FindingCriteria for Nonviable / Action
No cardiac activityCRL ≥7 mm (transvaginal) → nonviable
Empty amnionAmnion seen without embryo → nonviable
Large yolk sac>7 mm → abnormal
No GS growth<1 mm/day growth on follow-up → nonviable
EctopicExtrauterine GS with yolk sac/embryo = definitive; adnexal ring sign; free fluid
📄NEJM: Diagnostic Criteria for Nonviable Pregnancy Early in the First Trimester
Retained Products of Conception (RPOC)
Clinical context: RPOC should be considered after any pregnancy event — spontaneous abortion, therapeutic abortion, delivery, or molar pregnancy. Presents with abnormal uterine bleeding, pelvic pain, or fever. Transvaginal ultrasound (TVUS) is the primary imaging modality. Color Doppler is essential to distinguish RPOC from blood clot (no flow) and to assess vascularity, which guides management.
Threshold for diagnosis: Endometrial thickness alone is insufficient. An endometrial thickness >10 mm with heterogeneous echogenicity and/or internal vascularity on Doppler is the most predictive combination. Thin, homogeneous endometrium (<10 mm, no flow) makes RPOC unlikely but does not exclude it — clinical correlation essential.
FindingDescriptionSignificance
Endometrial thickness >10 mmSingle-layer measurement on sagittal transvaginal image; measure at thickest point excluding fluidMost used threshold; sensitivity ~80%, specificity ~65% alone — must combine with echogenicity and Doppler
Heterogeneous endometrial echogenicityMixed echogenic/hypoechoic material within endometrial cavity; may contain echogenic foci (calcification from prior procedure)Highly suspicious when combined with vascularity; heterogeneous contents without flow = likely blood clot
Intraendometrial vascularity (color Doppler)Color flow within endometrial contents — most specific sonographic finding for RPOC; high-velocity, low-resistance waveform on spectral Doppler (RI < 0.4 suggests retained trophoblastic tissue)Most specific finding; presence of flow strongly supports RPOC over hematoma; absence of flow favors organized clot
Gestational sac remnantRecognizable GS, yolk sac, or embryonic tissue within the cavityDefinitive — always represents RPOC
Echogenic endometrial massWell-defined echogenic intracavitary mass; may show shadowing if calcification present (chronic RPOC)Chronic RPOC may show echogenic calcified foci from prior incomplete evacuation
Free fluid in cul-de-sacNon-specific; may accompany RPOC with hemorrhage or infectionCorrelate with clinical symptoms; large free fluid + fever → consider septic RPOC
Doppler pearls: Subinvolution of the placental site (SIPS) — an underdiagnosed cause of late postpartum hemorrhage — shows prominent myometrial vascularity at the implantation site without discrete tissue. Distinguished from arteriovenous malformation (AVM) by absence of a tangle of vessels; AVM shows turbulent high-velocity low-resistance flow in a myometrial nidus often with color aliasing. Always assess the entire myometrium, not just the endometrium.
DifferentialKey Distinguishing Features
Blood clot / hematomaNo internal Doppler flow; may be echogenic or heterogeneous; changes shape with pressure; resolves on follow-up
Uterine AVM (arteriovenous malformation)Turbulent mosaic color flow in myometrium; high-velocity low-resistance waveforms; often post-curettage; do NOT curettage — hemorrhage risk
Subinvolution of placental site (SIPS)Myometrial vascularity at implantation site without discrete mass; late postpartum (>6 weeks); low-resistance flow; no tangle of vessels
EndometritisThickened endometrium with gas (echogenic foci with dirty shadowing); fever, uterine tenderness; may coexist with RPOC
Endometrial polypSmooth, well-defined echogenic intracavitary lesion; pedicle vessel on Doppler (feeding vessel); no recent pregnancy history
Gestational trophoblastic disease (GTD)Markedly elevated β-hCG; heterogeneous "snowstorm" appearance; prominent myometrial vascularity; follows molar pregnancy
📋 Reporting Checklist — RPOC
  • Endometrial thickness (mm) — single layer, at thickest point
  • Echogenicity of endometrial contents: homogeneous / heterogeneous / echogenic mass
  • Internal vascularity on color Doppler: present / absent
  • If flow present: waveform character (high-velocity, low-resistance RI <0.4 = trophoblastic)
  • Presence of recognizable gestational sac, yolk sac, or embryo
  • Myometrial vascularity: normal / increased (consider AVM or SIPS if prominent)
  • Free fluid: none / trace / moderate with location
  • Clinical context: post-SAB / post-TOP / post-delivery / post-molar — gestational age at event
  • Impression: findings consistent with / not suggestive of RPOC; recommend clinical correlation and β-hCG
📄Kamaya A et al. Retained Products of Conception. Semin Ultrasound CT MR. 2015;36(4):327-35. PMID 26254191.
📄Alcazar JL. Transvaginal ultrasonography combined with color velocity imaging and pulsed Doppler to detect residual trophoblastic tissue. Ultrasound Obstet Gynecol. 1998;11(1):54-8.
📄Radiopaedia — Retained products of conception

Retroperitoneal / Renal Transplant Normals
Testicular Torsion — Ultrasound
Surgical emergency — time is testis: <6h = >90% salvage · 6–12h = ~50% · 12–24h = ~10% · >24h = <10%. Do NOT delay surgical exploration if the clinical diagnosis is certain. Call the surgeon immediately when diagnosed on imaging.
FindingDescription
Absent/reduced Doppler flowMost sensitive finding; compare symmetrically to contralateral testis using identical settings; complete absence (complete torsion) or asymmetric reduction (partial/intermittent)
Heterogeneous testisHeterogeneous echotexture develops with infarction after ~6h; early torsion may have normal gray-scale — Doppler is critical
Whirlpool signTwisting of the spermatic cord as a whorled/spiraling appearance at the external ring or superior pole; highly specific (~99%)
Reactive hydroceleSimple ipsilateral hydrocele; common but nonspecific
Enlarged/edematous epididymisReactive; distinguish from epididymo-orchitis (increased epididymal Doppler flow)
Scrotal wall thickeningSecondary reactive edema; nonspecific
Critical pitfall: Normal or increased Doppler flow does NOT exclude torsion — intermittent/partial torsion and early torsion (<2h) can have preserved flow. If clinical suspicion is high, proceed to surgical exploration regardless of Doppler result.
📄Radiopaedia — Testicular torsion

Developmental Dysplasia of the Hip (DDH)
Graf alpha angle (ultrasound): >60° = normal · 50–60° = mildly dysplastic · <50° = dysplastic. Assess at 6 weeks (before ossification of femoral head). X-ray (after 4–6 months): Hilgenreiner's/Perkin's lines; Shenton's line continuity.
Hypertrophic Pyloric Stenosis (HPS)
Clinical context: Presents 2–8 weeks of age (peak 3–5 weeks); firstborn males more common; projectile non-bilious vomiting; palpable "olive" in epigastrium ~70%. US is diagnostic study of choice (sensitivity ~97–99%, specificity ~100%).
US CriterionNormalHPS (Positive)
Pyloric muscle thickness (single wall)<3mm≥4mm
Pyloric channel length<14mm≥17mm
Pyloric transverse diameter<13mm≥13mm
Technique and pitfalls: Measure on a RELAXED pylorus — do not measure during active contraction (falsely elevated). Real-time: absent gastric emptying through pylorus despite active antral peristalsis. Electrolyte consequence: hypochloremic, hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis — correct BEFORE surgical pyloromyotomy (Ramstedt procedure).
📄Radiopaedia — Hypertrophic pyloric stenosis
Intraventricular Hemorrhage Grading (Papile)
I
Germinal matrix hemorrhage only (subependymal)
II
IVH without ventricular dilation
III
IVH with ventricular dilation
IV
IVH + periventricular hemorrhagic infarction (PVHI) — not true extension
Intussusception
Most common bowel obstruction in children age 3 months–5 years. Ileocolic type ~95%. Classic triad: colicky pain + currant jelly stool + palpable mass — only ~50% present with all three. Treat with pneumatic or hydrostatic enema reduction under fluoroscopy or US guidance.
US findings: "Target sign" (donut sign) — concentric rings of bowel-within-bowel on transverse view; "pseudokidney sign" — elongated kidney-shaped mass on longitudinal view. US is primary diagnostic modality (sensitivity ~98%, specificity ~88%).
FeatureDescription
Target/donut sign (transverse US)Hypoechoic outer rim (intussuscipiens) surrounding echogenic center (intussusceptum with mesenteric fat); multiple concentric rings
Pseudokidney sign (longitudinal US)Elongated ovoid mass resembling a kidney; hyperechoic mesenteric fat surrounded by hypoechoic bowel wall layers; most common in RUQ/epigastrium
Lead pointIdentified in ~5% of cases; Meckel diverticulum, polyp, duplication cyst, lymphoma; suspect if age >5 years, recurrent, or identifiable mass
Free fluidSimple anechoic fluid does NOT preclude reduction; complex/echogenic free fluid → concern for perforation/ischemia
Trapped lymph nodesMesenteric nodes within intussusceptum; reactive post-viral lymphadenopathy is most common identifiable "lead point"
Contraindications to enema reduction: Pneumoperitoneum · Peritonitis · Hemodynamic instability. Success rate: ~80–90% pneumatic reduction. Recurrence: ~10%. Surgical consult for failed reduction, suspected lead point, or perforation.
📄Radiopaedia — Intussusception in children

Malrotation
Upper GI signs of malrotation: DJJ (duodenojejunal junction) NOT to the left of midline and NOT at level of duodenal bulb. Corkscrew duodenum = volvulus. Reverse SMA/SMV orientation on US (SMV left of SMA).

DiagnosisKey Features
HMD / RDSPremature; diffuse granular pattern; air bronchograms; low lung volumes
TTNTerm/near-term; perihilar streaking; fluid in fissures; resolves 24–48h
Meconium aspiration (MAS)Post-term; coarse asymmetric opacities; hyperinflation; pneumothorax risk
Neonatal pneumoniaIndistinguishable from HMD; consider in GBS exposure
BPDChronic (28+ days O2); hyperinflation; cystic changes; fibrosis
PIELinear/cystic lucencies; complication of HMD/ventilation

Non-Accidental Trauma (NAT)
High specificity injuries for NAT: Classic metaphyseal lesions (CML/bucket-handle fractures) · Posterior rib fractures · Subdural hematomas (especially interhemispheric, mixed-density) · Retinal hemorrhages · Bruising in non-mobile infant
Injury TypeSpecificity for NAT
Classic metaphyseal lesions (CML)High
Posterior rib fracturesHigh
Interhemispheric subduralHigh (abusive head trauma)
Scapular fracturesModerate-High
Spinous process fracturesModerate-High
Sternal fracturesModerate
Fractures of different agesHigh (context-dependent)
📋 Reporting Checklist — Non-Accidental Trauma
  • All fractures: list each by location, type, and estimated age (acute / healing / chronic)
  • High-specificity fractures: posterior rib fractures / classic metaphyseal lesions (CMLs) / interhemispheric subdural / scapular body
  • Moderate-high specificity: spinous process / sternal fractures
  • Dating discrepancy: multiple fractures of differing ages — yes / no
  • Intracranial findings: subdural (bilateral / posterior interhemispheric) / SAH / cortical contusions / DAI
  • Retinal hemorrhage: ophthalmology exam recommended — not assessable on CT
  • Visceral injuries without adequate trauma mechanism
  • Clinical history adequacy: mechanism adequate / inadequate / inconsistent with findings
  • Mandatory reporting obligation: note per institutional protocol

SCFE — Slipped Capital Femoral Epiphysis
Stable (can weight bear) vs Unstable (cannot — higher AVN risk). Klein's line should intersect >⅙ of femoral head on AP. Lateral view most sensitive.
Nuclear Medicine
V/Q · HIDA · GI Bleed · Brain Death · WBC · Renal · Bone · Meckel's
On This Page
V/Q Scan — PE HIDA Scan GI Bleed Scan Brain Death Study WBC & Gallium Renal Scan (MAG3) Bone Scan Meckel's Scan
V/Q Scan — PE Detection
Radiopharmaceuticals: Tc-99m MAA (perfusion, 2–4 mCi) + Tc-99m DTPA aerosol or Xe-133 gas (ventilation). SPECT V/Q preferred over planar — reduces indeterminate rate from ~30% to ~5% and improves both sensitivity and specificity. Preferred over CTPA in: renal insufficiency, contrast allergy, pregnancy (lower fetal dose), young women (lower breast dose).
CategoryScintigraphic Criteria (PIOPED II)PE ProbabilityNext Step
Normal No perfusion defects; perfusion outlines normal lung borders <1% PE excluded; no further workup
Very Low Non-segmental defects only (pleural effusion, cardiomegaly, enlarged hilum, aortic impression); ≤3 small (<25% segment) matched defects with normal CXR ~3% Clinical correlation; CTPA if high pre-test probability
Low Probability Small segmental perfusion defects (<25% of segment); any matched V/Q defects with/without CXR abnormality; non-segmental defects with corresponding CXR changes ~14% Low pre-test prob → PE unlikely; high pre-test prob → CTPA
Intermediate Single moderate (25–75% of segment) unmatched defect without CXR abnormality; neither clearly high nor low; difficult to categorize ~30% CTPA or bilateral lower extremity Doppler; do not treat on V/Q alone
High Probability ≥2 large (>75% of segment) unmatched segmental perfusion defects without corresponding ventilation or CXR abnormality; V/Q mismatch pattern >85% Treat for PE; confirmatory CTPA only if anticoagulation very high-risk
Combined pre-test probability + V/Q result (PIOPED II):
Pre-test Prob.High V/QIntermediate V/QLow / Very Low V/QNormal V/Q
High~96%~66% — image further~40% — CTPA required<5%
Intermediate~88%~30% — image further~16%<2%
Low~56% — CTPA required~16%~4%<1%
V/Q Pitfalls
COPD / bullous diseaseCreates matched defects → pushed to low probability; CTPA preferred in severe obstructive disease
PE with infarction (Hampton's hump)CXR consolidation matches perfusion defect → "triple match" → falsely low probability category; look for additional unmatched defects
Large pleural effusionCompresses lung → multiple non-segmental defects → very low probability; but large effusion can also obscure underlying segmental defects
Prior PEOld defects indistinguishable from acute; baseline V/Q or prior CTPA for comparison is critical
MAA particle clumpingCan create apparent focal perfusion defects; inject slowly with patient supine; use filtered preparation
Right-to-left shuntMAA particles reach systemic circulation → brain and kidney uptake; reduce dose in known shunt
📄PIOPED II Investigators. Value of V/Q Scan in Suspected PE. JAMA. 1990;263(20):2753–2759.
📄V/Q Scintigraphy — Radiopaedia
HIDA Scan (Hepatobiliary Scintigraphy)
Radiopharmaceutical: Tc-99m mebrofenin (Choletec) or Tc-99m disofenin, 5–8 mCi. IDA compounds excreted via biliary system. Patient NPO 2–4h (but not >24h — prolonged fasting or TPN → falsely absent GB visualization; pretreat with sincalide 0.02 µg/kg IV × 30 min). Morphine augmentation: 0.04 mg/kg IV at 60 min if GB not seen — contracts sphincter of Oddi, redirecting bile flow into GB.
FindingInterpretationSens / Spec
GB non-visualization at 4h (after morphine augmentation at 60 min) Acute cholecystitis — cystic duct obstruction Sens ~96%, Spec ~90%
Rim sign — pericholecystic band of increased hepatic parenchymal activity adjacent to GB fossa Severe / gangrenous cholecystitis — surgical emergency; indicates transmural inflammation or perforation Spec ~99%, Sens ~35%
GB visualization delayed (1–4h) but eventually fills Chronic cholecystitis — partial/intermittent cystic duct obstruction —
GB EF <35–40% after CCK (sincalide 0.02 µg/kg infused over 30–60 min) Biliary dyskinesia — functional cystic duct dysfunction; surgical referral (cholecystectomy) Sens ~78%, Spec ~87%
Focal accumulation of activity outside hepatobiliary system, increasing over time Bile leak — post-cholecystectomy, liver transplant anastomotic leak, trauma; activity pools dependently in abdomen/pelvis Sens ~94%
No CBD / bowel activity; intrahepatic duct dilation; activity retained in liver Complete CBD obstruction — choledocholithiasis, stricture, malignancy —
HIDA Pitfalls
Prolonged fasting / TPN (>24h NPO)Concentrated bile fills GB → GB does not fill with new radiotracer → false positive for cholecystitis; pretreat with sincalide
Hepatic dysfunction (bili >5–7 mg/dL)Delayed or absent biliary excretion; decreases sensitivity; consider US as primary modality
Duodenal bulb activity mistaken for GBDuodenum adjacent to GB fossa; follow activity dynamically — bowel activity moves, GB stays; use LAO view
Accessory bile duct / choledochal cystCan mimic bile leak or abnormal focal uptake; MRCP for anatomic clarification
Acalculous cholecystitisSame scintigraphic appearance as calculous cholecystitis; occurs in critically ill/ICU patients; sensitivity slightly lower (~80%)
📄Tulchinsky M et al. Nuclear Medicine in the Acute Clinical Setting. RadioGraphics. 2013;33(2):375–396.
📄HIDA Scan — Radiopaedia
GI Bleed Scan (Tc-99m RBC)
Radiopharmaceutical: Tc-99m labeled autologous RBCs, 20–25 mCi. In vitro labeling (UltraTag) preferred — highest labeling efficiency (~98%). Detects active bleeding at rates as low as 0.1–0.5 mL/min (vs. angiography threshold ~0.5 mL/min, CT angiography ~1 mL/min). Primary use: lower GI bleeding localization before IR angiography or surgical planning. Tagged RBCs circulate for 24h — delayed imaging possible for intermittent bleeds.
FindingInterpretation
Focal activity appearing at or before 5 min, distinct from blood-pool structures, increasing over time and moving in direction of peristalsis Active GI bleeding — localize to time of first appearance, not subsequent frames
Activity confined to liver, spleen, heart, great vessels, kidneys at all time points No active bleeding at time of imaging; if clinical suspicion remains, delayed images at 2h, 4h, 24h may detect intermittent bleed
Activity in renal collecting system and bladder on early images Free Tc-99m pertechnetate (poor in vivo labeling) — non-diagnostic; repeat with in vitro UltraTag kit
Persistent focal activity that does NOT move or increase over time Normal variant: penile blood pool, accessory spleen, hemangioma, uterus — not GI bleed; check multiple views
⚠ Critical rule — localize to earliest appearance: Tagged blood travels rapidly antegrade and retrograde through bowel loops. Activity first seen in the cecum at 5 min may appear to be in the sigmoid or transverse colon on later frames. Always record and use the time of first appearance for anatomic localization — not later redistribution images.
GI Bleed Scan Pitfalls
Intermittent / slow bleedingScan may be negative if bleeding has stopped; tagged RBCs have 24h half-life — delayed imaging at 2h, 4h, 24h can detect intermittent bleeds; serial imaging preferred
Poor labeling (in vivo method)Free Tc-99m → gastric, thyroid, renal activity → mimics bleed; always use in vitro UltraTag for best results
Penile / midline pelvic blood poolIn men: midline pelvic RBC pool can mimic rectal bleed; obtain lateral views; void and re-image
Upper GI bleedRapid transit can mimic lower GI; upper GI bleed activity will be seen in stomach first, then moves distally within minutes
Liver / spleen overlapping bowelHepatic flexure and splenic flexure bleeds may be obscured; SPECT/CT adds anatomic localization
📄Tulchinsky M et al. Nuclear Medicine in the Acute Clinical Setting. RadioGraphics. 2013;33(2):375–396.
📄GI Bleeding Scintigraphy — Radiopaedia
Brain Death Study
Radiopharmaceutical: Tc-99m HMPAO (Ceretec) or Tc-99m ECD (Neurolite), 20–25 mCi IV. Both cross intact blood-brain barrier and are retained in viable neurons proportional to cerebral perfusion. Confirmatory/ancillary test only — does NOT replace clinical brain death exam + apnea test. Required when clinical exam is confounded (barbiturate coma, hypothermia, metabolic encephalopathy, facial/spinal trauma).
FindingInterpretation
"Hollow skull" sign — absent cerebral parenchymal activity on planar; no cortical, cerebellar, or brainstem uptake; facial/scalp activity preserved from external carotid Brain death confirmed — no cerebral perfusion
Hot nose sign — prominent nasal soft tissue activity Supportive of brain death; ECA territory continues perfusing nasal mucosa when ICA flow ceases; high specificity when combined with absent cerebral uptake
No sagittal sinus activity on flow images Absent venous drainage consistent with brain death; helps confirm absent parenchymal perfusion
Normal symmetric cortical + cerebellar + deep grey uptake ("bat wings" on anterior view) Viable cerebral perfusion — brain death NOT confirmed
Protocol: Flow study (2 sec/frame × 60 frames) immediately after injection, then static images at 30–60 min. SPECT optional but improves detection of residual perfusion. Confounders that can suppress perfusion (must exclude clinically): Barbiturate/sedative coma · Severe hypothermia (<32°C) · Toxic-metabolic encephalopathy · Profound hypotension. Spinal cord and peripheral reflexes may persist with brain death and do not invalidate the study.
📄Brain Death Scintigraphy — Radiopaedia
WBC & Gallium Scans

Labeled WBC Scan (In-111 Oxine or Tc-99m HMPAO)

ParameterIn-111 Oxine WBCTc-99m HMPAO WBC
Dose0.5 mCi10–20 mCi
Imaging time24h and 48h4h and 24h
Normal biodistributionLiver, spleen, bone marrow — no bowel activity on early imagesLiver, spleen, bone marrow; kidneys/bladder (free Tc-99m)
Bowel activity significanceAlways abnormal on early (24h) images → infection or IBDNormal after ~24h due to free pertechnetate transit
Best clinical useOsteomyelitis, prosthetic joint, abdomen/pelvis infection, IBDFaster turnaround; cardiac device infection, fever of unknown origin
IndicationKey FindingsPearls
OsteomyelitisFocal WBC uptake > adjacent bone marrow baseline; compare with Tc-99m sulfur colloid marrow scan — discordant = osteomyelitisConcordant uptake on both WBC and marrow scan = normal marrow redistribution (no infection); MRI often preferred for vertebral osteomyelitis
Prosthetic joint infectionIncreased WBC uptake at prosthesis-bone interface beyond 12 months post-op; normal periprosthetic activity in first year (remodeling)Combined WBC + marrow scan has ~95% accuracy; concordant = no infection; discordant = infected
IBD (Crohn's / UC)Bowel activity on In-111 WBC early images (24h); Crohn's: patchy/skip, involves small bowel; UC: continuous, colon onlyGraded activity correlates with inflammation severity; useful for extent mapping without colonoscopy
Fever of unknown originAny focal uptake outside normal biodistribution; liver/spleen enlargement with increased activityGallium-67 preferred for chronic FUO and granulomatous disease; WBC scan better for acute bacterial infection

Gallium-67 Scan

PatternInterpretation
Normal distribution: liver > spleen, bone, lacrimal/salivary glands, bowel (variable at 48–72h)No acute infection/inflammation
Pulmonary uptake ≥ liver intensityActive pulmonary inflammation — PCP (AIDS), sarcoidosis, drug toxicity, ARDS
Lambda sign — bilateral paratracheal + bilateral hilar uptakeSarcoidosis (sensitivity ~90%)
Panda sign — bilateral lacrimal + bilateral parotid uptakeSarcoidosis (highly specific in combination with lambda sign)
Diffuse hepatic uptake > spleen (reversed liver-spleen ratio)Active hepatitis; hepatic lymphoma
Intense focal uptake in bone or soft tissueOsteomyelitis or soft tissue infection; combined with WBC scan for highest accuracy

Renal Scan (MAG3 / Lasix Renogram)
Radiopharmaceuticals: Tc-99m MAG3 (mercaptoacetylglycine, tubular secretion, 5–10 mCi) — preferred; works well at reduced GFR. Tc-99m DTPA (glomerular filtration) — used for GFR calculation; requires adequate function. Standard protocol: 3-phase (flow 1–2 min, function 20 min, excretion) + Lasix 0.3–1 mg/kg IV. F+20 protocol: Lasix at 20 min post-injection; F−15: Lasix 15 min before injection (preferred when dilated system suspected).
T½ After LasixInterpretationAction
<10 min No obstruction — normal washout No further workup needed
10–20 min Equivocal / indeterminate Ensure adequate hydration; consider F−15 protocol or diuretic renogram under controlled hydration; clinical correlation
>20 min Obstructed Urology referral; correlate with symptoms and ultrasound
Clinical ScenarioKey Scintigraphic Findings
UPJ obstructionActivity accumulates in dilated renal pelvis; little/no ureteral activity; delayed cortical transit time; T½ >20 min after Lasix
Renovascular hypertension (captopril renogram)Baseline MAG3 + repeat 1h after captopril 25–50 mg PO; Positive: asymmetric ↓ in function (>10%) or prolonged time-to-peak on captopril scan → significant renal artery stenosis
Renal transplant — ATNGood perfusion (flow phase normal) + poor tubular function (absent/markedly delayed excretion); improves over days
Renal transplant — RejectionDecreased perfusion AND decreased function; may have photopenic halo; worse prognosis than ATN
Urine leak (post-transplant)Focal accumulation of activity outside collecting system; pools dependently in pelvis; photopenic area within leak on delayed images
Split renal functionNormal ~45–55% each kidney; <40/60 split = significant asymmetry; <35% = poor function → urology evaluation
MAG3 Pitfalls
DehydrationReduces Lasix response → falsely prolonged T½; hydrate 10 mL/kg IV NS before and during scan
Markedly dilated pelvis (reservoir effect)Large extrarenal pelvis retains tracer even without true obstruction; T½ may be prolonged; correlate with degree of hydronephrosis on US
Severely impaired GFR (<15 mL/min)Inadequate Lasix response; equivocal results common; MR urography may provide better anatomic and functional data
📄Tulchinsky M et al. Nuclear Medicine in the Acute Clinical Setting. RadioGraphics. 2013;33(2):375–396.
📄MAG3 Renogram — Radiopaedia
Bone Scan (Tc-99m MDP)
Radiopharmaceutical: Tc-99m MDP (methylene diphosphonate), 20–25 mCi. Accumulates at sites of osteoblastic activity proportional to bone turnover and blood flow. 3-phase protocol: Flow (dynamic 2–3 sec/frame × 60 frames) → Blood pool (5–10 min post-injection) → Delayed whole body (3–4h). SPECT or SPECT/CT added for anatomic localization. Normal: symmetric uptake, kidneys and bladder visible, mild growth plate activity in children.
PatternDifferential / InterpretationKey Pearls
Focal hot spot (delayed)Metastasis, fracture, osteomyelitis, Paget's disease, arthritis — non-specificMost common pattern; CT/MRI correlation required for specificity
3-phase positive (↑ flow + ↑ blood pool + ↑ delayed at same focal site)Osteomyelitis — all 3 phases elevated at site of infectionSens ~90%, Spec ~73%; vs cellulitis: flow + blood pool diffusely elevated but delayed normal or mildly ↑
Flow + blood pool ↑, delayed normal or mildly ↑Cellulitis (no osseous involvement)Key distinction from osteomyelitis — if delayed becomes focally hot, consider underlying osteomyelitis
Cold / photopenic defectEarly AVN (before reactive bone formation), aggressive lytic tumor, multiple myeloma, radiation fieldEarly AVN: cold on bone scan, MRI gold standard; myeloma: cold or normal bone scan (unlike most mets)
Superscan — diffusely increased skeletal uptake; absent or faint renal/soft tissue activityDiffuse bone metastases (prostate, breast), metabolic bone disease (renal osteodystrophy, hyperparathyroidism)Can appear deceptively "normal" — absent kidney activity is the key; request clinical correlation with PSA/tumor markers
Flare phenomenonTransient worsening of bone scan activity 2–6 months after starting effective chemotherapy — represents healing (osteoblastic response), NOT progressionCorrelate with tumor markers and CT; serial scans typically show improvement after 6 months
📄Bone Scintigraphy — Radiopaedia
Meckel's Scan (Tc-99m Pertechnetate)
Indication: Painless rectal bleeding in children (most common cause: Meckel's diverticulum with ectopic gastric mucosa). Mechanism: Tc-99m pertechnetate (5 mCi adult; weight-based pediatric), 5–10 mCi — concentrates in gastric mucosa parietal cells (same as thyroid, salivary glands). Only detects Meckel's containing ectopic gastric mucosa — present in ~25–50% of all Meckel's but ~80–90% of symptomatic/bleeding Meckel's.
FindingInterpretation
Focal ectopic activity appearing simultaneously with gastric mucosa, in RLQ or ectopic location, increasing in intensity over time Meckel's diverticulum with ectopic gastric mucosa — positive scan. Sensitivity ~85–90% in children.
Activity appearing after gastric uptake, or activity that does not increase in intensity Normal variant (blood pool, ureteral activity) or negative scan — Meckel's not excluded if no gastric mucosa present
Pretreatment to improve sensitivity:
  • Cimetidine 300 mg PO q6h × 2 days before scan — blocks Tc-99m washout from gastric mucosa (most commonly used; increases sensitivity to ~95%)
  • Pentagastrin 6 µg/kg SQ × 15 min before — stimulates gastric mucosa uptake; avoid in active GI bleed
  • Glucagon 50 µg/kg IV — reduces bowel motility, decreases redistribution of activity
Meckel's Scan Pitfalls
Ureteral activityRight ureter can mimic RLQ ectopic uptake; always void before scanning; lateral views; activity should clear with voiding
IntussusceptionCan trap pertechnetate → false positive; correlate with US findings
No ectopic gastric mucosa~50% of asymptomatic Meckel's lack gastric mucosa — negative scan does not exclude Meckel's; surgical exploration may still be warranted with high clinical suspicion
Inflammatory bowel disease / duplication cystBoth can contain ectopic gastric mucosa → false positive; clinical and US correlation required
📄Meckel's Diverticulum Scan — Radiopaedia
📄Tulchinsky M et al. Nuclear Medicine in the Acute Clinical Setting. RadioGraphics. 2013;33(2):375–396.

Anticoagulation Periprocedural Guidelines
See your institution's specific protocols. The below is a general framework based on SIR guidelines.
Risk CategoryExamplesAnticoagulation
Low bleeding riskDialysis access, venography, IVC filter, PICC, central lineNo need to hold anticoagulation typically
Moderate bleeding riskCore biopsy (superficial), thoracentesis, paracentesis, drain placementIndividualize; check INR/platelet; hold anticoagulants per SIR
High bleeding riskOrgan biopsy (renal, liver), RF ablation, biliary drainageHold anticoagulation; INR <1.5; platelets >50K
SIR Thresholds (general guidance): INR <1.5 · Platelets >50,000 (moderate) / >100,000 (high risk) · Heparin: hold 4–6h; LMWH: hold 24h; Warfarin: hold 5 days; DOACs: hold 24–48h (renal-dependent for dabigatran)

Contrast & Premedication
Reactions · Renal function · Gadolinium · Premedication protocols
On This Page
Contrast reactions Premedication Renal function & iodinated Gadolinium & NSF Extravasation
Contrast Reaction Treatment
Key principle: Allergic-like (anaphylactoid) reactions are not dose-dependent and cannot be predicted by test doses. Physiologic reactions (nausea, warmth, flushing) are dose-related and self-limited. Most reactions occur within 20 minutes of injection — observe all patients for this period.
SeveritySigns / SymptomsTreatment
Mild Limited urticaria, pruritus, mild nausea/vomiting, flushing, headache, dizziness, single episode of emesis Observe and reassure · Diphenhydramine 25–50 mg PO/IM/IV (optional, for comfort)
Moderate Diffuse urticaria, facial or laryngeal edema (mild), bronchospasm (mild–moderate), tachycardia or bradycardia without hypotension Diphenhydramine 25–50 mg IV · O₂ 6–10 L/min · Bronchospasm: albuterol MDI 2–3 puffs · IV access; continuous monitoring · Consider epinephrine if progressing
Severe — Anaphylaxis Severe bronchospasm, laryngeal edema/stridor, profound hypotension, loss of consciousness, cardiac arrest Epinephrine 0.3–0.5 mg IM (1:1,000) to lateral thigh · Call code / RRT · O₂ · Large-bore IV + NS bolus 1–2 L · Repeat epi q5–15 min PRN · Diphenhydramine 50 mg IV · Corticosteroids (delayed benefit only)
Vasovagal Bradycardia + hypotension + pallor/diaphoresis — without urticaria (key distinction from anaphylaxis) Trendelenburg position · NS bolus · Atropine 0.6–1.0 mg IV if bradycardia persists; may repeat (max 3 mg) · O₂
Epinephrine dosing:
  • Adults: 0.3–0.5 mg IM (1:1,000 = 1 mg/mL) to outer thigh; may repeat q5–15 min
  • Pediatric: 0.01 mg/kg IM (max 0.5 mg) · ~0.15 mg if <30 kg · ~0.3 mg if ≥30 kg
  • EpiPen: 0.3 mg adult / 0.15 mg pediatric auto-injector
  • Cardiac arrest: Epinephrine 1 mg IV (1:10,000) per ACLS protocol
  • β-blocker patients with refractory reaction: Glucagon 1–5 mg IV, then 5–15 mcg/min infusion
Targeted Treatment by Symptom
Isolated bronchospasmAlbuterol MDI 2–4 puffs; if severe or not responding → epinephrine 0.3 mg IM
Isolated hypotension (no bradycardia)Epinephrine 0.3–0.5 mg IM; NS 1–2 L IV bolus; Trendelenburg
Diffuse urticariaDiphenhydramine 25–50 mg IV/IM; epinephrine IM if rapidly progressing
Laryngeal edema / stridorEpinephrine 0.3–0.5 mg IM immediately; O₂; prepare for intubation
SeizureProtect airway; benzodiazepine (diazepam 5–10 mg IV or lorazepam 2–4 mg IV)
Pulmonary edemaO₂; furosemide 40 mg IV; morphine 2–4 mg IV; upright position; consider CPAP
📄ACR Manual on Contrast Media (2023 edition)
Premedication Guidelines (ACR)
Indication: Prior allergic-like or unknown-type reaction to the same class of contrast agent (iodinated or GBCA) — any severity. Premedication reduces but does not eliminate risk; breakthrough reactions occur in ~10–17% of premedicated patients.
RegimenProtocolWhen to Use
Standard
(13–7–1 h)		 Prednisone 50 mg PO at 13 h, 7 h, and 1 h before contrast
+ Diphenhydramine 50 mg IV/IM/PO 1 h before		 Elective studies with ≥13 h preparation time; preferred regimen
Methylprednisolone
(12–2 h)		 Methylprednisolone 32 mg PO at 12 h and 2 h before contrast
± Diphenhydramine 50 mg PO/IV 1 h before (optional)		 Equally effective alternative; useful when prednisone unavailable or for cost
Emergency
(<5 h available)		 Methylprednisolone 40 mg IV (or hydrocortisone 200 mg IV) q4h until procedure
+ Diphenhydramine 50 mg IV 1 h before		 Urgent/emergent contrast needed; less effective; document risk/benefit discussion
Who needs premedication?
ScenarioPremedication?
Prior allergic-like reaction to same contrast class✅ Yes — standard or emergency regimen
Prior physiologic reaction only (nausea, warmth, flushing)❌ No — not indicated
Shellfish or iodine allergy❌ No — not higher risk than general population (common misconception)
Asthma / atopy (no prior contrast reaction)⚠️ Consider — elevated baseline risk; individualize
Prior reaction to a different contrast class❌ No — class-specific risk; iodinated ≠ GBCA
β-blocker use⚠️ Note — may blunt epinephrine; have glucagon available if reaction occurs
⚠ Breakthrough reactions still occur despite premedication (~10–17%). Breakthrough reactions tend to mirror the index reaction in severity. Always have epinephrine and emergency cart immediately available regardless of premedication status.
📄ACR Manual on Contrast Media (2023)
Iodinated Contrast & Renal Function
PC-AKI replaces "CIN": Per ACR/NKF 2020 consensus, "contrast-induced nephropathy" is replaced by post-contrast AKI (PC-AKI) — defined as ≥0.3 mg/dL or ≥1.5× baseline creatinine rise within 48–72 h. Prior studies overestimated risk by failing to control for AKI from the underlying illness. Absolute risk is <1% for eGFR ≥30 with modern low-osmolality agents.
eGFR (mL/min/1.73m²)IV Iodinated ContrastPrehydration
≥45✅ May give; no special precautions neededNot required
30–44May give; consider risk/benefit for elective studiesConsider if additional risk factors (CHF, DM, NSAID use, volume depletion)
15–29Use with caution; weigh alternatives; document discussionRecommended: NS 1 mL/kg/h × 3–6 h before and 6 h after (or NaHCO₃ equivalent)
<15 or dialysisMay give — contrast is NOT contraindicated even in dialysis patientsNot needed in ESRD on regular dialysis; timing of next dialysis session does not need to change urgently
AKI / unstable renal functionUse with caution; treat as eGFR <30; defer elective contrast if possibleConsider prehydration; reassess after stabilization
Metformin management (ACR 2023)
eGFRAction
≥45Continue metformin; no interruption needed for contrast
30–44Hold metformin at the time of or prior to iodinated contrast; restart 48 h later if renal function stable
<30Metformin already contraindicated per prescribing guidelines; will not be taking it
Emergent contrast, unknown eGFRAdminister contrast; hold metformin after procedure; check Cr at 48 h before restarting
Mechanism: Contrast → PC-AKI → ↓ metformin clearance → metformin accumulation → lactic acidosis. Risk is rare but serious.
Factors That Increase PC-AKI Risk
Strongest risk factorsPre-existing CKD (eGFR <30), diabetes with CKD, volume depletion, concomitant nephrotoxins
Contrast-related factorsHigh-osmolality agents (HOCM) > low-osmolality (LOCM); larger contrast volume; multiple doses in 24–48 h
Weak/debated risk factorsCHF, hypertension, age >75, single kidney — only meaningful in context of reduced eGFR
📄Davenport MS et al. Use of IV Iodinated Contrast in Patients with Kidney Disease: ACR/NKF Consensus Statements. Radiology. 2020;294(3):660–668.
Gadolinium-Based Contrast & NSF
Nephrogenic Systemic Fibrosis (NSF): Rare fibrosing disorder (skin, joints, organs) associated with GBCA exposure in severe renal impairment. Risk is essentially zero with macrocyclic agents. No confirmed new NSF cases reported with macrocyclic GBCAs. All NSF cases were associated with Group I (linear) agents — now largely withdrawn from US market.
GBCA ClassAgents (generic / trade name)NSF Risk
Group I — Linear
Highest risk — largely withdrawn or avoided		 Gadodiamide (Omniscan) · Gadopentetate dimeglumine (Magnevist) · Gadoversetamide (OptiMARK) High — avoid if eGFR <30 or AKI
Group II — Linear, protein-binding Gadobenate dimeglumine (MultiHance) · Gadoxetate disodium (Eovist / Primovist) Intermediate — caution if eGFR <30
Group III — Macrocyclic
Preferred agents for all patients		 Gadobutrol (Gadavist) · Gadoterate meglumine (Dotarem) · Gadoteridol (ProHance) Lowest — no confirmed NSF cases; preferred
eGFRMacrocyclic (Group III) — PreferredLinear Agents (Groups I–II)
≥30✅ May give at standard dose; no special precautionsGroup I: generally avoid (alternatives available) · Group II: may give
15–29May give; use lowest effective dose; document risk/benefitGroup I: avoid · Group II: use with caution, lowest effective dose
<15 (non-dialysis)Use only if clinically essential; lowest effective doseGroup I: contraindicated · Group II: avoid
Dialysis (ESRD)May use with caution; prompt post-injection dialysis not required but may reduce Gd exposure if convenientGroup I: contraindicated
AKITreat as eGFR <30 until resolved; defer elective contrast if possibleGroup I & II: avoid
Gadolinium retention (all agents): Small quantities of Gd deposit in brain (dentate nucleus, globus pallidus), bone, and skin with repeated doses. Macrocyclic agents show significantly less deposition than linear agents. No established adverse clinical effects in patients with normal renal function. No dose-limiting recommendations from ACR for standard renal function. Relevance in patients receiving many lifetime doses (e.g., MS, oncology) is under study.
📄Weinreb JC et al. Use of IV GBCA in Patients with Kidney Disease: ACR/NKF Consensus Statements. Radiology. 2021;298(1):28–35.
Contrast Extravasation
Most contrast extravasations are minor and resolve without intervention. Iodinated contrast and GBCAs are both low-osmolality and generally well-tolerated in soft tissue. The key concern is compartment syndrome — rare but limb-threatening.
Volume ExtravasatedInitial ManagementDisposition
Minor (<10 mL) Elevate extremity · Cold compress 15–20 min q1–4h × 24 h · Reassure patient Observe 30 min; discharge with return precautions if no progression
Moderate (10–30 mL) Elevate extremity · Cold compress · Monitor for compartment syndrome signs × 2–4 h Return precautions; follow-up call at 24 h; low threshold for ED evaluation
Large (>30 mL) or High-Risk Site Elevate extremity · Cold compress · Urgent surgery/plastics consultation Admit or ED for observation; possible surgical irrigation or fasciotomy
⚠ Signs of compartment syndrome — requires urgent surgical consultation:
  • Increasing pain or tightness disproportionate to the injection site
  • Skin blistering, bullae, or visible skin breakdown
  • Paresthesia, numbness, or decreased sensation distal to site
  • Decreased capillary refill, pallor, or pulselessness distally
High-risk extravasation characteristics: distal site (hand, foot, antecubital fossa) · volume >50 mL · HOCM agents · impaired arterial circulation / peripheral vascular disease · immunocompromised or lymphedematous limb
Agent TypeTissue Injury Profile
Low-osmolality iodinated (LOCM)Mild — osmolality near physiologic; generally well-tolerated in soft tissue
High-osmolality iodinated (HOCM)Higher injury risk — hyperosmolar; causes more osmotic tissue damage; rarely used today
Gadolinium (GBCA)Mild — low-osmolality, similar to LOCM; same management protocol
📄ACR Manual on Contrast Media (2023) — Chapter on Adverse Events
Incidentaloma Guidelines
ACR Incidental Findings Committee · Society consensus recommendations
On This Page
Adnexal Mass Adrenal Mass Chest & Mediastinum Gallbladder & Biliary Liver Lesions Pancreatic Cysts Pituitary Renal Mass Splenic Lesions & Nodes Thyroid Nodules Abdominal Vasculature
General principle: These guidelines apply to incidentally detected findings in patients without known malignancy, immunosuppression, or signs/symptoms referable to the finding. When the patient has a known malignancy or is immunocompromised, a lower threshold for workup is appropriate. Always correlate with clinical context.
Adnexal Mass
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Patel MD et al. JACR 2020. Applies to premenopausal and postmenopausal women with incidentally detected adnexal lesions on CT or MRI. Transvaginal ultrasound (TVUS) is the modality of choice for characterization when follow-up is recommended.

Postmenopausal Women

Key principle: Postmenopausal ovaries should be atrophic. Any new lesion warrants lower-threshold evaluation. Simple cysts ≤1 cm are near-universal incidentals and require no follow-up. Size thresholds for action are substantially lower than for premenopausal women.
Lesion TypeSize / FeaturesRecommendation
Simple cyst≤1 cmNo follow-up (near-universal finding; essentially benign)
Simple cyst1.1–3 cmTVUS in 1 year; if stable/resolved → discontinue
Simple cyst3.1–5 cmTVUS or MRI in 6–12 months; if simple and stable → 1 additional year then d/c
Simple cyst>5 cmMRI; surgical evaluation (lower size threshold than premenopausal)
Hemorrhagic cystAny sizeTVUS characterization; if persistent at 6–12 weeks → MRI and gynecology referral
Endometrioma (new finding)Any sizeMRI and gynecology/oncology referral — new endometrioma in a postmenopausal woman is an abnormal finding requiring evaluation for malignant transformation
Dermoid≤3 cm, classicTVUS follow-up; lower threshold for surgical evaluation vs. premenopausal
Dermoid>3 cm or atypicalSurgical evaluation
Complex / solid componentAny sizeMRI with and without contrast; Gyn-Onc referral regardless of size
CA-125—Not required for asymptomatic simple cysts; may be obtained at clinical discretion for any complex or solid lesion
📄Patel MD et al. ACR Incidental Adnexal Findings on CT and MRI. JACR. 2020;17(2):248–254.
Adrenal Mass
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Mayo-Smith WW et al. JACR 2017. Key principle: most incidental adrenal masses are benign adenomas. The primary goal is to distinguish adenoma from malignancy (primary adrenocortical carcinoma or metastasis) and from functioning lesions (pheochromocytoma, Conn's, Cushing's).

Unenhanced CT AttenuationInterpretationRecommendation
≤10 HULipid-rich adenoma (high specificity ~98%)No further imaging workup needed; biochemical evaluation only if clinically indicated
11–30 HUIndeterminate — may be lipid-poor adenomaAdrenal protocol CT washout OR chemical shift MRI

Adrenal Washout CT CriteriaThresholdInterpretation
Absolute percentage washout (APW)≥60%Adenoma (sensitivity ~86–88%, specificity ~96%)
Relative percentage washout (RPW)≥40%Adenoma (use when no unenhanced phase available)
Size / Clinical ContextRecommendation
<4 cm, indeterminate attenuation (no prior malignancy)Adrenal washout CT or chemical shift MRI; if benign characteristics → no further imaging
≥4 cm or growing (>1 cm/yr or >20% increase)Surgical referral regardless of imaging characteristics
Any size, known extra-adrenal malignancyPET or biopsy (after ruling out pheochromocytoma biochemically)
Suspected pheochromocytoma (heterogeneous, >3 cm, hemorrhage, cystic)Biochemical evaluation before biopsy or surgery; MIBG or DOPA-PET if needed
Myelolipoma (macroscopic fat, −30 to −100 HU)No follow-up if <4 cm and typical; surgical referral if symptomatic or large
Simple adrenal cystNo follow-up if <4 cm, no internal structure
Adrenal washout calculator: APW = (enh HU − 15min HU) ÷ (enh HU − precontrast HU) × 100. RPW = (enh HU − 15min HU) ÷ enh HU × 100. Use MDCalc adrenal washout calculator.
📄Mayo-Smith WW et al. ACR Incidental Adrenal Mass. JACR. 2017;14(8):1038-1044.
Chest & Mediastinum Incidentals
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Munden RF et al. JACR 2018 & 2021. Covers incidentally detected mediastinal masses, lymph nodes, pericardial findings, and lung parenchymal findings (separate from Fleischner Society pulmonary nodule guidelines, which apply to nodules ≥35 years).

Mediastinal Masses

FindingRecommendation
Anterior mediastinal mass <3 cm, smooth, homogeneous (likely thymic cyst or small thymoma)CT chest with contrast in 3 months, then annually × 2 if stable; surgical referral if >3 cm or heterogeneous
Anterior mediastinal mass ≥3 cm or heterogeneous / irregularCT with contrast; surgical or oncology referral
Middle mediastinal mass (lymph node enlargement, bronchogenic cyst)CT with contrast for characterization; if isolated bronchogenic cyst <3 cm and typical → no f/u; if atypical or >3 cm → surgical referral
Posterior mediastinal mass (neurogenic, vertebral origin)MRI spine/chest for characterization; surgical referral for most neurogenic tumors
Thymic cyst (<3 cm, homogeneous, non-enhancing)No follow-up if <3 cm and clearly cystic

Mediastinal Lymph Nodes

Short Axis SizeRecommendation
<10 mm (non-enlarged)No follow-up
10–19 mm, no known malignancyCT in 3 months
≥20 mm OR enlarging OR necrotic/calcifiedPET-CT or tissue sampling; oncology/pulmonology referral
Any size, known malignancyPET-CT or biopsy as clinically indicated

Pericardial Findings

FindingRecommendation
Pericardial cyst (smooth, water-density, no enhancement, ≤3 cm)No follow-up
Pericardial cyst >3 cm or symptomaticEchocardiography; cardiology referral
Pericardial effusion (small, asymptomatic)Echocardiography for characterization and hemodynamic assessment
Pericardial thickening or massCardiac MRI; cardiology referral

Incidental Lung Parenchymal Findings

FindingRecommendation
Pulmonary nodule (solid, subsolid, part-solid)Follow Fleischner Society 2017 guidelines (see Chest section). For patients <35 years or with known malignancy, use clinical judgment.
Pulmonary cyst (<2 cm, thin wall, no nodule)No follow-up if <2 cm and no high-risk features (smoking, family history of LAM/BHD); if >2 cm or multiple → CT in 12 months
Ground glass opacity (pure GGO, <6 mm, solitary)No follow-up. If ≥6 mm → CT at 2 and 4 years (see Fleischner)
Reticular / interstitial opacities (UIP, NSIP pattern)Pulmonology referral for evaluation of ILD; HRCT if not already performed
Incidental bronchiectasis (asymptomatic)Pulmonology referral; evaluate for underlying cause (NTM, CF, immunodeficiency)
📄Munden RF et al. ACR Incidental Chest Findings. JACR. 2018;15(8):1087-1096.
📄Munden RF et al. ACR Incidental Lung Findings Update. JACR. 2021.
Gallbladder & Biliary
Sources: ACR Incidental Findings Committee (Sebastian S et al. JACR 2013) for gallbladder findings; SRU 2022 consensus supersedes ACR 2013 for gallbladder polyps specifically.

Gallbladder Wall / Lumen Findings

FindingRecommendation
GB wall thickening (>3 mm), no symptomsUltrasound for characterization; clinical correlation for cholecystitis, hepatitis, CHF, portal hypertension
Gallstones (incidental, asymptomatic)No follow-up; counsel patient; cholecystectomy only if symptomatic
Porcelain gallbladder (calcified wall)Cholecystectomy referral — historically associated with malignancy; risk now debated but surgical referral standard
GB adenomyomatosis (focal/diffuse)Ultrasound to confirm; no follow-up if classic appearance; surgery if symptomatic

Gallbladder Polyps — SRU 2022 Guidelines

Polyp SizeNo Risk FactorsRisk Factors Present*

≥10 mmCholecystectomy referralCholecystectomy referral
Any size, sessile, growing >2 mmCholecystectomy referralCholecystectomy referral
*Risk factors for GB polyp malignancy: age >60, PSC (primary sclerosing cholangitis), familial adenomatous polyposis, symptomatic, sessile morphology, single polyp.

Liver Lesions
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Gore RM et al. JACR 2017. Applies to patients with no known hepatic disease, no cirrhosis, and no known malignancy. Cirrhotic patients: use LI-RADS. Patients with known malignancy: lower threshold for workup.
FindingLow-Risk Patient (no malignancy, no cirrhosis)High-Risk Patient (known malignancy or cirrhosis)
Simple hepatic cyst (imperceptible wall, water density, no enhancement)≤2 cm: no f/u · >2 cm: confirm with US; no f/u if typicalMRI for characterization if atypical
Hemangioma (confirmed typical appearance)No follow-up regardless of sizeMRI to confirm; if confirmed → no f/u
FNH (focal nodular hyperplasia, confirmed)No follow-upMRI with hepatobiliary agent to confirm
Indeterminate lesion ≤1.5 cmMRI with contrast in 6 months; if stable → no f/uMultiphasic CT or MRI; hepatology referral
Indeterminate lesion 1.5–3 cmMRI with contrast (hepatobiliary agent preferred)MRI or multiphasic CT; hepatology/oncology referral
Indeterminate lesion >3 cmMRI with contrast; hepatobiliary surgery referralMRI + hepatology/oncology; biopsy if needed
Hypervascular lesion (early enhancement, washout)MRI with hepatobiliary agent; HCC evaluation if any cirrhosis riskLI-RADS system; hepatology referral
Calcified lesion (granuloma, calcified cyst)No follow-up if clearly calcified and stableClinical correlation; usually benign
Cirrhotic liver: Use LI-RADS (Liver Imaging Reporting and Data System) for all observations in patients with cirrhosis or at risk for HCC. LI-RADS 1–2: definitely/probably benign. LI-RADS 3: intermediate. LI-RADS 4–5: probably/definitely HCC → hepatology/transplant referral.
📄Gore RM et al. ACR Incidental Liver Lesions. JACR. 2017;14(11):1429-1437.
Pancreatic Cysts
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Megibow AJ et al. JACR 2017. Most incidental pancreatic cysts are benign (serous cystadenoma, pseudocyst) or low-grade malignant potential (IPMN). Key distinction: main duct vs. branch duct involvement. High-risk stigmata warrant GI/surgical referral.
High-risk stigmata (always refer to GI/surgery): Main pancreatic duct ≥10 mm · Obstructive jaundice with pancreatic head lesion · Mural nodule with enhancement · Suspicious solid component · Positive cytology · Abrupt MPD caliber change with distal parenchymal atrophy.

Pituitary Incidentaloma
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Hoang JK et al. JACR 2018. Most incidental pituitary findings are microadenomas (nonfunctioning). Key questions: size, mass effect on optic chiasm, and hormonal activity. Endocrinology referral is the standard for macroadenomas.
ModalitySize / FindingRecommendation
CT (incidental)Any pituitary lesionDedicated pituitary MRI (3T preferred) for characterization; distinguish from normal variant
MRI — microadenoma (<10 mm)<6 mmNo follow-up imaging; endocrinology only if clinical concern for hormonal excess
MRI — microadenoma6–9 mmRepeat pituitary MRI in 12 months; if stable → discontinue; endocrinology referral
MRI — macroadenoma (≥10 mm)10–29 mm, no chiasm contactEndocrinology referral; repeat MRI in 6 months; ophthalmology if any visual symptoms
MRI — macroadenoma≥30 mm or chiasm contact / compressionUrgent endocrinology + neurosurgery referral; visual field testing
FDG-PET (incidental uptake)Any focal pituitary uptakeDedicated pituitary MRI; endocrinology referral
Biochemical screen for all macroadenomas: Prolactin (rule out prolactinoma — medical Rx first), cortisol/ACTH (Cushing's), IGF-1 (acromegaly), thyroid function, LH/FSH/testosterone or estradiol.
📄Hoang JK et al. ACR Incidental Pituitary Findings. JACR. 2018;15(7):966-972.
Renal Mass
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Herts BR et al. JACR 2018. Two key decision trees: (1) cystic masses → Bosniak classification; (2) solid masses → size and enhancement. Most incidental renal masses are benign (simple cysts, oncocytoma, AML).
