Bosniak CategoryFeaturesMalignancy RiskRecommendation
ISimple cyst: imperceptible wall, water density, no calcification, no enhancement<2%No follow-up
IIMinimally complicated: thin septa (1–2), fine calcification, <3 cm high-density cyst, no enhancement<2%No follow-up
IIFMultiple thin septa, minimal smooth thickening of septa or wall, no enhancing soft tissue, ≥3 cm high-density cyst~6–15%CT or MRI at 6 months, then annually × 5 years
IIIIndeterminate: thick or irregular septa/wall, measurable enhancement of septa~50%Urology referral; surgical excision vs. close follow-up depending on clinical context
IVClearly malignant: enhancing soft-tissue component, irregular walls, nodular enhancement>90%Urology referral; surgical resection standard

Solid / Indeterminate Renal Masses

Mass Size & TypeRecommendation
Solid, enhancing <1 cmCT or MRI at 12 months; if stable × 2 → discontinue (most are benign oncocytoma or small RCC)
Solid, enhancing 1–3 cmMRI with and without contrast to characterize; urology referral; consider biopsy
Solid, enhancing >3 cmUrology referral; surgical planning
Solid, macroscopic fat (AML)If <4 cm and classic fat-containing AML → no f/u; if ≥4 cm or symptomatic → urology referral (bleed risk)
Too small to characterize on CT (<1.5 cm)MRI; if still indeterminate → CT or MRI in 12 months; most are benign
Any mass, known malignancy or immunosuppressionMRI for characterization; urology/oncology referral at lower threshold
📄Herts BR et al. ACR Incidental Renal Mass. JACR. 2018;15(2):264-273.
📄Silverman SG et al. Bosniak Classification v2019. Radiology. 2019;292(2):475-488.
Splenic Lesions & Abdominal Nodes
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Heller MT et al. JACR 2013. Most incidental splenic lesions are benign (cysts, hemangiomas, granulomas). Abdominal lymph node thresholds: >1 cm short axis in retroperitoneum/mesentery is the standard size criterion for enlargement.

Splenic Lesions

Lesion Type / SizeRecommendation
Splenic cyst <2 cm (simple, no internal structure)No follow-up
Splenic cyst ≥2 cmUS or MRI in 6–12 months; if stable → annual × 2 then discontinue
Hemangioma (confirmed typical: hyperechoic on US, T2-bright on MRI)No follow-up
Solid lesion <1 cm, low-risk patientNo follow-up if clearly benign appearance; repeat CT/MRI in 3–6 months if indeterminate
Solid lesion ≥1 cm or indeterminateMRI or CEUS for characterization; hematology/oncology referral if suspicious
Calcified granuloma (multiple small calcifications)No follow-up (prior granulomatous disease)
Splenomegaly (>13 cm in long axis) incidentalClinical evaluation; CBC, LFTs, consider hematology

Abdominal Lymph Nodes

Short AxisCharacteristicsRecommendation
<10 mmFatty hilum, oval shape (normal morphology)No follow-up
10–19 mm, no known malignancyRound, indistinct hilum, or isolated findingCT in 3 months; if stable → no further f/u
≥20 mm, OR necrotic, OR hypervascularAny morphologyPET-CT or tissue sampling; oncology referral
Cluster of ≥3 nodes in one station OR ≥2 in two separate regionsEven if each <10 mmClinical correlation; consider further evaluation
📄Heller MT et al. ACR Incidental Splenic & Nodal Findings. JACR. 2013;10(11):833-839.
Thyroid Nodules
Source: ACR Incidental Findings Committee — Hoang JK et al. JACR 2015 (CT/MRI/PET incidentals). For dedicated thyroid ultrasound, use ACR TI-RADS. These recommendations apply specifically to thyroid nodules found incidentally on non-thyroid imaging (CT, MRI, PET).

Incidental on CT or MRI

FindingRecommendation
Single nodule <1 cm, no suspicious features (smooth margins, no calcification, homogeneous)No further workup
Single nodule 1–1.5 cm OR multiple nodules <1.5 cm, no suspicious featuresThyroid ultrasound if clinically appropriate; endocrinology referral optional
Any nodule ≥1.5 cmThyroid ultrasound and endocrinology referral
Suspicious features on CT/MRI (irregular margins, microcalcifications, taller-than-wide, extracapsular extension)Thyroid ultrasound regardless of size; endocrinology referral
Diffuse thyroid enlargement / multinodular goiter without dominant noduleThyroid function tests; ultrasound if clinical concern

Incidental on FDG-PET

FindingRecommendation
Focal FDG uptake in thyroidThyroid ultrasound + endocrinology referral — malignancy risk ~30–35% for focal uptake
Diffuse FDG uptakeThyroid function tests — likely thyroiditis; imaging follow-up not routinely required
ACR TI-RADS for dedicated thyroid ultrasound: uses composition, echogenicity, shape, margin, and echogenic foci to assign points (TR1–TR5) with size-dependent biopsy thresholds. ACR TI-RADS Calculator
📄Hoang JK et al. ACR Incidental Thyroid Findings. JACR. 2015;12(2):143-150.
Abdominal Vasculature
Sources: ACR Incidental Findings Committee (Khosa F et al. JACR 2013) and SVS Clinical Practice Guidelines on visceral aneurysms (Chaer RA et al. J Vasc Surg 2020).
Vessel / FindingThresholdRecommendation
AAA (infrarenal aortic aneurysm)<3.0 cm: normal · 3.0–3.9 cm: aneurysm, US q3yr · 4.0–4.9 cm: US q12mo · 5.0–5.4 cm: US or CT q6moSurgical referral: ≥5.5 cm (men) or ≥5.0 cm (women); or growth ≥1 cm/yr
Iliac artery aneurysm<3.0 cm: normal · 3.0–3.5 cm: follow-up in 6 monthsSurgical referral: >3.5 cm or rapidly growing
Splenic artery aneurysm<2 cm: annual surveillance if >1.5 cmSurgical/IR referral: ≥2 cm; or women of childbearing age, liver transplant candidates, portal hypertension (rupture risk higher)
Hepatic artery aneurysmAny true aneurysmSurgical/IR referral — high rupture risk
SMA aneurysmAny true aneurysmSurgical/IR referral
Renal artery aneurysm<1.5 cm: 1–2 yr f/uSurgical referral: ≥1.5 cm or in women of childbearing age
Pancreaticoduodenal artery aneurysmAnyAll should be referred — high rupture risk regardless of size
Penetrating aortic ulcer (PAU)Asymptomatic, incidentalAnnual CT surveillance; expeditious referral if symptomatic or enlarging
IVC dilation / anomalyIVC >28 mm or duplicated IVCEchocardiography to exclude right heart failure; CT for anatomic variant documentation
Pelvic venous dilation (female)Ovarian vein >4 mm or pelvic varicositiesConsider pelvic congestion syndrome if symptomatic; asymptomatic → no f/u
📄Khosa F et al. ACR Incidental Vascular Findings. JACR. 2013;10(10):789-794.
📄Chaer RA et al. SVS Guidelines on Visceral Aneurysms. J Vasc Surg. 2020;72(1S):3S-39S.

ACR MR Safety Guidance
ACR
MRI Labeling Terminology
LabelDefinition
MR SafeNo known hazards in any MR environment
MR ConditionalSafe in specific MR conditions (field strength, SAR, gradient limits) — check device label
MR UnsafePoses hazards in all MR environments — do not scan
Retained Foreign Body Considerations
Foreign BodyMRI SafetyAction
Cardiac pacemaker / ICDConditional (most modern) / Unsafe (legacy)Check model; MR-conditional pacing protocol required
Cochlear implantsConditional (most)Check model; magnet may need removal
Intracranial aneurysm clipsConditional/Unsafe depending on materialCheck mrisafety.com before scanning
Ocular metallic foreign bodyUnsafe if intraocular metalPlain films of orbits if history of metal work to eyes
Orthopedic implants (screws, plates)Usually conditional; cause artifactMost stainless steel / titanium implants can be scanned
Bullet / shrapnelContext-dependentAvoid scanning if near vital structures; check location

Search Patterns
Systematic study-by-study approach — MRI studies follow sequence order (T2 → DWI → T1 → T1+C → specialty sequences)
How to use: Select a study type from the TOC. Each numbered step is a major search category to evaluate in sequence. MRI studies follow sequence order (T2 → DWI → T1 → T1+C → specialty). Sub-bullets list specific findings within each step. MSK MRI studies include ↗ reference links to Radiology Assistant anatomy articles.

Chest X-Ray (PA) XR
CXR — Chest X-Ray (PA)
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Clinical context: acute vs. routine; symptoms (SOB, cough, chest pain, fever)
    • Prior CXRs or CT for comparison
  • 2
    Scout / Image Quality
    • Rotation: clavicles equidistant from spinous process
    • Inspiration: ≥6 anterior ribs above diaphragm
    • Exposure: vertebrae visible through cardiac shadow; vascular markings in lungs
  • 3
    Airways
    • Tracheal position and caliber
    • Carina angle (normal <70°)
    • Visible bronchi for central mass or mucous plug
  • 4
    Cardiac Silhouette
    • Cardiothoracic ratio ≤0.5 on PA
    • Cardiac borders: right (SVC, RA), left (aortic knuckle, PA, LAA, LV)
    • Mediastinal width — superior (aortic knuckle, trachea deviation)
  • 5
    Hila
    • Hilar size, density, and position (left normally slightly higher)
    • Hilar fullness: adenopathy vs. vascular prominence
  • 6
    Lung Zones
    • Upper zones: vascular caliber, apical disease
    • Mid zones: perihilar masses, consolidation
    • Lower zones: consolidation, effusion, atelectasis
    • Interstitial pattern: Kerley B lines, reticular, nodular
    • Ground-glass opacity or consolidation distribution
  • 7
    Pleural Spaces
    • Costophrenic angles: blunting (>200–300 mL)
    • Fissures: major (oblique) and minor (horizontal) — for lobar collapse localization
    • Pneumothorax: visceral pleural line, absent lung markings
  • 8
    Diaphragm & Below
    • Right hemidiaphragm slightly higher (1.5–2.5 cm)
    • Subphrenic free air
    • Gastric bubble, bowel loops
  • 9
    Bones & Soft Tissues
    • Ribs: fractures (acute vs. healing), lytic/sclerotic lesions
    • Clavicles, scapulae, humeral heads
    • Soft tissues: subcutaneous emphysema, breast shadows, calcifications
Quality Checks
CXR Quality Criteria
  • Q1
    No RotationClavicular symmetry confirmed
  • Q2
    Adequate Inspiration≥6 anterior ribs above diaphragm
  • Q3
    Adequate ExposureVertebrae visible through cardiac shadow
  • Q4
    Full CoverageEntire chest from apices to costophrenic angles
Chest X-Ray (Lateral) XR
CXR Lateral — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Correlate with PA: localize abnormalities to anterior vs. posterior, upper vs. lower
    • Prior lateral CXR for comparison
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • True lateral: posterior ribs overlap
    • Rotation check: rib posterior arcs should align
  • 3
    Retrosternal Space
    • Clear space anterior to heart (Raider triangle)
    • Anterior mediastinal mass (4 Ts): thyroid, thymoma, teratoma, terrible lymphoma
  • 4
    Cardiac Borders
    • Anterior border: RV
    • Posterior border: LA (upper), LV (lower)
    • IVC where it enters RA
  • 5
    Retrocardiac Space
    • Posterior to heart — should be clear
    • Consolidation/mass in lower lobes
  • 6
    Trachea & Posterior Tracheal Band
    • Trachea: anterior wall, posterior tracheal stripe
    • Hilar overlay sign
  • 7
    Fissures
    • Major (oblique) fissure: runs from T4–5 posterior to anterior diaphragm
    • Minor fissure: horizontal, anterior
  • 8
    Vertebrae
    • Progressive lucency from upper to lower thoracic spine (normally gets darker)
    • Loss of disc space height, vertebral body compression
    • Dense vertebral body = mass or fracture
  • 9
    Diaphragm & Hemidiaphragms
    • Right: anterior (liver) to posterior; left: anterior (stomach bubble) — lower
    • Posterior costophrenic sulcus: effusion blunting
  • 10
    Retrotracheal Triangle & Posterior Mediastinum
    • Posterior mediastinal masses: neurogenic, paraspinal abscess
  • 11
    Bones & Soft Tissues
    • Sternum: fracture (look for angulation)
    • Thoracic spine: kyphosis, fractures
    • Scapulae, shoulder joints
Portable / ICU CXR XR
Technique limitations: AP projection (heart appears larger), supine (effusions layer posteriorly, PTX may be anterior), portable equipment (lower kVp, more scatter). Always note technique.
ICU CXR — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Current clinical status: post-procedure (line/tube placement) vs. interval change (new respiratory failure, desaturation)
    • Compare with most recent prior portable CXR
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • AP vs. PA (note magnification)
    • Rotation, inspiration, exposure
    • Supine vs. upright position noted
  • 3
    Lines & Tubes — Check Every One
    • ETT: tip 3–7 cm above carina, at level of aortic knuckle (~T4–6); not in right mainstem
    • NGT/OGT: tip below left hemidiaphragm; side port below GEJ
    • CVL (IJ/subclavian): tip in SVC/cavoatrial junction; no pneumothorax
    • PICC: tip in SVC or cavoatrial junction
    • Chest tube: tip position, lung re-expansion
    • PA catheter: tip in right or left PA, no more than 5 cm from midline
    • IABP: tip at level of aortic knuckle (T4–6), 1–2 cm below left subclavian
    • Pacemaker leads: RA appendage, RV apex
  • 4
    Interval Pneumothorax
    • Supine PTX: deep sulcus sign, hyperlucent hemithorax (anteromedial or subpulmonic PTX)
    • Post-procedure (line placement, thoracentesis, barotrauma)
  • 5
    Airspace Disease
    • New consolidation vs. prior
    • Atelectasis vs. true pneumonia (typically more rapid change)
    • Pulmonary edema: vascular redistribution, Kerley B lines, bilateral confluent opacities
  • 6
    Pleural Effusion (Supine)
    • Homogeneous opacity over entire hemithorax or layering along dependent pleura
    • Apical capping
    • Obscuration of hemidiaphragm
  • 7
    Cardiac Silhouette
    • Interval widening: pericardial effusion, mediastinal hematoma
    • Pneumomediastinum: lucency outlining mediastinal structures
  • 8
    Miscellaneous
    • Subcutaneous emphysema
    • Rib fractures (trauma patients)
    • Osseous changes
⚠ Check every line and tube position before evaluating for new pathology.
CT Chest CT
Use lung windows (W:1500/L:-600) for parenchyma, mediastinal windows (W:400/L:40) for soft tissues, bone windows for ribs/spine.
CT Chest — Systematic Review
  • 1
    Triage / Gestalt
    • Scroll through entire study at low magnification first
    • Identify obvious dominant abnormality (consolidation, mass, effusion, pneumothorax)
    • Note contrast phase: non-contrast, arterial, venous, or delayed
  • 2
    Scout Image
    • Cardiac size, mediastinum, bony thorax
    • Vertebral alignment on scout lateral if available
  • 3
    History & Clinical Context
    • Indication: staging, infection, ILD, trauma, post-op?
    • Prior CT or CXR for comparison
  • 4
    Adequacy
    • Coverage: lung apices through adrenals/upper abdomen
    • Contrast timing if contrast-enhanced
    • Motion/artifact
  • 5
    Support Devices
    • Lines and tubes (if ICU patient)
    • Pacemaker/defibrillator leads position
  • 6
    Airways
    • Trachea: caliber, deviation, intraluminal lesion
    • Main, lobar, segmental bronchi: obstruction, mucous plugging
    • Bronchiectasis (traction or cylindrical)
  • 7
    Mediastinum (Mediastinal Windows)
    • Superior: vascular structures, trachea, lymph nodes
    • Middle: heart chambers, pericardium, aortic root, PA
    • Posterior: esophagus, descending aorta, paraspinal
  • 8
    Hila
    • Hilar lymph nodes: size >1 cm short axis
    • Hilar masses: primary lung vs. nodal
  • 9
    Pleural Spaces
    • Effusion: free vs. loculated, unilateral vs. bilateral
    • Pleural thickening, calcification, masses
    • Pneumothorax: size estimation
  • 10
    Lung Parenchyma (Lung Windows)
    • Nodules: size, density (solid/subsolid/GGO), morphology, distribution
    • Consolidation: distribution, air bronchograms, density
    • Ground-glass opacity: extent, distribution
    • Interstitial pattern: septal thickening, reticulation, honeycombing, distribution (UIP vs. NSIP)
    • Emphysema: centrilobular/panlobular/paraseptal
    • Mosaic attenuation: air trapping vs. perfusion vs. infiltrative
    • Lymphadenopathy: mediastinal and hilar nodes
  • 11
    Extrathoracic Structures
    • Chest wall and ribs: fractures, lytic/blastic lesions
    • Thoracic spine: vertebral body height, alignment, marrow density
    • Thyroid (if visualized): nodules, goiter extension
    • Upper abdomen: liver dome, adrenals, spleen, stomach
    • Axillae: lymphadenopathy
    • Breast tissue (incidental)
  • 12
    Last Checks
    • Bone windows for rib and vertebral fractures (don't miss)
    • Review coronal/sagittal reconstructions
    • Compare with prior imaging
    • Measure any significant findings (nodule diameter, lymph node SAX)
CT Pulmonary Angiography (CT-PA) CT
Adequate opacification: PA attenuation ≥200 HU. Check for filling defects centrally before peripherally (saddle → main → lobar → segmental → subsegmental).
CT-PA — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Clinical Context
    • Pre-test probability (Wells score, PERC)
    • Prior CT-PA or V/Q for comparison
    • Anticoagulation status
  • 2
    Adequacy / Technical Quality
    • PA opacification ≥200 HU (check pulmonary trunk)
    • Respiratory motion — streak artifact
    • Coverage from above aortic arch through bilateral costophrenic angles
    • Bolus timing: arterial phase adequate
  • 3
    Pulmonary Arteries — Central
    • Saddle embolus: filling defect straddling bifurcation
    • Main PA: bilateral filling defects
    • Right PA and left PA trunk
  • 4
    Pulmonary Arteries — Lobar
    • RUL, RML, RLL lobar arteries
    • LUL, lingula, LLL lobar arteries
  • 5
    Pulmonary Arteries — Segmental & Subsegmental
    • All segmental branches bilaterally
    • Subsegmental emboli: report presence; note clinical significance in isolation
  • 6
    Signs of Right Heart Strain
    • RV:LV diameter ratio >0.9 (or >1.0) on axial
    • Interventricular septal flattening or bowing toward LV (D-sign)
    • Contrast reflux into IVC/hepatic veins
  • 7
    Lung Parenchyma
    • Hampton's hump: pleural-based wedge consolidation (infarction)
    • Westermark sign: oligemia distal to embolus
    • Atelectasis, ground-glass opacity adjacent to infarct
    • Concurrent pneumonia, consolidation
  • 8
    Pleural Spaces
    • Effusion (exudative if infarction)
    • Hemothorax (trauma setting)
  • 9
    Mediastinum & Heart
    • Mediastinal widening, aortic caliber
    • Pericardial effusion
    • Cardiac chambers
  • 10
    Pulmonary Hypertension Signs
    • PA diameter >29 mm
    • PA:aorta diameter ratio >1
    • RV enlargement, tricuspid regurgitation signs
  • 11
    Extrathoracic Incidentals
    • Axillae, chest wall, bones
    • Upper abdomen (liver, adrenals, kidneys)
    • Thyroid
  • 12
    Last Checks
    • Coronal and sagittal reconstructions
    • Deep vein thrombosis if legs included
    • Chronic vs. acute PE features: eccentric/calcified/web-like vs. central/acute filling defect
⚠ Isolated subsegmental PE: correlation with clinical probability required before treatment decision.
MRI Chest MRI
Sequences vary: cardiac MRI uses ECG-gated cine GRE/SSFP; mediastinal/pleural MRI uses T1, T2, STIR, T1+C. Note indication-specific protocol.
MRI Chest — Sequence-Based Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Cardiac MRI (function, ischemia, cardiomyopathy, valvular disease) vs. mediastinal/pleural mass vs. chest wall
    • Prior echo, CT, or cardiac MRI for comparison
    • Gadolinium given? Note timing and dose
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Cardiac gating quality (for cardiac MRI)
    • Sequences present: T1, T2, STIR, SSFP/cine, phase-contrast, LGE, T1+C
    • Respiratory motion, artifact level
    • Coverage: cardiac MRI (heart + great vessels) vs. mediastinal (lung apices to diaphragm)
  • 3
    T2 / STIR Sequences
    • Cardiac: myocardial edema (T2-bright = myocarditis, acute ischemia)
    • Mediastinal masses: T2 characterization — cystic (very bright), solid, mixed
    • Pericardium: T2-bright pericardial effusion vs. thickening
    • Pleural disease: T2-bright effusion vs. solid tumor (mesothelioma)
    • Chest wall: muscle edema, rib marrow signal
  • 4
    Cine / SSFP Sequences (Cardiac MRI)
    • Biventricular function: LV EF, RV EF (normal LV EF ≥55%)
    • Wall motion: regional RWMA (segment, territory)
    • LV wall thickness: hypertrophy distribution (concentric vs. asymmetric)
    • Valves: regurgitant/stenotic jets, morphology
    • Pericardial motion: constriction signs (septal bounce, respirophasic variation)
  • 5
    T1 Pre-Contrast
    • Fat content: T1-bright = fat (lipoma, dermoid, thymolipoma), hemorrhage, proteinaceous fluid
    • Mediastinal mass characterization: anterior (4Ts), middle (vascular), posterior (neurogenic)
    • Cardiac masses: T1 vs. T2 signal to differentiate thrombus vs. tumor
  • 6
    T1 Post-Contrast / Late Gadolinium Enhancement (LGE)
    • LGE pattern for cardiomyopathy: subendocardial (ischemic) vs. mid-wall/epicardial (non-ischemic)
    • Pericardial enhancement: pericarditis, constrictive pattern
    • Mediastinal/pleural mass enhancement: solid enhancement = malignancy or thymoma
    • Perineural spread if neck/mediastinal mass
    • Myocarditis: focal mid-wall LGE typical in lateral wall
  • 7
    Phase-Contrast / Flow Sequences (if performed)
    • Quantify valvular regurgitant fractions
    • Aortic/pulmonary flow assessment
    • Congenital shunt quantification: Qp:Qs
  • 8
    Vascular Structures
    • Aorta: caliber, MRA if included (aneurysm, dissection flap)
    • Pulmonary arteries: thrombus, compression by mass
    • SVC/IVC: patency, thrombus, extrinsic compression
  • 9
    Extrathoracic Structures
    • Liver dome, spleen, adrenals (upper abdomen)
    • Chest wall and ribs: bone marrow signal, cortical destruction
    • Axillary soft tissues and lymph nodes
    • Thyroid and lower neck
  • 10
    Last Checks
    • Coronal and sagittal reformats
    • Compare with prior echo or CT
    • Report EF, volumes, mass dimensions if applicable
Abdominal X-Ray (AXR) XR
AXR — Abdominal X-Ray
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Obstruction? Perforation? Constipation? Post-procedure?
    • Prior AXR or CT for comparison
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • Coverage: from diaphragm to pubic symphysis
    • Exposure and positioning
  • 3
    Bowel Gas Pattern
    • Small bowel: central position, valvulae conniventes (stack of coins), normal <3 cm
    • Large bowel: peripheral/haustra, normal <6 cm (cecum <9 cm)
    • Obstruction: dilated loops proximal to transition; step-ladder pattern (SBO)
    • Ileus: diffuse non-obstructive dilation
    • Pneumatosis intestinalis: intramural air
  • 4
    Free Air
    • Rigler sign: both sides of bowel wall visible
    • Football sign: free air outlining falciform ligament
    • Subdiaphragmatic air on upright/decubitus (if available)
  • 5
    Solid Organs
    • Liver size (right flank stripe)
    • Spleen size (left flank stripe)
    • Kidneys: renal outlines; psoas shadow obliteration
  • 6
    Calcifications
    • Renal calculi: opaque, overlying collecting system
    • Gallstones: 10–15% opaque, RUQ
    • Pancreatic: chronic pancreatitis
    • Phleboliths vs. ureteral stones (phleboliths have lucent center)
    • Vascular: aortic/iliac calcification, AAA
  • 7
    Masses / Soft Tissue
    • Pelvic mass displacing bowel loops
    • Retroperitoneal mass: lateral displacement of ascending/descending colon
  • 8
    Bones
    • Lumbar spine: alignment, disc spaces, pedicle integrity
    • Pelvic bones: iliac wings, SI joints, pubic symphysis
    • Hip joints: femoral heads
  • 9
    Extraabdominal
    • Lung bases: pleural effusion, consolidation
    • Lower rib fractures
    • Subcutaneous tissues
Fluoroscopic Esophagram Fluoro
Single-contrast (water-soluble if perforation suspected) vs. double-contrast (barium + effervescent). Obtain multiple projections. Lateral neck for pharynx/Zenker. RAO for esophagus.
Esophagram — Fluoroscopic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Dysphagia (oropharyngeal vs. esophageal), odynophagia, GERD, suspected perforation, post-op assessment
    • Prior endoscopy or barium results
  • 2
    Swallow Mechanics & Pharynx
    • Oral phase: bolus formation, premature spillage
    • Pharyngeal phase: velopharyngeal closure, laryngeal elevation, epiglottic retroversion, cricopharyngeal relaxation
    • Aspiration: barium in larynx/trachea; penetration vs. aspiration
    • Pharyngeal residue: valleculae, pyriform sinuses
    • Pharyngeal masses or mucosal irregularity
  • 3
    Cervical Esophagus
    • Cricopharyngeal bar/impression
    • Zenker diverticulum: posterior midline outpouching above cricopharyngeus
    • Webs: thin anterior defects
  • 4
    Thoracic Esophagus
    • Caliber: narrowing (stricture, extrinsic compression, ring)
    • Mucosal pattern: smooth vs. irregular (carcinoma), fold thickening
    • Peristalsis: primary, secondary, tertiary contractions
    • Extrinsic impressions: aorta, left atrium, aberrant subclavian
  • 5
    Esophageal Motility
    • Primary peristalsis: complete stripping wave
    • Achalasia: dilated esophagus, bird-beak tapering at GEJ, aperistalsis
    • Diffuse esophageal spasm: corkscrew, tertiary contractions
    • Scleroderma: aperistalsis lower 2/3, dilated, patulous GEJ
  • 6
    Gastroesophageal Junction
    • Schatzki ring: mucosal ring at B-ring (squamocolumnar junction), symptomatic if <13 mm
    • Hiatal hernia: gastric mucosa above GEJ above diaphragm
    • Reflux: barium reflux above GEJ (note: seen in normals; clinical correlation needed)
  • 7
    Filling Defects & Strictures
    • Carcinoma: irregular mucosal destruction, shouldering, apple-core lesion
    • Benign stricture: smooth, tapered
    • Leiomyoma: smooth filling defect, intact overlying mucosa
    • Varices: tortuous longitudinal defects; worm-like
  • 8
    Last Checks
    • Full column and mucosal relief views
    • Lateral fluoroscopic views of pharynx for posterior lesions
    • Delayed images if achalasia suspected
Fluoroscopic Upper GI Series Fluoro
Standard double-contrast (barium + gas). Include esophagus (single-contrast), stomach, duodenum. Prone and supine positions. Dedicated esophagram may be separate.
Upper GI — Fluoroscopic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Epigastric pain, nausea/vomiting, suspected leak (post-op — use water-soluble), PUD, Crohn's, malignancy workup
    • Prior endoscopy or upper GI results
  • 2
    Esophagus
    • Quick assessment: caliber, peristalsis, GEJ, hiatal hernia
    • If dedicated esophagram needed, order separately
  • 3
    Stomach — Fundus & Cardia
    • Position: fundus under left hemidiaphragm
    • Fundal masses, linitis plastica pattern
  • 4
    Stomach — Body & Antrum
    • Rugal folds: thickened (Ménétrier, Zollinger-Ellison), effaced (carcinoma, linitis plastica)
    • Gastric ulcer: lesser curvature (benign — Hampton's line, ulcer collar) vs. greater curvature (malignant features: Carman meniscus sign)
    • Gastric outlet obstruction: antral narrowing, delayed emptying
  • 5
    Duodenum — Bulb
    • Bulb: triangular shape; ulcer niche — base, apex, posterior wall
    • Deformity: chronic scarring from PUD
    • Filling defects: polyp, Brunner gland hyperplasia
  • 6
    Duodenum — Second through Fourth Portions
    • C-loop: widening (pancreatic mass, annular pancreas)
    • Mucosal pattern: thickened folds (Zollinger-Ellison, Crohn's, celiac, lymphoma)
    • Duodenal masses, web, malrotation
  • 7
    Small Bowel (if included)
    • Caliber: normal jejunum <3 cm, ileum <2.5 cm
    • Fold pattern: jejunum (plicae circulares close together) vs. ileum (fewer folds)
    • String sign of Crohn's
    • Entero-enteric fistula, obstruction
CT Chest / Abdomen / Pelvis (CAP) CT
Most often venous phase (70–80 s). Lung windows for chest; soft tissue and bone windows throughout. Oral contrast may be given. Check all three body cavities systematically.
CT CAP — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Oncologic staging/surveillance vs. infection/inflammation vs. trauma
    • Prior CT or PET-CT for comparison
    • Contrast phase: arterial, venous, delayed, or portal
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • Coverage: lung apices to pubic symphysis
    • Contrast timing adequate
    • Motion artifact
  • 3
    Chest (Lung Windows)
    • Nodules/masses: size, density, location
    • Pleural effusions, pneumothorax
    • Lymphadenopathy: mediastinal, hilar
    • Airways: obstruction
    • Interstitial changes
  • 4
    Chest (Mediastinal Windows)
    • Heart and pericardium
    • Great vessels: aorta caliber, PE if arterial phase
    • Esophagus: wall thickening
    • Chest wall, ribs
  • 5
    Liver
    • Lesion detection: hypodense (venous), hypervascular (arterial)
    • Morphology: cirrhosis (nodular surface, caudate hypertrophy, lobar atrophy)
    • Biliary: bile duct dilation, stones, air
  • 6
    Gallbladder & Bile Ducts
    • Gallbladder: wall thickening, stones, pericholecystic fluid
    • Common bile duct: diameter >6 mm (>10 mm post-cholecystectomy)
  • 7
    Pancreas
    • Enhancement pattern: hypo- vs. hypervascular lesion
    • Pancreatic duct dilation (>3 mm)
    • Peripancreatic fat stranding
    • Cystic lesions: IPMN, serous/mucinous cystadenoma
  • 8
    Spleen
    • Size (>13 cm = splenomegaly)
    • Focal lesions: hemangioma, metastasis, lymphoma
    • Splenic vein patency
  • 9
    Adrenal Glands
    • Size: >10 mm limb width = adenoma vs. metastasis
    • Attenuation: <10 HU = adenoma (lipid-rich)
    • Bilateral enlargement: metastases, lymphoma, CAH
  • 10
    Kidneys & Ureters
    • Renal mass: Bosniak classification for cysts, solid enhancement
    • Hydronephrosis: level of obstruction
    • Stones: hyperdense in collecting system
  • 11
    Bowel
    • Small bowel: wall thickness, mesenteric fat stranding, obstruction
    • Colon: wall thickening, diverticulitis, mass
    • Appendix: diameter >6 mm, periappendiceal fat stranding
    • Free fluid / free air
  • 12
    Pelvis
    • Bladder: wall thickening, mass, stones
    • Uterus/ovaries or prostate/seminal vesicles
    • Pelvic lymph nodes (iliac, obturator)
    • Pelvic free fluid
  • 13
    Retroperitoneum & Vasculature
    • Aorta: diameter, aneurysm, dissection
    • Iliac arteries, portal vein, IVC patency
    • Para-aortic and pelvic lymphadenopathy (>1 cm SAX)
  • 14
    Bones
    • Vertebral bodies: compression fractures, lytic/blastic lesions
    • Pelvic bones, sacrum, femoral heads
    • Ribs: fractures, lytic lesions
CT Abdomen / Pelvis (AP) CT
CT Abdomen/Pelvis — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Acute abdomen vs. staging vs. follow-up
    • Contrast phase: non-contrast, arterial, venous, portal, excretory
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • Coverage: diaphragm to pubic symphysis
    • Contrast timing, motion artifact
  • 3
    Free Air / Free Fluid
    • Pneumoperitoneum: free air under diaphragm, outlining liver (Rigler sign on scout)
    • Ascites: dependent free fluid; hemoperitoneum (hyperdense >30 HU)
  • 4
    Liver
    • Lobar anatomy (Couinaud segments for surgical planning)
    • Lesions: hypodense (metastasis, abscess), hypervascular (HCC, hemangioma, FNH)
    • Cirrhosis morphology, portal hypertension (varices, splenomegaly, ascites)
    • Bile duct dilation: intrahepatic vs. extrahepatic
  • 5
    Gallbladder & Bile Ducts
    • Cholecystitis: wall >3 mm, pericholecystic fluid, Murphy sign on CT
    • Choledocholithiasis: hyperdense stone in CBD
  • 6
    Pancreas
    • Pancreatitis: stranding, necrosis (non-enhancing), pseudocyst, abscess
    • Mass: pancreatic adenocarcinoma (hypo), IPMN (duct or cystic), PNET (hypervascular)
  • 7
    Spleen
    • Size, focal lesions, trauma (laceration, subcapsular hematoma), infarct
  • 8
    Adrenals
    • Bilateral (mets, pheochromocytoma, adrenal hyperplasia) vs. unilateral (adenoma, mets)
    • Attenuation <10 HU or washout >60% = adenoma
  • 9
    Kidneys
    • Mass characterization: Bosniak cyst, RCC enhancement
    • Hydronephrosis and collecting system
    • Renal infarct (wedge-shaped perfusion defect)
    • Pyelonephritis: striated nephrogram, perinephric stranding
  • 10
    Bowel
    • Small bowel obstruction: transition point, closed loop, ischemia signs
    • Colitis: wall thickening, distribution (infectious vs. ischemic vs. IBD)
    • Appendicitis: >6 mm, periappendiceal fat, appendicolith
    • Diverticulitis: sigmoid fat stranding, abscess, fistula
    • Bowel ischemia: pneumatosis, portal venous gas, mesenteric vascular thrombosis
  • 11
    Mesentery & Retroperitoneum
    • Mesenteric lymph nodes, fat stranding
    • Retroperitoneal fibrosis, masses, hematoma
  • 12
    Pelvis
    • Bladder: mass, diverticulum, hematoma
    • Uterus, ovaries (cysts, TOA, mass), fallopian tubes
    • Prostate enlargement or mass
    • Inguinal/femoral hernias
  • 13
    Vasculature
    • Aorta: atherosclerosis, aneurysm (>3 cm = ectatic, >5.5 cm = operative threshold), dissection
    • IVC: thrombus, Budd-Chiari
    • Mesenteric vessels: SMA/SMV thrombosis (acute ischemia)
    • Portal vein
MRI Abdomen MRI
Standard protocol: T2 axial/coronal, MRCP (if biliary), DWI, T1 in/out of phase, T1 fat-sat dynamic (arterial 25–30 s, portal 70 s, delayed 3–5 min). Liver-specific agents (Eovist/Primovist) have hepatobiliary phase at 20 min.
MRI Abdomen — Sequence-Based Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Liver lesion characterization (HCC, metastasis, hemangioma, FNH, adenoma)
    • Biliary pathology (MRCP)
    • Pancreatic mass characterization
    • Prior CT, MRI, or US for comparison; gadolinium/liver-specific agent?
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Sequences present: T2 axial + coronal, DWI, T1 in-phase/out-of-phase, T1 fat-sat multiphasic dynamic, T1+C delayed
    • MRCP present if biliary indication
    • Breath-holding quality, motion artifact, coverage (diaphragm to iliac crests)
  • 3
    T2-Weighted Images
    • Liver signal: T2-bright lesions (hemangioma very bright, HCC intermediate, metastasis variable)
    • Bile ducts: intrahepatic and extrahepatic duct caliber
    • Gallbladder: wall signal, stones (T2 dark)
    • Spleen: homogeneous T2 bright; focal lesions
    • Pancreas: T2 signal, duct visibility
    • Kidneys: corticomedullary differentiation, cysts (very T2 bright), solid masses
    • Ascites: T2-bright free fluid
    • Bowel: wall thickness, luminal dilation
  • 4
    MRCP (if performed)
    • Biliary tree: CBD caliber, intrahepatic ducts, tapering vs. abrupt cutoff
    • Pancreatic duct: caliber, side branches (IPMN — main duct vs. side branch)
    • Choledocholithiasis: T2-dark stone in duct
    • Strictures: primary sclerosing cholangitis (beading), cholangiocarcinoma (dominant stricture)
    • Post-surgical anatomy: bilioenteric anastomosis
  • 5
    DWI / ADC Map
    • Diffusion-restricting lesions in liver: HCC, metastasis, abscess, cholangiocarcinoma
    • Pancreatic adenocarcinoma: hypointense on ADC
    • Lymph nodes: size + diffusion restriction = malignancy
    • Bowel wall diffusion restriction: ischemia, colitis
  • 6
    T1 In-Phase / Out-of-Phase
    • Signal drop on opposed-phase = intracellular fat (adrenal adenoma, HCC with fatty change, NAFLD/steatosis)
    • Diffuse hepatic steatosis: liver-to-spleen signal ratio
    • Focal fat deposition: geographic, perivascular
    • Adrenal lesions: chemical shift analysis
    • T1-bright lesions on in-phase: fat (angiomyolipoma), blood products
  • 7
    T1 Fat-Sat Dynamic Post-Contrast
    • Arterial phase (25–30 s): hypervascular lesions — HCC (arterial enhancement), FNH (spoke-wheel), hemangioma (peripheral nodular), PNET, hypervascular mets
    • Portal venous phase (70 s): hypovascular metastases (most conspicuous), liver parenchymal phase
    • Delayed phase (3–5 min): hemangioma fill-in, cholangiocarcinoma progressive enhancement, capsular retraction
    • Hepatobiliary phase (Eovist, 20 min): hypointense HCC vs. FNH (isointense or bright), adenoma dropout
  • 8
    Extrahepatic Organs
    • Spleen: focal lesions, size
    • Adrenal glands: unilateral vs. bilateral, enhancement pattern
    • Kidneys: enhancement pattern, collecting system
    • Retroperitoneum: lymph nodes, aorta
    • Bowel: wall enhancement, mesentery
  • 9
    Last Checks
    • Coronal and sagittal reformats
    • LI-RADS scoring if HCC risk: size, enhancement, washout, capsule, threshold growth
    • Measure all significant lesions (size, location, segment)
    • Compare with prior to determine stability/growth
MRI Pelvis (General) MRI
Multiplanar T2 is the workhorse: axial (oblique to uterine axis for uterine studies), coronal, sagittal. DWI and T1+C for lesion characterization.
MRI Pelvis — Sequence-Based Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Uterine (fibroid mapping, endometrial cancer staging, adenomyosis) vs. cervical cancer staging vs. ovarian mass vs. prostate (separate protocol) vs. male pelvic
    • Hormonal status, menstrual phase (affects endometrial/myometrial signal)
    • Prior imaging; gadolinium planned?
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Sequences present: T2 sagittal, T2 axial (true axial + oblique axial to uterus), T2 coronal, DWI, T1, T1+C
    • Coverage: iliac crests to pubic symphysis; include kidneys if hydronephrosis suspected
    • Motion artifact, bladder fill
  • 3
    Sagittal T2 — Pelvic Anatomy Overview
    • Uterine position: anteverted/retroverted/retroflexed; size
    • Zonal anatomy (premenopausal): endometrium (bright), junctional zone (dark, <12 mm), outer myometrium (intermediate)
    • Junctional zone thickening >12 mm or ill-defined = adenomyosis
    • Endometrial thickness and signal
    • Cervix: fibrous stroma (T2 dark), canal, ectocervix
    • Rectosigmoid, bladder, cul-de-sac
  • 4
    Axial T2 — Detailed Organ Assessment
    • Uterus: fibroids (T2 dark whorled), adenomyosis foci, endometrial mass
    • Cervix: parametrial invasion (disruption of dark cervical ring = ≥FIGO IIB)
    • Ovaries: follicles, cysts (functional vs. endometrioma vs. dermoid vs. serous/mucinous)
    • Endometrioma: T2-shading, low T2 signal
    • Fallopian tubes: hydrosalpinx (dilated fluid-filled)
    • Bladder: wall thickness, mucosal irregularity
    • Rectosigmoid: wall thickening, endometriosis (nodules, tethering)
  • 5
    DWI / ADC
    • Endometrial carcinoma: restricted diffusion (depth of myometrial invasion)
    • Cervical cancer: tumor conspicuity, parametrial/pelvic sidewall extension
    • Ovarian malignancy: solid components, peritoneal implants
    • Lymph node assessment: size + diffusion restriction
    • Bowel wall abnormalities
  • 6
    T1 Pre-Contrast
    • T1-bright lesions: endometrioma (blood products — T1 bright), dermoid (fat — T1 very bright), hemorrhagic cyst
    • Pelvic hematoma
    • Bone marrow signal in pelvic bones
  • 7
    T1 Fat-Sat Post-Contrast
    • Myometrial enhancement and depth of invasion for endometrial cancer
    • Cervical tumor enhancement: bladder/rectal invasion (loss of fat plane)
    • Ovarian mass: solid enhancing components (malignancy), mural nodules
    • Peritoneal implants: nodular enhancement
    • Pelvic lymph node necrosis/enhancement
  • 8
    Pelvic Floor & Levator Ani
    • Levator ani integrity (particularly post-partum or pelvic floor disorders)
    • Obturator internus: invasion in cervical cancer
    • Piriformis, iliopsoas
  • 9
    Lymph Nodes & Vasculature
    • External iliac, internal iliac, obturator, common iliac, presacral nodes
    • Size ≥10 mm SAX or irregular enhancement = suspicious
    • Iliac vessel patency
  • 10
    Extrapelvic Structures
    • Lower lumbar spine and sacrum: metastatic involvement
    • Inguinal regions
    • Visualized small bowel and sigmoid colon
    • Sciatic notch: nerve involvement
  • 11
    Last Checks
    • FIGO staging if gynecologic malignancy
    • Coronal reformats for parametrial disease
    • Measure primary tumor in 3 planes
    • Compare with prior
MRI Prostate (mpMRI / PI-RADS) MRI
PI-RADS v2.1: Primary sequence is DWI for PZ (DWI score dominant) and T2 for TZ (T2 score dominant). DCE positive = upgrades PI-RADS 3 PZ lesion to 4.
MRI Prostate (mpMRI) — Sequence-Based / PI-RADS Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • PSA level and PSA density (PSA/gland volume)
    • Prior prostate biopsy (Gleason score, laterality)
    • Prior treatment: radiation, HIFU, focal ablation (post-treatment changes)
    • Prior mpMRI for comparison
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Sequences: T2 axial + coronal + sagittal, DWI (multiple b-values including ≥1400), ADC map, DCE (T1 dynamic)
    • Endorectal coil (higher SNR) vs. surface coil only
    • Field strength: 3T preferred
    • Motion artifact, rectal distention (gas artifacts)
    • Note if prior biopsy hemorrhage (T1-bright on pre-contrast)
  • 3
    T1 Pre-Contrast — Hemorrhage Mapping
    • T1-bright signal in prostate = post-biopsy hemorrhage (can mimic or obscure lesions)
    • If hemorrhage present: ADC and DCE reliability reduced in affected areas
    • Seminal vesicles: T1-bright = hemorrhage
    • Biopsy-naïve prostate: T1 is baseline for hemorrhage exclusion
  • 4
    T2 Multiplanar — Anatomy & Zonal Assessment
    • Peripheral zone (PZ): normally homogeneously T2 bright; focal hypointensity = concern
    • Transition zone (TZ): heterogeneous (BPH nodules); TZ primary score based on T2
    • Central zone (CZ): T2 dark, around ejaculatory ducts — can mimic tumor
    • Seminal vesicles: T2 bright lumen; loss of signal = invasion (SVI)
    • Neurovascular bundles: posterolateral 5 and 7 o'clock — ECE assessment
    • Capsule: intact (smooth) vs. ECE (bulging, retraction, obliteration of rectoprostatic angle)
    • Bladder neck: invasion = T3b
    • Urethra and verumontanum: orientation landmarks
  • 5
    DWI (High b-Value ≥1400) / ADC Map — PRIMARY for PZ
    • High b-value DWI: focal hypointense lesion in PZ = PI-RADS 4–5 candidate
    • ADC map: focal markedly hypointense = clinically significant (Gleason ≥3+4)
    • ADC quantification: values <900–1000 μm²/s suggest significance
    • DWI primary score for PZ: 1–5 scale
    • TZ: DWI secondary to T2 for scoring
  • 6
    DCE T1 Dynamic Post-Contrast — SECONDARY for PZ
    • Positive DCE: focal early enhancement (earlier than or simultaneous with surrounding tissue)
    • Positive DCE upgrades PI-RADS 3 PZ lesion → PI-RADS 4
    • Negative DCE: no early focal enhancement
    • DCE not used for TZ scoring
  • 7
    Locoregional Extent
    • Extracapsular extension (ECE): capsular bulge, retraction, irregular margin, neurovascular bundle involvement (T2 + DCE)
    • Seminal vesicle invasion (SVI): T2 dark mass in SV lumen, loss of T2-bright lumen (T3b)
    • Bladder neck invasion (T3b)
    • Lymph nodes: obturator, external iliac, hypogastric; size ≥8–10 mm SAX or DWI restriction
    • Rectum: rectal wall invasion
  • 8
    Bones & Pelvic Structures
    • Sacrum, iliac wings, pubis, femoral heads: T1 low signal (replace normal fat) = metastasis
    • Pelvic floor and levator ani
  • 9
    Last Checks / PI-RADS Scoring
    • Assign PI-RADS score to all lesions ≥PI-RADS 3
    • Note lesion size, location (clock face, zone, apex/mid/base, distance from capsule)
    • Record total prostate volume for PSA density
    • Compare lesion locations with prior biopsy mapping
⚠ PI-RADS 4–5 warrants biopsy. PI-RADS 3 is equivocal — correlate with PSA density, prior biopsy, DCE.
Ultrasound — Abdomen US
US Abdomen — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • RUQ pain vs. LUQ vs. diffuse; jaundice; hepatitis; renal colic
    • Prior US or CT for comparison
  • 2
    Technical Adequacy
    • Patient fasting (6 h for gallbladder)
    • Bowel gas limiting views — note
    • All organs adequately imaged
  • 3
    Liver
    • Echogenicity: increased = steatosis (compare with right kidney cortex)
    • Morphology: surface nodularity, caudate/right lobe ratio (cirrhosis >0.65)
    • Focal lesions: hyperechoic (hemangioma), hypoechoic, complex cyst
    • Doppler: portal vein flow direction, hepatic vein waveform
    • Biliary: intrahepatic duct dilation (>3 mm), CHD >6 mm
  • 4
    Gallbladder
    • Wall thickness >3 mm (fasting) = cholecystitis
    • Stones: hyperechoic with posterior shadowing
    • Sludge: echogenic non-shadowing dependent material
    • Sonographic Murphy sign: maximal tenderness with transducer pressure over GB
    • Polyps: <5 mm = follow, >10 mm = cholecystectomy
  • 5
    Common Bile Duct
    • Normal: ≤6 mm (≤8 mm post-cholecystectomy, ≤10 mm elderly)
    • Dilation: level of obstruction — stone (shadowing), stricture, mass
    • Color Doppler: distinguish from hepatic artery
  • 6
    Pancreas
    • Body and tail visibility (often limited by gas)
    • Echogenicity: increased = fatty infiltration or chronic pancreatitis
    • Pancreatic duct: normal ≤3 mm body
    • Masses: hypoechoic head (pancreatic adenocarcinoma)
  • 7
    Spleen
    • Length >13 cm = splenomegaly
    • Echogenicity, focal lesions (cysts, hemangioma, infarct)
    • Splenic vein dilation (portal hypertension)
  • 8
    Kidneys
    • Size (normal 9–12 cm)
    • Echogenicity: increased cortex = CKD, medical renal disease
    • Hydronephrosis: grade 1–4
    • Renal calculi: hyperechoic with shadowing, twinkling artifact on color Doppler
    • Renal masses: solid vs. cystic (Bosniak by US)
  • 9
    Aorta & IVC
    • Aortic diameter: normal <3 cm; AAA >3 cm
    • IVC patency and collapsibility (volume status)
  • 10
    Free Fluid
    • Ascites: free-flowing anechoic in dependent spaces (Morrison's pouch, pelvic cul-de-sac)
    • Complex fluid: septations, echogenicity (exudate, blood, infection)
  • 11
    Doppler Assessment
    • Portal vein: flow direction, velocity (normal hepatopetal)
    • Hepatic veins: triphasic waveform (loss = elevated right heart pressure, Budd-Chiari)
    • Renal arteries if indicated: RI, PSV
    • Hepatic artery: post-transplant assessment
FAST Exam (Focused Assessment with Sonography in Trauma) US
Goal: detect FREE FLUID (blood) in pericardium and peritoneal/pleural spaces. Positive FAST + hemodynamic instability = immediate surgery without CT.
FAST Exam — Window-by-Window Review
  • 1
    History & Mechanism
    • Blunt vs. penetrating trauma
    • Hemodynamic status: stable vs. unstable
  • 2
    Pericardial Window (Subxiphoid or Parasternal)
    • Pericardial effusion: anechoic fluid around heart
    • Cardiac tamponade: RV diastolic collapse
    • Cardiac activity present?
  • 3
    Right Upper Quadrant — Morrison's Pouch
    • Hepatorenal space: free fluid between liver and right kidney
    • Interface between liver and diaphragm
    • Right paracolic gutter
  • 4
    Left Upper Quadrant — Splenorenal Space
    • Splenorenal recess: free fluid between spleen and left kidney
    • Perisplenic fluid
    • Left subphrenic space
  • 5
    Suprapubic / Pelvic Window
    • Bladder as acoustic window
    • Pouch of Douglas (females): fluid posterior to uterus
    • Rectovesical pouch (males): fluid posterior to bladder
    • Pelvic hematoma
  • 6
    Extended FAST (E-FAST) — Pneumothorax
    • Bilateral anterior chest (2nd ICS midclavicular line)
    • Absence of lung sliding (M-mode: barcode sign vs. seashore sign)
    • B-lines absent at apex
    • Pleural effusion/hemothorax at bases
  • 7
    Last Checks
    • Quantify free fluid if possible (trace vs. moderate vs. large)
    • Correlate with mechanism and vital signs
    • Serial FAST if clinical deterioration
⚠ Hemodynamic instability + positive FAST = operative intervention. Stable patient = proceed to CT.
Ultrasound — Pelvis US
US Pelvis — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Pelvic pain, menstrual irregularity, suspected pregnancy, mass, postmenopausal bleeding
    • Transabdominal (full bladder) vs. transvaginal (TV-US, superior resolution); combined preferred
  • 2
    Technique & Adequacy
    • Bladder fill for transabdominal
    • TV-US performed if uterus and ovaries not adequately seen
    • Doppler for vascularity
  • 3
    Uterus
    • Size: normal 7×4×5 cm (parous); postmenopausal smaller
    • Endometrium: thickness (normal <4–5 mm postmenopausal; premenopausal phase-dependent)
    • Myometrium: fibroids (hypoechoic, calcified shadows), adenomyosis (asymmetric thickening, heterogeneous, subendometrial cysts)
    • Cervix: Nabothian cysts (normal benign)
    • Uterine position: anteverted, retroverted
  • 4
    Ovaries
    • Size: normal ≤3 cm in each dimension (postmenopausal smaller)
    • Follicles: physiologic (<3 cm); dominant follicle
    • Ovarian cysts: simple vs. complex — septations, mural nodules, solid components
    • Endometrioma: ground-glass echogenicity, posterior acoustic enhancement (chocolate cyst)
    • Corpus luteum: thick hyperechoic ring with crenulated border
    • Dermoid (teratoma): hyperechoic with posterior shadowing, fat-fluid level, Rokitansky nodule
  • 5
    Adnexal Regions
    • Hydrosalpinx: tortuous fluid-filled tube, incomplete septa, "beads on a string"
    • Tubo-ovarian abscess: complex adnexal collection with thick irregular walls
    • Pelvic inflammatory disease: free fluid, tubal wall thickening, power Doppler hyperemia
  • 6
    Free Fluid
    • Physiologic small amount in cul-de-sac during luteal phase
    • Complex or large free fluid: blood (ectopic rupture), pus (PID), malignant ascites
  • 7
    Early Pregnancy (if applicable)
    • Intrauterine pregnancy: gestational sac (at 4–5 weeks), yolk sac (5–6 wk), embryo (6 wk), cardiac activity (6 wk)
    • Empty uterus + elevated β-hCG + adnexal mass: rule out ectopic
    • Subchorionic hematoma: crescent of blood adjacent to sac
Mammography XR
Standard views: CC and MLO bilaterally. Digital breast tomosynthesis (DBT) replaces standard 2D in most centers. ACR BI-RADS lexicon: 0 (incomplete) → 6 (known malignancy).
Mammography — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Screening vs. diagnostic (targeted to area of clinical concern)
    • Age, risk factors (BRCA, dense breasts, prior biopsy), implants
    • Prior mammograms — essential for comparison
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • Positioning: MLO — pectoral muscle visible to nipple level, PNL, IMF open; CC — medial tissue, nipple in profile
    • Compression: adequate (motion blur)
    • Exposure: technically adequate on all four views
  • 3
    Breast Composition (ACR Density)
    • A: Almost entirely fatty (lowest masking)
    • B: Scattered fibroglandular (mild masking)
    • C: Heterogeneously dense (moderated masking — ultrasound may be recommended)
    • D: Extremely dense (significant masking — consider supplemental screening)
  • 4
    Masses
    • Shape: round, oval, irregular
    • Margins: circumscribed (likely benign), obscured, microlobulated, indistinct, spiculated (high suspicion)
    • Density: high, equal, low, fat-containing (lipoma, hamartoma, oil cyst, galactocele)
    • Location: quadrant, clock face, depth (anterior/middle/posterior third)
  • 5
    Calcifications
    • Typically benign: round/punctate, milk of calcium, vascular (tram-track), coarse/popcorn, skin, eggshell
    • Suspicious: amorphous, coarse heterogeneous, fine pleomorphic, fine linear/branching (casting)
    • Distribution: diffuse, regional, grouped/clustered, linear, segmental (most suspicious)
  • 6
    Asymmetries
    • Global: large area without mass — usually normal
    • Focal: seen on two projections — needs further assessment
    • Developing: new or growing on prior — high suspicion
    • One-view only: summation vs. real finding
  • 7
    Architectural Distortion
    • Radiating lines without central mass
    • Scar (prior surgery — correlate with history)
    • Radial scar/complex sclerosing lesion
    • Malignancy — high-suspicion finding; needs diagnostic workup
  • 8
    Skin & Nipple
    • Skin thickening: >2 mm diffuse (heart failure, lymphedema, inflammatory breast Ca), focal
    • Nipple retraction: acquired vs. congenital
    • Areolar thickening: Paget disease
  • 9
    Lymph Nodes & Axilla
    • Intramammary and axillary lymph nodes
    • Abnormal: round, dense, loss of fatty hilum
    • Axillary nodes: ≥3 cm or round = metastatic until proven otherwise
  • 10
    BI-RADS Assignment & Comparison
    • Compare with prior mammograms: new finding vs. stable
    • BI-RADS 0: needs additional imaging
    • BI-RADS 1–2: routine; BI-RADS 3: 6-month follow-up (≤2% malignancy)
    • BI-RADS 4: biopsy (4A 2–10%, 4B 10–50%, 4C 50–95%)
    • BI-RADS 5: highly suspicious (≥95%), biopsy
Skull X-Ray XR
Largely superseded by CT. Mainly used for: foreign body, fracture assessment in limited-resource settings, craniofacial measurements, pediatric NAT.
Skull X-Ray — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Trauma (non-accidental trauma in children), headache, post-procedure (shunt, hardware)
    • Prior imaging
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • Views obtained: PA, lateral, Towne (occipital), and/or Waters (sinus)
    • Adequate exposure and positioning
  • 3
    Calvarium
    • Cortical integrity: linear vs. stellate fracture vs. diastatic fracture
    • Depressed fracture: tangential view needed
    • Distinguishing suture from fracture: suture — interdigitated, regular, bilateral; fracture — lucent, irregular, may cross sutures
  • 4
    Sutures
    • Premature fusion (craniosynostosis): sclerotic/obliterated suture vs. open
    • Widened sutures: raised ICP, trauma
  • 5
    Sella Turcica
    • Enlargement: pituitary tumor, increased ICP
    • J-shaped sella: optic nerve glioma
    • Eroded floor: pituitary adenoma
  • 6
    Vascular Markings
    • Middle meningeal artery groove: fracture crossing = epidural hematoma risk
    • Venous sinuses: groove visibility
  • 7
    Intracranial Calcifications
    • Physiologic: pineal gland (midline marker), choroid plexus, falx
    • Pathologic: craniopharyngioma (suprasellar), meningioma (plaque-like), AVM, neurocysticercosis
  • 8
    Soft Tissues & Facial Bones
    • Scalp hematoma overlying fracture site
    • Opacified sinuses
    • Facial bone asymmetry
Cervical Spine X-Ray XR
Standard 3-view (lateral, AP, odontoid) or 5-view (+ bilateral obliques). Lateral view most informative. If trauma, CT preferred — XR has limited sensitivity for fractures.
C-Spine X-Ray — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Trauma (NEXUS/Canadian C-Spine criteria), neck pain, radiculopathy, post-op
    • Prior XR or CT for comparison
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • Lateral: must visualize C7-T1 junction (swimmer's view if not seen)
    • AP: spinous process alignment
    • Odontoid: open-mouth view — dens visible, lateral masses of C1
  • 3
    Alignment (Lateral View)
    • Anterior vertebral line (smooth lordotic curve)
    • Posterior vertebral line (PVL)
    • Spinolaminar line
    • Posterior spinous line
    • Any step-off or angulation >3.5 mm = instability until proven otherwise
  • 4
    Atlanto-Axial Relationship
    • Atlanto-dens interval (ADI): ≤3 mm adults, ≤5 mm children
    • Open-mouth: lateral mass overhang C1 vs. C2 sum >7 mm = Jefferson fracture (rule of Spence)
    • Craniovertebral junction: Basion-dens interval ≤12 mm, Wackenheim line
  • 5
    Vertebral Bodies
    • Height and shape: compression fracture (anterior wedging)
    • End plates: Schmorl's nodes, degenerative changes
    • Bone density, lytic/blastic lesions
  • 6
    Disc Spaces
    • Height: narrowing = degeneration or infection (diskitis — rapid narrowing)
    • Vacuum phenomenon: degenerative
    • Widening: acute disk herniation with posterior element fracture
  • 7
    Posterior Elements
    • Facet joints: perched (subluxed 50%) or jumped facet (bilateral = unstable)
    • Pedicles: cortical disruption
    • Spinous processes: clay-shoveler fracture (C6–7 spinous process)
    • Laminar fractures
  • 8
    Prevertebral Soft Tissues
    • Normal width: <7 mm at C2 (retropharyngeal); <22 mm at C6 (retrotracheal)
    • Thickening: hematoma (fracture/ligamentous injury), abscess, mass
  • 9
    Foramina (Oblique Views)
    • Neuroforaminal narrowing: osteophyte, disk herniation — radiculopathy correlation
    • Uncovertebral joint hypertrophy
  • 10
    Miscellaneous
    • Congenital variants: Klippel-Feil (fused segments), os odontoideum
    • Atlantoaxial instability: Down syndrome, RA, os odontoideum
⚠ Inadequate lateral (C7–T1 not visualized) = limited study. Swimmer's view or CT needed.
Thoracic Spine X-Ray XR
T-Spine X-Ray — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Back pain, trauma, scoliosis, myelopathy evaluation, metastatic disease follow-up
    • Prior XR or MRI
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • AP and lateral views
    • Coverage T1–T12; count ribs to identify level
    • Exposure: dark enough for disc spaces but bony detail visible
  • 3
    Alignment
    • Thoracic kyphosis: normal 20–40°; hyperkyphosis (Scheuermann's >45°)
    • Scoliosis: Cobb angle; curve type
    • Lateral listhesis
  • 4
    Vertebral Bodies
    • Height: compression fracture (anterior wedge, burst, chance)
    • End plates: Schmorl's nodes (Scheuermann's disease)
    • Lytic/blastic lesions: metastases
    • Ivory vertebra: dense uniform sclerosis (Paget, lymphoma, mets)
  • 5
    Disc Spaces
    • Height: disc degeneration
    • Calcification: CPPD, hyperparathyroidism
  • 6
    Posterior Elements
    • Pedicles: "winking owl" sign = lytic pedicle (metastasis)
    • Spinous processes, transverse processes
    • Facet joints
  • 7
    Ribs
    • Fractures: posterior ribs (high specificity for NAT), healing stages
    • Lytic/expansile lesions: multiple myeloma, metastasis
    • Rib notching: coarctation of aorta (inferior surfaces, ribs 3–8)
  • 8
    Soft Tissues
    • Paraspinal lines: widened = paraspinal abscess or hematoma (T4–T12)
    • Aortic calcification
    • Lung bases visible at lateral margins
  • 9
    Miscellaneous
    • Transitional vertebrae
    • Scheuermann's disease: ≥3 adjacent vertebral end-plate irregularities with >5° wedging
Lumbar Spine X-Ray XR
L-Spine X-Ray — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Low back pain, radiculopathy, spondylolysis/spondylolisthesis, post-op, metastatic disease
    • Prior XR or MRI
  • 2
    Image Quality
    • Views: AP, lateral; flexion-extension (if instability suspected); oblique (pars defects)
    • Count from sacrum upward; note transitional anatomy (lumbosacral variant — Bertolotti)
  • 3
    Alignment
    • Lumbar lordosis: normal; loss = muscle spasm
    • Spondylolisthesis: grade I–V (Meyerding); anterolisthesis vs. retrolisthesis
    • Scoliosis: degenerative vs. idiopathic
  • 4
    Vertebral Bodies
    • Height: anterior wedge (compression fracture — acute vs. chronic: indeterminate on XR alone)
    • Bone density: osteoporosis (biconcave deformity, codfish vertebra)
    • Lytic/blastic lesions: metastases, myeloma, primary bone tumor
  • 5
    Disc Spaces
    • Height: narrowing = degeneration
    • Vacuum phenomenon: intranuclear cleft gas
    • Calcification: CPPD
  • 6
    Posterior Elements
    • Pedicles: intact oval ovoid cortex; lytic = metastasis ("winking owl")
    • Pars interarticularis: oblique view — Scottie dog (collar = spondylolysis)
    • Facet joints: arthropathy, asymmetry (tropism)
    • Spinous processes
  • 7
    Sacrum & SI Joints
    • Sacral fractures: oblique lucency
    • SI joints: widening or sclerosis (sacroiliitis — ankylosing spondylitis)
    • S1 superior end plate integrity
  • 8
    Soft Tissues
    • Psoas shadow: symmetric bilateral
    • Abdominal aorta calcification
  • 9
    Transitional Anatomy
    • Sacralization of L5 (partial/complete, Bertolotti syndrome)
    • Lumbarization of S1
    • Note and number correctly from superior
  • 10
    Flexion-Extension Views (if obtained)
    • Translational movement ≥3 mm or angulation ≥10° = instability
    • Compare adjacent levels
CT Head CT
Windows: Brain (W:80/L:40), Bone (W:3000/L:500), Subdural (W:200/L:80). Non-contrast CT is first-line for acute symptoms.
CT Head — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Acute stroke vs. headache vs. trauma vs. altered mental status vs. seizure vs. post-op
    • Time of symptom onset (stroke — thrombolytics window)
    • Prior CT head or MRI for comparison
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Coverage: skull vertex to foramen magnum
    • Non-contrast vs. contrast-enhanced vs. CT perfusion
  • 3
    Blood / Hyperdense Lesions (First Pass)
    • Identify any hyperdense blood immediately
    • Epidural: biconvex lens-shaped, lenticular — lens does not cross sutures (temporal/temporal-parietal, MMA injury)
    • Subdural: crescent shape, crosses sutures, follows brain contour; acute (hyperdense >55 HU), subacute (isodense), chronic (hypodense)
    • Subarachnoid: cerebral sulci (interpeduncular, suprasellar, perimesencephalic cisterns), sylvian fissures — hyperdense
    • "SAH until proven otherwise": CT + LP if CT negative within 6 h of headache
    • Intraparenchymal hemorrhage: location (lobar — amyloid, hypertensive nuclei: putamen, thalamus, pons, cerebellum)
    • Intraventricular hemorrhage: ventricle density, hydrocephalus
  • 4
    Ischemia / Hypodense Lesions
    • Early infarct signs: loss of gray-white differentiation, insular ribbon sign, effacement of sulci
    • Hyperdense MCA sign: clot in MCA
    • Established infarct: hypodense, cortical ribbon, volume loss
    • ASPECTS score for MCA territory: 10 = normal, <7 = poor thrombolysis outcome
  • 5
    Ventricles & Cisterns
    • Ventricular size: hydrocephalus (communicating vs. non-communicating)
    • Third ventricle: dilated = obstruction at cerebral aqueduct or downstream
    • Fourth ventricle: compressed (posterior fossa mass) or dilated
    • Basal cisterns: effacement = raised ICP (herniation risk)
    • Transfalcine herniation: shift >5 mm = significant (mass effect)
  • 6
    Brain Parenchyma
    • Cerebral edema: diffuse sulcal effacement, loss of gray-white differentiation
    • Mass lesions: ring-enhancing (abscess, high-grade glioma, met), solid (low-grade glioma, lymphoma), calcified
    • Calcifications: physiologic (pineal, choroid) vs. pathologic (toxoplasmosis, NCC)
    • White matter changes: periventricular, subcortical (leukoaraiosis)
  • 7
    Midline Structures
    • Falx and tentorium: acute blood layering
    • Pineal gland: midline position (deviation = mass effect)
    • Midline shift measurement at level of foramen of Monro
  • 8
    Posterior Fossa
    • Cerebellum: hemorrhage, infarct (PICA, SCA territory), mass
    • Brainstem: hemorrhage (pons = hypertensive), mass
    • Fourth ventricle position
  • 9
    Skull & Calvarium
    • Fractures: linear vs. depressed; temporal bone fractures (longitudinal vs. transverse on CT temporal windows)
    • Pneumocephalus: air in intracranial compartment (trauma, post-op)
    • Calvarial lesions: lytic (mets, myeloma) vs. sclerotic
    • Sinuses: opacification (hemorrhage in trauma, sinusitis)
  • 10
    Extracranial Structures
    • Scalp: soft tissue hematoma (contrecoup)
    • Orbits: trauma, foreign body
    • Mastoid air cells
    • Cervical spine on scout
  • 11
    Herniation Patterns
    • Subfalcine: cingulate gyrus under falx
    • Uncal: medial temporal lobe through tentorial notch → CN III compression, posterior communicating artery, PCA
    • Central: downward displacement of diencephalon
    • Tonsillar: cerebellar tonsils through foramen magnum → cardiorespiratory arrest
  • 12
    CT Angiography (if CTA performed)
    • Vessel occlusion (LVO): ICA terminus, MCA M1/M2, basilar artery
    • Aneurysm: location (anterior communicating most common), size, morphology
    • Carotid/vertebral dissection: intimal flap, wall hematoma (crescent sign)
  • 13
    Last Checks
    • Subdural windows (W:200/L:80) for isodense subdurals
    • Bone windows for all skull fractures
    • Measure any shift, hemorrhage volume (ABC/2 method)
    • Soft copy review with adjust window/level
⚠ SAH: if clinical suspicion high and CT negative, LP at ≥6 h of onset (xanthochromia) or CT angiography.
CT Temporal Bones CT
High-resolution CT (HRCT), 0.5–1 mm slices. Bone algorithm. Review bilaterally with dedicated bone windows. Axial + coronal reconstructions.
CT Temporal Bones — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Sensorineural (SNHL) vs. conductive hearing loss (CHL), chronic ear disease, cholesteatoma, otosclerosis, temporal bone trauma, pre-cochlear implant
    • Prior CT temporal bones for comparison
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Coverage: bilateral from external auditory canals to mastoid tips
    • Bone algorithm, <1 mm slices, axial + coronal
  • 3
    External Auditory Canal
    • Stenosis or atresia (congenital)
    • Soft tissue mass: exostosis (surfer's ear — broad-based), osteoma (pedunculated)
    • Otitis externa: soft tissue in EAC; necrotizing (malignant) OE: bone erosion at EAC floor
  • 4
    Middle Ear & Ossicles
    • Air vs. fluid vs. soft tissue in middle ear
    • Ossicular chain: malleus, incus, stapes; erosion (cholesteatoma)
    • Tympanic membrane: perforation (soft tissue defect at edge of pars tensa)
    • Tegmen tympani: dehiscence, fracture
    • Facial nerve canal: dehiscence (tympanic segment)
  • 5
    Cholesteatoma
    • Blunted scutum (Prussak's space — pars flaccida, most common)
    • Soft tissue mass with bony erosion: lateral wall of epitympanum → ossicular chain → tegmen
  • 6
    Mastoid
    • Mastoid air cell opacification: mastoiditis (loss of bony septa = coalescent)
    • Sclerotic mastoid: chronic otomastoiditis
    • Mastoid tegmen: dehiscence or erosion
  • 7
    Inner Ear
    • Cochlea: otosclerosis (fenestral — spongiotic halo around oval window; retrofenestral — cochlear capsule lucency)
    • Vestibule, semicircular canals: dysplasia (Mondini — incomplete partition, enlarged vestibule)
    • Endolymphatic duct: enlarged (endolymphatic hydrops)
    • Cochlear implant: assess cochlear patency pre-op (obliterative labyrinthitis)
  • 8
    Internal Auditory Canal (IAC)
    • IAC caliber: asymmetric widening >2 mm = acoustic neuroma until proven otherwise
    • Fundus of IAC
  • 9
    Facial Nerve Canal
    • Labyrinthine, tympanic, mastoid segments
    • Dehiscence: common in tympanic segment
    • Fracture through facial nerve canal
CT Soft Tissue Neck CT
Typically contrast-enhanced venous phase. Review in soft tissue AND bone windows. Coverage: skull base through thoracic inlet.
CT Soft Tissue Neck — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Neck mass (age-related differential), dysphagia, airway compromise, infection, post-op, oncologic staging/surveillance
    • Prior CT neck or PET-CT
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Coverage: skull base to clavicles/thoracic inlet
    • Contrast enhancement — adequate opacification
    • Dental/beam hardening artifact
  • 3
    Airway
    • Trachea: deviation, narrowing, intraluminal lesion
    • Subglottis and glottis: lesion encroaching airway
    • Larynx: symmetry of arytenoids, aryepiglottic folds, pyriform sinuses
    • Epiglottis: thickening (epiglottitis)
  • 4
    Mucosal Spaces & Pharynx
    • Nasopharynx: adenoid tissue (age-appropriate vs. asymmetric mass), fossa of Rosenmüller asymmetry (SCCa origin)
    • Oropharynx: base of tongue, tonsils (asymmetric vs. mass vs. abscess)
    • Hypopharynx: pyriform sinuses (asymmetric, mucosal lesion, widening)
    • Larynx: supraglottis, glottis, subglottis — mass, asymmetry, cartilage invasion (sclerosis = early; lysis = invasion)
  • 5
    Deep Spaces of the Neck
    • Parapharyngeal space: fat plane preservation (fat displaced = adjacent mass)
    • Masticator space: pterygoid muscles, mandible, perineural spread along V3
    • Parotid space: mass characterization (pleomorphic adenoma, Warthin, malignancy, nodes)
    • Submandibular space: mass, sialolithiasis
    • Carotid space: ICA/IJV, vagus nerve, paraganglioma (splaying of ICA/ECA)
    • Retropharyngeal space: effacement = fluid/abscess; node metastases
    • Danger space: extends to mediastinum — descending necrotizing mediastinitis risk
    • Visceral space: thyroid, parathyroid, trachea, esophagus
    • Posterior cervical space: lymph nodes (level V, accessory chain), lipoma, schwannoma
  • 6
    Thyroid & Parathyroid
    • Thyroid: nodules >1 cm, heterogeneous gland, goiter with substernal extension, calcifications (suspicious = fine/punctate)
    • Parathyroid: posterior to thyroid lobes; adenoma — hyperdense, early arterial blush
  • 7
    Lymph Node Mapping
    • Levels I–VI and retropharyngeal nodes
    • Size criteria: >10 mm SAX, except lateral retropharyngeal ≥8 mm
    • Central necrosis = highly specific for metastatic node
    • Calcified nodes: papillary thyroid cancer, treated lymphoma
    • Extranodal extension (ENE): irregular margin, infiltration of adjacent fat
  • 8
    Vascular Structures
    • Carotid arteries: stenosis, dissection (crescent sign), aneurysm
    • Internal jugular vein: thrombosis (Lemierre — septic thrombophlebitis)
    • Vertebral arteries: caliber asymmetry (normal variant common)
  • 9
    Salivary Glands
    • Parotid: mass (pleomorphic adenoma, Warthin, mucoepidermoid), sialolithiasis, parotitis
    • Submandibular: Wharton's duct stone (submandibular obstruction)
    • Sublingual: ranula, dermoid
  • 10
    Skull Base & Posterior Fossa
    • Skull base invasion by nasopharyngeal mass
    • Carotid canal, jugular foramen
    • Clivus: erosion vs. sclerosis
  • 11
    Last Checks
    • Bone windows: mandible, hyoid, laryngeal cartilage (thyroid/cricoid/arytenoids)
    • Lung apices on inferior images
    • Mediastinal nodes on inferior images
    • Compare with prior
CT Cervical Spine CT
Standard trauma CT protocol. Sagittal and coronal reconstructions essential. Bone algorithm. Most fractures missed on sagittal or coronal alone — review all planes.
CT Cervical Spine — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Trauma mechanism: flexion/extension/axial load/direct blow; neurologic deficit?
    • Prior CT or XR; anticoagulation status
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Coverage: occiput through C7–T1 (T4 on some trauma protocols)
    • Sagittal + coronal reconstructions
    • Bone and soft tissue windows
  • 3
    Atlanto-Occipital & Craniovertebral Junction
    • Occipitoatlantal dissociation: condyle-C1 joint space, basion-dens interval (≤12 mm)
    • Condylar fractures: types I–III (III = unstable into foramen magnum)
  • 4
    C1 (Atlas)
    • Jefferson fracture: axial load → bilateral ring fractures; rule of Spence (>7 mm total lateral overhang)
    • Posterior arch fractures (most common C1 fracture)
    • Rotatory subluxation: atlanto-axial asymmetry
  • 5
    C2 (Axis) & Odontoid
    • Odontoid fractures: type I (tip, stable), II (base of dens — most common, unstable, nonunion risk), III (body, usually stable)
    • Hangman's fracture: bilateral pedicle/pars fractures of C2; traumatic spondylolisthesis of C2 on C3
    • ADI >3 mm on lateral = transverse ligament injury
  • 6
    C3–C7 Vertebral Bodies
    • Compression fractures: anterior wedge (flexion), burst (axial — posterior cortex disruption)
    • Fracture morphology: anterior vs. posterior element involvement — stability assessment (Denis three-column model)
    • Teardrop fracture: avulsion (stable, extension) vs. flexion teardrop (unstable — ruptured ALL, posterior ligament complex)
    • Facet fractures: unilateral vs. bilateral facet dislocation
  • 7
    Posterior Elements
    • Facet joints: malalignment, jumped/perched facet (50% subluxation = unilateral, >50% = bilateral)
    • Laminar fractures: "double fracture" sign
    • Spinous process fractures: Clay-shoveler (C6–7, avulsion by nuchal ligament)
    • Pedicle fractures
  • 8
    Alignment (Sagittal Reconstructions)
    • Anterior, posterior vertebral lines, spinolaminar line
    • Retrolisthesis or anterolisthesis >3.5 mm = unstable
    • Loss of lordosis: muscle spasm or ligamentous injury
  • 9
    Prevertebral & Paraspinal Soft Tissues
    • Prevertebral soft tissue swelling: hematoma — normal <7 mm at C2, <22 mm at C6
    • Retropharyngeal abscess or empyema
  • 10
    Spinal Canal
    • Canal diameter: compromise by displaced fracture fragment
    • Cord signal not assessable on CT (need MRI)
  • 11
    Last Checks
    • Bone windows through entire cervical spine
    • Coronal + sagittal reformats in sequence
    • Check all three columns at every level
    • Note levels of previous surgery or fusion
⚠ Denis three-column instability: disruption of posterior + middle columns (or all 3) = unstable fracture.
CT Thoracic Spine CT
CT Thoracic Spine — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Trauma (mechanism, energy level, neurologic deficit), myelopathy, known/suspected metastases, spondylitis
    • Prior imaging
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Coverage: cervicothoracic junction through thoracolumbar junction
    • Sagittal and coronal reconstructions
  • 3
    Vertebral Bodies
    • Height: anterior compression (wedge — flexion) vs. burst (axial — posterior wall retropulsion)
    • Chance fracture: flexion-distraction (horizontal fracture through body, pedicles, spinous process — seatbelt injury)
    • End plates: irregularity (infection, Schmorl's nodes)
    • Bone density, lytic/blastic lesions
  • 4
    Posterior Elements
    • Facet fractures and dislocations
    • Transverse process fractures: associated aortic injury (left-sided high-energy T4 transverse process)
    • Spinous process fractures
    • Pedicle integrity
  • 5
    Alignment (Sagittal + Coronal)
    • Kyphosis: normal 20–40°; traumatic kyphosis (post-fracture angulation)
    • Coronal: scoliosis, lateral translation
  • 6
    Spinal Canal
    • Retropulsed fragment: percentage canal compromise
    • Epidural hematoma: hyperdense epidural collection (MRI more sensitive)
  • 7
    Chest Structures
    • Aorta: traumatic aortic injury (wide mediastinum on scout — CT angiography)
    • Pneumothorax, hemothorax
    • Rib fractures: posterior ribs, number
    • Lung parenchyma
  • 8
    Bones
    • Ribs: fractures, lytic/blastic lesions
    • Sternum: fractures on sagittal/axial
    • Scapulae
  • 9
    Paraspinal Soft Tissues
    • Widened paraspinal stripe: abscess or hematoma
    • Anterior longitudinal ligament calcification (DISH — flowing syndesmophytes)
  • 10
    Last Checks
    • Count from cervicothoracic junction down
    • Sagittal and coronal reconstructions for alignment and fracture completeness
    • Lung windows at lateral margins for pneumothorax/pulmonary contusion
CT Lumbar Spine CT
CT Lumbar Spine — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Acute fracture (trauma, fragility), radiculopathy/stenosis (post-myelogram), metastatic disease, post-op hardware assessment
    • Prior CT or MRI
  • 2
    Adequacy
    • Coverage: T12–L1 junction through sacrum
    • Sagittal and coronal reconstructions; axial for canal
  • 3
    Vertebral Bodies
    • Height: compression fracture — acute (cortical disruption) vs. chronic (eburnation, rounded margins)
    • Burst fracture: retropulsion of posterior cortex into canal
    • Chance fracture: horizontal split through body and posterior elements
    • Lytic/blastic lesions: metastases (lung/breast/prostate), myeloma (lytic, "punched out"), Paget (enlarged, coarsened trabeculae)
  • 4
    Disc Spaces & End Plates
    • Disc height loss: degeneration
    • Vacuum phenomenon: degeneration; absent in acute fracture with edema
    • End plate destruction: diskitis-osteomyelitis (bilateral end plate erosion, disc signal change — CT less sensitive than MRI)
    • Schmorl's nodes: intravertebral disc herniation through end plate
  • 5
    Posterior Elements
    • Pedicles: lytic destruction ("winking owl" sign on AP)
    • Pars defects: spondylolysis (oblique or parasagittal reformation — "Scotty dog collar")
    • Facet joint arthropathy: hypertrophy, gas, cysts (intraspinal extension)
    • Spinous process fractures
  • 6
    Spinal Canal & Foramina
    • Central canal: measure AP diameter; critical stenosis <10 mm
    • Lateral recesses: nerve root compression
    • Foraminal stenosis: grade I–IV; fat obliteration of foramen
    • Ligamentum flavum hypertrophy: posterior canal compromise
  • 7
    Alignment
    • Spondylolisthesis: grade (Meyerding I–V); anterolisthesis vs. retrolisthesis
    • Degenerative scoliosis: lateral listhesis
  • 8
    Sacrum & SI Joints
    • Sacral insufficiency fractures: H-pattern or vertical sacral fractures
    • Zone I/II/III sacral fractures (Denis classification)
    • SI joint: widening, erosion (sacroiliitis), ankylosis
  • 9
    Post-Surgical Assessment
    • Hardware: pedicle screws (trajectory, tip position), rods (failure/breakage), cages (subsidence, position)
    • Post-laminectomy/fusion changes
    • Pseudo-arthrosis: lucency around hardware, motion on flex-ext
  • 10
    Paraspinal & Soft Tissues
    • Psoas abscess: hypoattenuating, rim-enhancing collection if contrast given
    • Aorta/iliac vessel calcification, aneurysm on lateral images
    • Retroperitoneal lymphadenopathy
  • 11
    Last Checks
    • Window level for bone detail (cortex, trabeculae)
    • Sagittal + coronal reformats in sequence
    • Count from sacrum upward (note Bertolotti/transitional anatomy)
    • CT myelogram: evaluate intrathecal contrast distribution, nerve root filling defects
CTA Head & Neck CT
Arterial phase (15–25 s after aortic arch opacification). Thin-slice axial + MIP + 3D VR reconstructions. Review source images for intraluminal detail.
CTA Head & Neck — Systematic Review
  • 1
    History & Indication
    • Acute stroke workup (LVO screening), subarachnoid hemorrhage (aneurysm), trauma (dissection), TIA, carotid stenosis, pre-surgical vascular planning
    • CT head without contrast should precede CTA in stroke
  • 2
    Adequacy / Technique
    • Opacification: aortic arch opacification ≥250 HU; intracranial vessels clearly visible
    • Coverage: aortic arch through circle of Willis (neck CTA) or intracranial only
    • Streak artifact from shoulders/dental hardware — note limitation
  • 3
    Cervical Vessels — Carotid Arteries
    • Common carotid arteries: caliber, atherosclerotic plaque (calcified vs. non-calcified)
    • Carotid bifurcation: stenosis (NASCET method — distal ICA reference); significant ≥70%
    • Internal carotid: intimal flap/dissection (eccentric T1-bright wall hematoma on MRI; crescent sign)
    • External carotid: branches (not typically assessed unless AVM/fistula)
  • 4
    Cervical Vessels — Vertebral Arteries
    • Dominant vertebral artery (left usually larger)
    • V1 (origin, subclavian to C6), V2 (transverse foramina), V3 (C2 to foramen magnum), V4 (intracranial)
    • Dissection, stenosis, hypoplasia vs. aplasia
    • Subclavian steal: pre/post subclavian stenosis
  • 5
    Circle of Willis & Intracranial Arteries
    • ICA siphon: stenosis, aneurysm (ophthalmic, posterior communicating, anterior choroidal)
    • MCA: M1 occlusion (LVO), M2 division, aneurysm (MCA bifurcation most common)
    • ACA: A1/A2, anterior communicating artery (most common aneurysm location overall)
    • PCA: posterior communicating arteries, basilar tip
    • Posterior circulation: basilar artery (aneurysm — 5th most common), SCA, PICA, AICA
  • 6
    Aneurysm Assessment
    • Location, sac morphology (bilobed, daughter sac = rupture risk), neck width
    • Size: ≥7 mm = generally treated; any size if symptomatic or growing
    • Relationship to parent artery and bone (surgical vs. endovascular approach planning)
  • 7
    Intracranial Arterial Occlusion (Stroke)
    • Hyperdense vessel sign on non-contrast CT → vessel cutoff on CTA
    • Collateral score: leptomeningeal filling distal to occlusion
    • ASPECTS ≥6 for potential thrombectomy (ICA/MCA occlusion)
  • 8
    Venous Structures (if protocol includes)
    • Dural venous sinuses: superior sagittal, transverse, sigmoid, straight
    • Filling defect = cerebral venous sinus thrombosis (CVST)
    • Cortical vein thrombosis
  • 9
    Parenchyma & Skull Base
